Allah's Existence

6 min
The First Argument for the Indivisible Part: Separation
Presents the argument that separation proves the indivisible part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of complete separation leads to the necessity of indivisible parts (atoms) in kalam theology.

Core Idea
Separation proves atoms: infinite divisibility would leave remnants of union.

In this lesson, we examine the first of several arguments for the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza), a cornerstone of kalam atomism. The argument appeals to a simple observable event: two objects that were together become completely separated. The mutakallimun argue that this complete separation would be impossible if bodies were divisible without limit.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the concise logical structure: the argument begins with a premise, gives a reason, and concludes with a proof by contradiction.
2
Understand the key terms
Identify the terms: 'iftiraq' (separation), 'qudra' (power), 'al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza' (indivisible part), and 'al-diddan' (the two opposites, i.e., union and separation). Recognize that 'qudra' refers to divine power.
3
Analyze the logical steps
Follow the logic: (1) Power relates equally to both opposites (union and separation). (2) When separation occurs completely, it must be a real possibility. (3) If there were no ultimate indivisible parts, then after any separation, some contact would remain because parts could be further subdivided. (4) This contradicts the observed complete separation. Hence, indivisible parts must exist.
This is a reductio ad absurdum (khalaf).
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Understand that the argument does not assume atomism; it derives it from the fact of complete separation. If bodies were infinitely divisible, separation could never be absolute.
This connects to the broader kalam position on atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الافتراق، بحيث لا يبقى اجتماع أصلاً، وذلك لأن نسبة القدرة إلى الضدين على السواء، وإذا حصل الافتراق ثبت الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ، إذ لو كان قابلاً للتجزيء لكان الاجتماع باقياً هذا خلف.
Separation, such that no union remains at all, and that is because the relationship of power to the two opposites is equal. And if separation occurs, the indivisible part is established, for if it were divisible, union would remain — this is a contradiction.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza). It begins by considering the phenomenon of separation (iftiraq) where two bodies that were together become completely separate, leaving no union. The proof argues that power (qudra) is equally related to both opposites (i.e., both union and separation are equally possible). So when separation actually occurs, it must be that the body in question is composed of indivisible parts. Why? Because if the body were divisible indefinitely (i.e., had no indivisible part), then upon separation, there would always be some contact remaining; the parts could be further divided and thus remain in contact. This would contradict the observed complete separation. The author concludes: 'this is a contradiction' (khalaf), meaning the assumption of no indivisible part leads to an absurdity.

Key Terms
الافتراق
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
القدرة
الضدان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes that all bodies are composed of indivisible parts from the start.
Correction: The argument does not assume atomism; it derives the necessity of indivisible parts from the fact of complete separation. It argues that without an indivisible part, complete separation could not occur.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of the first argument for the indivisible part?
A) The equal capacity of power to both opposites
B) The finite size of bodies
C) The impossibility of infinite regress
D) The nature of motion

Key Takeaway
Complete separation is impossible without indivisible parts; thus, the observable phenomenon of separation proves atomism.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If God's power is equally able to create both union and separation, what does that mean for the things in your life you treat as fixed or inevitable? Can you identify one area where you have unconsciously denied God's power over the outcome?
?
The argument uses a contradiction to establish truth. Are there contradictions in your own faith that you have accepted rather than resolved? Will you commit to using reason, like this proof, to strengthen your conviction, or will you remain content with unresolved doubts?
?
Complete separation from sin requires an indivisible commitment. Is there a sin you are still partially 'united' with, hoping it can be divided and kept? What would a total separation—leaving no residue—look like in your life, and what would it cost you to make that commitment today?

The Core Proof: Without the Indivisible Part, All Bodies Would Be Equal
Sanusi presents the core inference of the second proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the central claim of the second argument for the indivisible part: that without it, a mountain and a mustard seed would be equal in magnitude.

Core Idea
If there's no indivisible part, all bodies are equal in magnitude.

This lesson introduces the second proof for the existence of the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhī lā yatajazza'). The argument hinges on the absurd conclusion that a mountain and a mustard seed would be equal in size if both were infinitely divisible. Let's read Sanusi's crisp formulation.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without annotating. Then read the English translation to grasp the literal meaning.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice that the passage is a conditional: 'If not established... then not greater.' The conclusion is equality (tasawi).
3
Reflect on the key term 'tasawi'
Equality here means having no superiority in magnitude. Ask yourself why this conclusion is considered absurd.
This sets up the need for the indivisible part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لو لم يثبت الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لما كان الجبل أعظم من الخردلة، لأن كلاً منهما حينئذ يكون قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية فتكون أجزاء كل منهما غير متناهية من غير تفاضل، وهو معنى التساوي. فإن قيل: غايته لزوم الاستواء في عدد الأجزاء، بأن يكون أجزاء كل منهما غير متناهية العدد، وهو غير محال والمحال استواء مقداريهما، وهو غير لازم. أجيب بأن الاستواء في الأجزاء يستلزم الاستواء في المقدار، ضرورة أن تفاوت المقادير إنما هو بتفاوت الأجزاء، بمعنى أن ما يكون مقداره أعظم تكون أجزاؤه أكثر، فما لا تكون أجزاؤه أكثر لا يكون مقداره أعظم. وقد تقرر هذا الوجه بحيث لا يبتنى على استلزام قبول الانقسام حصول الأقسام، وهو أنه لو كان كل من السماء والخردلة قابلاً للانقسام من غير انتهاء إلى ما لا يقبل الانقسام أصلاً، كانت الأجزاء الممكنة حينئذ في كل منهما متساوية للتي في الأجزاء الآخر، وأمكن أن يفصل من الخردلة صفائح تغمر وجهى السماء، بل أجزاء تغمر الوجهين، وتملأ ما بين السطحين وبطلانه ضروري وجوابه بعد تسليم البطلان ما سبق أن ليس معنى قبول الانقسامات الغير المتناهية إمكان خروجها من القوة إلى الفعل. فمن أين يلزم إمكان حصول أقسام لا نهاية لها، وإمكان انفصالها؟
The second: If the indivisible part were not established, then the mountain would not be greater than the mustard seed, because each of them would then be divisible into infinite divisions, so the parts of each would be infinite without superiority, and that is the meaning of equality. If it is said: Its consequence is only the equality in number of parts, in that the parts of each are infinite in number, which is not impossible, and the impossible is the equality of their magnitudes, which is not necessary. The answer: Equality in parts necessitates equality in magnitude, necessarily because the difference in magnitudes is only due to the difference in parts, meaning that what has greater magnitude has more parts, so what does not have more parts does not have greater magnitude. And this point has been established such that it does not depend on the acceptance of division entailing the actual existence of parts, namely that if both the sky and the mustard seed were divisible without ending at what does not accept division at all, then the possible parts in each would be equal to those in the other, and it would be possible to separate from the mustard seed sheets covering the two faces of the sky, rather parts covering both faces, and filling what is between the two surfaces, and its falsity is necessary. And its answer, after conceding the falsity, is what came before: that the meaning of accepting infinite divisions is not the possibility of their emergence from potentiality to actuality. So how does there follow the possibility of obtaining infinite parts and the possibility of their separation?
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the indivisible part. It argues that without the indivisible part, a mountain and a mustard seed would be equal in size because both would be infinitely divisible, thus having infinite parts with no superiority — equality (tasawi). An objector then says that the equality is only in the number of parts (both infinite), but that does not imply equality in magnitude, since infinite numbers are equal. The answer (jawab) affirms that equality in number of parts does imply equality in magnitude because magnitude is determined by the number of parts; greater magnitude means more parts. The argument is then strengthened: even if we consider potential parts (i.e., the parts into which a body could be divided), if both are infinitely divisible, then the potential parts of the sky and the mustard seed would be equal in number, and it would be possible to take a part of the mustard seed that could cover the entire sky — an obvious absurdity. The author then responds that this objection concedes the falsity of the consequence but the earlier answer already shows that infinite divisibility does not mean these parts ever emerge from potentiality to actuality; so the impossibility of such separation is not entailed. Thus the argument holds.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
التساوي
الأجزاء غير المتناهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the proof claim would happen if there were no indivisible part?
A) The mountain would be smaller than the mustard seed.
B) The mountain and the mustard seed would be equal in magnitude.
C) The mountain would have more parts than the mustard seed.
D) The indivisible part would still exist.

Key Takeaway
Without the indivisible part, all bodies would be equal in magnitude—an absurd consequence that proves the part must exist.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the mountain and mustard seed were equal in Allah's sight, would you still value the 'big' and 'small' in your life as you do? This proof shows that if not for the indivisible part, all would collapse into equality. Are you living as if Allah's creation has no foundation, ignoring the clear signs of order He placed?
?
The proof argues that infinite division leads to absurdity. In your own life, have you ever chased an infinite regress of excuses or justifications, only to find they all lead to the same emptiness? This lesson invites you to accept that there must be a stopping point—a foundation. Will you let this truth anchor your soul?

Objection and Answer: Equality in Number of Infinite Parts
Sanusi records an objection and provides his answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how an objector challenges the proof by claiming equality in number of infinite parts does not entail equality in magnitude, and the author's response that magnitude depends on number of parts.

Core Idea
Magnitude is determined by the number of parts, even if both are infinite.

The previous proof concluded that without the indivisible part, all bodies would be equal. But an objector argues that having the same number of infinite parts does not mean having the same size. Sanusi responds by clarifying that magnitude is a function of part count. Let's examine this exchange.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Identify what the objector concedes (equality in number of parts) and what he denies (equality in magnitude).
2
Read the answer
Notice that the author asserts a necessary connection between part count and magnitude.
3
Understand the reasoning
The author holds that magnitude differences are due to differences in the number of parts. If two bodies have the same number of parts (infinite), their magnitudes must be equal.
This is a key premise in kalam atomism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لو لم يثبت الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لما كان الجبل أعظم من الخردلة، لأن كلاً منهما حينئذ يكون قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية فتكون أجزاء كل منهما غير متناهية من غير تفاضل، وهو معنى التساوي. فإن قيل: غايته لزوم الاستواء في عدد الأجزاء، بأن يكون أجزاء كل منهما غير متناهية العدد، وهو غير محال والمحال استواء مقداريهما، وهو غير لازم. أجيب بأن الاستواء في الأجزاء يستلزم الاستواء في المقدار، ضرورة أن تفاوت المقادير إنما هو بتفاوت الأجزاء، بمعنى أن ما يكون مقداره أعظم تكون أجزاؤه أكثر، فما لا تكون أجزاؤه أكثر لا يكون مقداره أعظم. وقد تقرر هذا الوجه بحيث لا يبتنى على استلزام قبول الانقسام حصول الأقسام، وهو أنه لو كان كل من السماء والخردلة قابلاً للانقسام من غير انتهاء إلى ما لا يقبل الانقسام أصلاً، كانت الأجزاء الممكنة حينئذ في كل منهما متساوية للتي في الأجزاء الآخر، وأمكن أن يفصل من الخردلة صفائح تغمر وجهى السماء، بل أجزاء تغمر الوجهين، وتملأ ما بين السطحين وبطلانه ضروري وجوابه بعد تسليم البطلان ما سبق أن ليس معنى قبول الانقسامات الغير المتناهية إمكان خروجها من القوة إلى الفعل. فمن أين يلزم إمكان حصول أقسام لا نهاية لها، وإمكان انفصالها؟
The second: If the indivisible part were not established, then the mountain would not be greater than the mustard seed, because each of them would then be divisible into infinite divisions, so the parts of each would be infinite without superiority, and that is the meaning of equality. If it is said: Its consequence is only the equality in number of parts, in that the parts of each are infinite in number, which is not impossible, and the impossible is the equality of their magnitudes, which is not necessary. The answer: Equality in parts necessitates equality in magnitude, necessarily because the difference in magnitudes is only due to the difference in parts, meaning that what has greater magnitude has more parts, so what does not have more parts does not have greater magnitude. And this point has been established such that it does not depend on the acceptance of division entailing the actual existence of parts, namely that if both the sky and the mustard seed were divisible without ending at what does not accept division at all, then the possible parts in each would be equal to those in the other, and it would be possible to separate from the mustard seed sheets covering the two faces of the sky, rather parts covering both faces, and filling what is between the two surfaces, and its falsity is necessary. And its answer, after conceding the falsity, is what came before: that the meaning of accepting infinite divisions is not the possibility of their emergence from potentiality to actuality. So how does there follow the possibility of obtaining infinite parts and the possibility of their separation?
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the indivisible part. It argues that without the indivisible part, a mountain and a mustard seed would be equal in size because both would be infinitely divisible, thus having infinite parts with no superiority — equality (tasawi). An objector then says that the equality is only in the number of parts (both infinite), but that does not imply equality in magnitude, since infinite numbers are equal. The answer (jawab) affirms that equality in number of parts does imply equality in magnitude because magnitude is determined by the number of parts; greater magnitude means more parts. The argument is then strengthened: even if we consider potential parts (i.e., the parts into which a body could be divided), if both are infinitely divisible, then the potential parts of the sky and the mustard seed would be equal in number, and it would be possible to take a part of the mustard seed that could cover the entire sky — an obvious absurdity. Thus the argument holds.

Key Terms
التساوي
المقدار
الأجزاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two bodies with infinite parts can have different magnitudes if the parts themselves have different sizes.
Correction: The author assumes all parts are of equal size, so magnitude is proportional to the number of parts. If both have the same number (infinite), their magnitudes are equal.

Checkpoint
How does the author respond to the objection?
A) He concedes that equality in parts does not imply equality in magnitude.
B) He argues that magnitude is determined by the number of parts.
C) He says that infinite parts cannot exist.
D) He introduces a new proof.

Key Takeaway
The author defends the proof by asserting that magnitude is a function of the number of parts, so equal part count means equal size.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you think about the infinite, do you trust logical reasoning even when it contradicts your imagination? What would it take for you to accept a conclusion that seems counterintuitive?
?
This argument shows that God's power is beyond our finite measuring. In what area of your life do you try to measure God's power by your own finite standards? What would change if you truly believed that His magnitude cannot be compared?
?
The objector tried to avoid the conclusion by splitting hairs. Are there areas in your faith where you use technicalities to avoid submitting to a clear truth? How will you respond to that now?

Strengthened Argument: Potential Parts and the Absurdity of Covering the Sky
Sanusi presents a strengthened version of the proof and its answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the strengthened version of the proof that considers potential parts and the absurd consequence that a mustard seed could cover the sky, and the final answer that potentiality does not imply actuality.

Core Idea
Infinite potential parts do not entail actual infinite parts.

The author now reinforces the argument by considering the potential parts of a body—the parts into which it could be divided. If both sky and mustard seed are infinitely divisible, then their potential parts would be equal in count, and one could theoretically take a part of the mustard seed that covers the entire sky. This absurdity is resolved by distinguishing potentiality from actuality.

Guided Path
1
Read the strengthened argument
Notice that it does not require actual parts; it only considers potential (possible) parts.
2
Identify the absurd consequence
The argument claims that from equal potential parts, one could separate sheets from the mustard seed that would cover the sky. Why is this absurd?
3
Understand the answer about potentiality
The author says that infinite divisibility does not mean that those potential parts can ever become actual. So the separation and covering cannot happen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لو لم يثبت الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ لما كان الجبل أعظم من الخردلة، لأن كلاً منهما حينئذ يكون قابلاً لانقسامات غير متناهية فتكون أجزاء كل منهما غير متناهية من غير تفاضل، وهو معنى التساوي. فإن قيل: غايته لزوم الاستواء في عدد الأجزاء، بأن يكون أجزاء كل منهما غير متناهية العدد، وهو غير محال والمحال استواء مقداريهما، وهو غير لازم. أجيب بأن الاستواء في الأجزاء يستلزم الاستواء في المقدار، ضرورة أن تفاوت المقادير إنما هو بتفاوت الأجزاء، بمعنى أن ما يكون مقداره أعظم تكون أجزاؤه أكثر، فما لا تكون أجزاؤه أكثر لا يكون مقداره أعظم. وقد تقرر هذا الوجه بحيث لا يبتنى على استلزام قبول الانقسام حصول الأقسام، وهو أنه لو كان كل من السماء والخردلة قابلاً للانقسام من غير انتهاء إلى ما لا يقبل الانقسام أصلاً، كانت الأجزاء الممكنة حينئذ في كل منهما متساوية للتي في الأجزاء الآخر، وأمكن أن يفصل من الخردلة صفائح تغمر وجهى السماء، بل أجزاء تغمر الوجهين، وتملأ ما بين السطحين وبطلانه ضروري وجوابه بعد تسليم البطلان ما سبق أن ليس معنى قبول الانقسامات الغير المتناهية إمكان خروجها من القوة إلى الفعل. فمن أين يلزم إمكان حصول أقسام لا نهاية لها، وإمكان انفصالها؟
The second: If the indivisible part were not established, then the mountain would not be greater than the mustard seed, because each of them would then be divisible into infinite divisions, so the parts of each would be infinite without superiority, and that is the meaning of equality. If it is said: Its consequence is only the equality in number of parts, in that the parts of each are infinite in number, which is not impossible, and the impossible is the equality of their magnitudes, which is not necessary. The answer: Equality in parts necessitates equality in magnitude, necessarily because the difference in magnitudes is only due to the difference in parts, meaning that what has greater magnitude has more parts, so what does not have more parts does not have greater magnitude. And its answer, after conceding the falsity, is what came before: that the meaning of accepting infinite divisions is not the possibility of their emergence from potentiality to actuality. So how does there follow the possibility of obtaining infinite parts and the possibility of their separation?

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the indivisible part. It argues that without the indivisible part, a mountain and a mustard seed would be equal in size because both would be infinitely divisible, thus having infinite parts with no superiority — equality (tasawi). An objector then says that the equality is only in the number of parts (both infinite), but that does not imply equality in magnitude, since infinite numbers are equal. Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the final answer, why does the absurd consequence of the mustard seed covering the sky not follow?
A) Because the sky is too big.
B) Because infinite parts cannot exist in potentiality.
C) Because infinite divisibility does not mean potential parts can become actual.
D) Because the mustard seed is too small.

Key Takeaway
Potential infinite parts cannot be actualized, so arguments that require their actualization are invalid.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You learned that potential parts cannot become actual simply because they are possible. In your life, where are you assuming that your good intentions (potential) will inevitably translate into actions (actual)? What is one step you can take today to make a single intention real, before it fades?
?
The argument shows the absurdity of thinking that infinite divisibility allows actual infinite separation. Are there any beliefs you hold that, if pushed to their logical conclusion, lead to absurdity—but you've never examined them? What would it take for you to sincerely question that belief today?

3 min
The Argument from Infinite Extension
States the consequence of infinite divisibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why infinite divisibility of bodies leads to an absurd conclusion, thus proving the existence of an indivisible part.

Core Idea
If bodies can be divided infinitely, even a mustard seed would have infinite extension.

This lesson presents the first part of the third argument for the indivisible part. The author states a conditional: if bodies could be divided without end, then every body, no matter how small, would have infinite extension. This absurdity shows that division must eventually reach a part that cannot be divided further.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: 'if... then...'.
2
Understand the logical structure
Identify the hypothesis (infinite divisibility) and the consequence (infinite extension). Ask why this consequence is impossible.
A mustard seed does not have infinite extension.
3
Reflect on the implication
Since the consequence is absurd, the hypothesis must be false. Therefore, division must end at an indivisible part.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث: أنه لو لم ينته انقسام الجسم إلى ما لا يكون له امتداد وقبول انقسام لزم أن يكون امتداد كل جسم حتى الخردلة غير متناه القدر، لتألفه من امتدادات غير متناهية العدد. ومنها ما يبتنى على كون الحركة عبارة عن حصولات متعاقبة من غير استقرار والزمان عبارة عن آنات متتالية وهو وجه واحد تقريره أن وجود الحركة في المسافة معلوم بالضرورة، مع القطع بأن الماضي منها ليس بموجود الآن، بل حين كان حاضراً، والمستقبل إنما يصير موجوداً حين يصير حاضراً. فالموجود منها هو الحاضر لا غير، وهو لا يقبل الانقسام وإلا لكان شيء منه قبل أو بعد، لكونه غير قار الذات، فلا يكون بتمامه حاضراً هذا خلف. أو نقول لو انقسم الحاضر لكان في الحركة اجتماع أجزاء فيكون قار الذات هذا خلف. وإذا ثبت في الحركة جزء غير منقسم وهي منطبقة على المسافة، بمعنى أن كل جزء منها على جزء منها ثبت في المسافة جزء غير منقسم، لامتناع انطباق غير المنقسم على المنقسم، وهو المطلوب. ثم إذا حاولنا إثبات ما هو المقصود قلنا الحاضر يحصل عقيب انقضاء جزء آخر حاضر غير منقسم يكون هو الموجود من الحركة، وهكذا إلى أن ينتهي. فإذن الحركة مركبة من أجزاء لا تتجزأ. أو قلنا: كل جزء من الحركة حاضر حيناً ما، وكل ما هو حاضر حيناً ما هو غير منقسم بالضرورة، فكل جزء منها غير منقسم، وهو معنى تركبها من أجزاء لا تتجزأ. فكذا المسافة لانطباقها عليها. وقد يستعان في ذلك بالزمان، لأن عدم الاستقرار فيه أظهر، حتى كأنه نفس ماهيته، ولا يتوهم فيه ما يتوهم في الحركة من تخلل سكون أو لزوم وقوع أي جزء منها في زمان قابل للانقسام. فيقال: الموجود منه هو الحاضر الذي لا يقبل الانقسام ولو بالفرض، لأن معناه صحة فرض شيء غير شيء وهذا ينافي عدم الاستقرار الذاتي.
The third: If the division of a body does not end at what has no extension and does not accept division, then the extension of every body, even the mustard seed, would be infinite in quantity, because it is composed of infinite extensions. And among them [the arguments] is what is based on the fact that motion consists of successive occurrences without stability, and time consists of successive moments, and it is one point whose elaboration is that the existence of motion in the distance is known necessarily, with the certainty that the past of it does not exist now, but existed when it was present, and the future only comes to exist when it becomes present. So the existent of it is only the present, and it is not divisible, otherwise part of it would be before or after, because it is non-stable in essence, so it would not be entirely present — this is a contradiction. Or we say: if the present were divided, there would be in motion a gathering of parts, so it would be stable in essence — this is a contradiction. And if an indivisible part is established in motion, and motion conforms to distance, meaning that each part of it corresponds to a part of it, then there is established in distance an indivisible part, because it is impossible for the indivisible to conform to the divisible. That is what is sought. Then if we attempt to prove what is intended, we say: The present occurs after the passing of another present indivisible part, and that is the existent of motion, and so on until it ends. So motion is composed of indivisible parts. Or we say: Every part of motion is present at a certain time, and everything that is present at a certain time is necessarily indivisible, so every part of it is indivisible, and that is the meaning of its being composed of indivisible parts. Similarly for distance because it conforms to it. And assistance is sought in time, because the absence of stability is more apparent in it, even as if it is its very essence, and one does not imagine in it what is imagined in motion of the intrusion of rest or the necessity that any part of it occurs in a divisible time. So it is said: The existent of it is the present which is not divisible even by supposition, because its meaning is the validity of supposing something other than something, and this contradicts the essential lack of stability.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the indivisible part (al-juz' alladhi la yatajazza). The author gives two interconnected arguments: one based on infinite extension and one based on motion and time. The first part: if bodies are infinitely divisible, then every body would have infinite extension because it would be composed of an infinite number of extended parts — an absurdity. The second, more detailed, part: motion (haraka) is known to occur across a distance (masafa). The past part of motion no longer exists, the future does not yet exist, so only the present moment (al-hadir) of motion exists. This present moment is indivisible, for if it were divisible, it would have parts that are before or after, contradicting its being entirely present. Thus motion is composed of indivisible present moments (i.e., instants). Since motion 'applies' (intibaq) to distance (each part of motion corresponds to a part of distance), distance must also be composed of indivisible parts. The author further elaborates that the present moment is followed by another present indivisible moment, so motion is a composition of such parts. The argument is strengthened by considering time (zaman), where absence of stability is even more apparent. In time, the present moment is also indivisible by supposition. Therefore, the existence of the indivisible part is proven.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
الامتداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of bodies being infinitely divisible?
A) They would have finite extension.
B) They would have infinite extension.
C) They would have no extension.
D) They would become atoms.

Key Takeaway
Infinite divisibility leads to absurd infinite extension, so division must end in an indivisible part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that endless division leads to absurdity. Where in your life do you tend to over-analyze or endlessly dissect situations until you lose sight of the whole? What would it mean for you this week to stop at the fundamental truth and act on it?
?
The mustard seed—tiny yet finite—reminds us that everything created has a limit. How does recognizing your own limits (in knowledge, control, or lifespan) change the way you approach your relationship with the Creator? Will you actively practice surrender in one area today?
?
If even the smallest particle is not infinitely divisible, then there is a ultimate reality beneath all appearances. How does that truth affect the way you value the physical world? Are you willing to see the signs of Allah in the very structure of matter, and let that reshape your priorities?

The Argument from Motion and Time
Proves indivisible part from motion's present moment.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the nature of motion and time proves the existence of indivisible parts in both motion and spatial extension.

Core Idea
Each present moment of motion is indivisible, proving motion and space are composed of indivisibles.

This lesson unpacks the second, more detailed argument for the indivisible part. By analyzing motion as a succession of present moments without stability, and noting that each present moment must be indivisible, the author concludes that motion and the distance it traverses are composed of indivisible parts. The argument is reinforced by considering time, where the absence of stability is even more obvious.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the full Arabic passage and translation carefully. Note the references to motion, distance, present moment, and time.
2
Follow the argument on the present moment
The author argues that only the present moment of motion exists, and it must be indivisible. Understand the two versions given.
3
Connect motion to distance
Since each part of motion corresponds to a part of distance, the indivisibility in motion implies indivisibility in distance.
4
Consider the time reinforcement
The argument is strengthened by time, where the present moment is even more clearly indivisible.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think an instant of motion can be divided into smaller units, e.g., milliseconds.
Correction: The argument shows that any division would create parts that are before or after the present, which contradicts the definition of the present moment as entirely present.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
Time is composed of these atoms, each a gift from Allah. How many of these atoms have you wasted in heedlessness? Will you wait for another 'now' to repent, or will you seize this indivisible present to turn back to Him?

Motion Differences Disprove the Indivisible Part
Argument from differential motion speed as proof against atomism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of differential motion speed is used as a proof against the existence of an indivisible part (atom) in classical Islamic theology.

Core Idea
Speed differences in motion cannot be explained by inserted moments of rest.

This passage presents a sophisticated physical argument against atomism, which claimed that motion is composed of discrete jumps with rest in between. The author argues that if that were true, then slower motion would have more rests, but the observed differences in speed, especially in examples like a millstone or a moving man, show that such rests are either impossible or lead to absurdities. Let's examine the argument and its supporting examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the core argument
Read the first part of the passage where the author states that the difference in speed is not due to inserted rests, either because it's impossible or leads to absurdity. Identify the two reasons given.
The two reasons are: impossibility in itself, or leading to false conclusions like disintegration of contiguous objects.
2
Study the six illustrative examples
The author provides six physical scenarios that demonstrate the speed difference. For each, consider how the motion of different parts or objects relates in speed and why rests cannot explain it.
Examples include a millstone, a compass, a turning man, celestial motions, shadows, and a bucket-clamp system.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
The passage concludes by restating that the argument negates the indivisible part. Ask yourself how the examples collectively undermine atomism.
The conclusion ties back to the principle that rests cannot account for speed variation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تفاوت الحركتين دليل على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ قوله (ومنها) [ما يبتنى على أن ليس البطء لتحلل السكنات إما لاستحالته في نفسه أو لتأديه إلى ما هو ظاهر الانتفاء من تفكك المتصلات، وانفكاك المتلازمات، ويقرر ذلك في صور أحدها : حركة طرفي الرحى. الثاني : حركة الفرجار ذي الشعب الثلاث . الثالث: حركة عقب الإنسان وأطرافه حين يدور على نفسه. الرابع : حركة المنطقة والمدارات التي تقرب القطب . الخامس : حركة الشمس، وظل الشجر. السادس: حركة الدلو المشدود (١) على طرف حبل مشدود (٢) طرفه الآخر في وسط البئر قد جعل فيه كلاب يمد به الحبل. فالدلو تقطع مسافة البئر حين ما تقطع الكلاب نصفها]. أي ومن طرق الاحتجاج على نفي الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ ما يبتنى على أن تفاوت الحركتين بالسرعة والبطء ليس لتخلل (٣) سكنات بين أجزاء الحركة البطيئة، إما لكونه مستحيلاً في نفسه بما ذكر عليه من الدليل، وإما لاستلزامه أمراً معلوم
The difference between two motions is a proof for negating the part that does not divide. He said (including): [It is based on that slowness is not due to the insertion of rests (sakanāt), either because it is impossible in itself or because it leads to what is obviously false, such as the disintegrating of contiguous things and the separation of necessary concomitants. And that is established in several forms: one: the motion of the two ends of a millstone. Two: the motion of a compass with three legs. Three: the motion of a man's heel and limbs when he turns around himself. Four: the motion of the region and the circles near the pole. Five: the motion of the sun and the shadow of trees. Six: the motion of a bucket tied (1) to the end of a rope whose other end is tied (2) in the middle of a well, in which a clamp (kilāb) is placed to pull the rope. So the bucket traverses the distance of the well when the clamp traverses half of it]. That is, among the methods of argument for negating the part that does not divide is what is based on that the difference between two motions in speed and slowness is not due to the interpenetration (3) of rests between the parts of the slow motion, either because it is impossible in itself, as the proof was given for it, or because it necessitates something known [to be false].
Source pages p. 42

Commentary
This passage is a proof against the indivisible part (atom) based on the phenomenon of differential motion speed. The main idea is that the difference in speed between two motions is not due to the insertion of quiescent instants (sakanāt) between the parts of the slower motion, because that is either impossible in itself or leads to absurd consequences like the breaking apart of continuous objects or separation of inseparables. The argument is then illustrated with six physical examples: (1) the two ends of a millstone moving at different speeds; (2) a compass with three legs; (3) a man's heel and limbs when turning; (4) the motion of the celestial region near the pole; (5) the motion of the sun and tree shadows; (6) a bucket in a well with a clamp system. In the last example, the bucket traverses the entire well when the clamp traverses only half, showing a speed difference that cannot be explained by inserted rests. The passage concludes by restating that the argument establishes that speed variation is not due to interpenetration of rests in the slower motion, either because rests are impossible or because they would entail known absurdities.

Key Terms
الجزء الذي لا يتجزأ
السكنات
تفاوت الحركتين
البطء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Speed differences occur because slower motions have more inserted moments of rest (sakanāt).
Correction: The passage argues that such an explanation is either impossible in itself or leads to absurd consequences, thus atomism is false.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main point used to negate the indivisible part?
A) The impossibility of motion altogether
B) The difference in speed between two motions cannot be due to inserted rests
C) The existence of indivisible atoms is proven by logic
D) The six examples show that all motion is uniform

Key Takeaway
The observable difference in motion speed disproves the atomistic concept of discrete motion with intervening rests.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the world around you, do you see motion as a continuous flow or as a series of snapshots? How would your view of God's creation change if you realized that every movement is seamless and sustained by His power?
?
This argument uses physical examples to disprove a philosophical claim. Have you ever held a belief that seemed plausible until you examined the real-world evidence? What would it take for you to abandon a deeply held assumption if logic and experience contradicted it?
?
The author says that slowness cannot be explained by inserting rests because it leads to absurdities. In your own life, do you sometimes insert 'rests'—excuses or distractions—to avoid facing the truth? What might happen if you stopped allowing those rests and confronted the motion of your own soul toward its purpose?

The Atom's Volume, Area, and Length in Kalam
Debates on atom's extension: volume, area, length.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the debates among kalam scholars about whether a single atom has volume, area, and length, and the specific positions of Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and Abu Hashim.

Core Idea
Atom's extension: a shared concept, but its details deeply divided the Mu'tazila.

This passage opens a window into the intricate debates among early mutakallimun about the properties of the indivisible atom. While all agreed that atoms compose bodies, they disagreed on whether an atom itself possesses spatial dimensions like length and area. The passage highlights the agreement and disagreement between two leading Mu'tazila scholars, Abu Ali and his son Abu Hashim, on this issue.

Guided Path
1
Understand the concept of volume (hajm)
Read how the passage defines volume as something with extension and magnitude, and how it relates to body and composition.
Volume is more apparent than the atom itself, as it implies spatial extent.
2
Identify the Mu'tazila positions on area and length
Note that the Mu'tazila generally said the atom has a share of area, and Ibn al-Rawandi added that it has a share of length.
This sets the stage for the specific disagreement between Abu Ali and Abu Hashim.
3
Examine the agreement on length
Both Abu Ali and Abu Hashim agreed that the atom has no share of length, because length depends on composition of parts in a direction.
4
Analyze the disagreement on area
Abu Ali denied area for the atom, while Abu Hashim affirmed it based on the atom's spatial occupation (tahayyuz) and bulk (jirm).
This is the core doctrinal split presented in this passage.

قبول الانقسام، بل ربما كان ذلك في الحجم أظهر لأنه اسم لما له امتداد ومقدار ما، بحيث إذا كان ذلك في الجهات كان جسماً، وإن أريد أن له مدخلاً في الحجمية والمساحة حيث يزيد ذلك بزيادة الأجزاء، فكذا في الطول والعرض، والمذكور في كلام المعتزلة أن له حظاً من المساحة، ومن الطول عند ابن الراوندي، واتفق أبو علي وأبو هاشم، على أن لاحظ له من الطول، لأن مرجعه إلى التأليف الذي تذهب به الأجزاء في جهة مخصوصة، ثم اختلفا فذهب أبو علي إلى أن لاحظ له من المساحة لأنها أيضاً باعتبار التأليف، وذهب أبو هاشم إلى أن له حظاً من المساحة لأنها اسم لتحيز الجوهر وجرمه الموجب لتكاثفه عند انضمام أمثاله إليه، ومنها اختلافهم في أن الجوهر الواحد هل يوصف بالجهات؟ وفي أنه هل يجوز أن يرى؟ وفي أنه هل يجوز أن يصير بثقل الجبل؟ وفي أنه كم يجوز أن يلقاه من الجواهر؟ وفي أنه هل يجوز أن يخلقه الله تعالى على الانفراد؟ وفي أنه هل يجوز أن تحله الحركة والسكون على البدل؟ وفي أنه هل يجوز أن تحله أعراض كثيرة؟ وتفاصيل ذلك مذكورة في المطولات، ونحن لا نبالي أن كتابنا إلى القصور بإعوازه لما لا طائل فيه، ونسأل الله سبحانه لمن اجتهد في نفض ذلك الغبار عن الكلام شكر مساعيه
acceptance of division, but perhaps that is more apparent in volume because it is a name for what has extension and some magnitude, such that if that is in directions, it is a body. And if it is intended that it has a role in voluminousness and area, since that increases with the increase of parts, so too in length and width. And what is mentioned in the speech of the Mu'tazila is that it has a share of area, and of length according to Ibn al-Rawandi. Abu Ali and Abu Hashim agreed that it has no share of length, because its reference is to composition by which parts move in a particular direction. Then they differed: Abu Ali held that it has no share of area because it too is by way of composition, while Abu Hashim held that it has a share of area because it is a name for the occupying of space (tahayyuz) of the atom and its bulk (jirm) which necessitates its condensation when its likes are joined to it. And among them is their disagreement on whether a single atom is described by directions? And on whether it can be seen? And on whether it can become as heavy as a mountain? And on how many atoms can meet it? And on whether God can create it alone? And on whether motion and rest can successively inhere in it? And on whether many accidents can inhere in it? And the details of that are mentioned in lengthy works. And we do not care that our book falls short due to its lack of what is of no benefit. And we ask God to reward with thanks for his efforts whoever strove to clear that dust from kalam.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage lists the disagreements among the mutakallimun who hold that bodies are composed of indivisible atoms (jawhar fard). It begins with the discussion on whether an atom has a share of volume, area, length, and width. The author notes that the Mu'tazila said the atom has a share of area, and Ibn al-Rawandi said it has length. Abu Ali al-Jubba'i and his son Abu Hashim agreed it has no length but differed on area: Abu Ali denied it, Abu Hashim affirmed it because he defined area as the spatial occupation of the atom that causes densification when more atoms join. Then the author lists several other disputed questions: whether the atom can be described with directions, whether it can be seen, whether it can become heavy like a mountain, how many atoms can meet it, whether God can create it alone, whether motion and rest can alternate in it, and whether many accidents can inhere in it. The author dismisses these details as belonging to longer works and expresses that his own book does not concern itself with such fruitless inquiries, praying for those who have clarified them.

Key Terms
الجوهر
الجزء
الحجم
المساحة
التحيز
الجرم
التأليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All Mu'tazila agreed on the atom's extended attributes.
Correction: The passage shows clear disagreement: Abu Ali and Abu Hashim differed on area, and Ibn al-Rawandi added length not accepted by others.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why did Abu Ali and Abu Hashim agree that the atom has no share of length?
A) Because length is an accident that cannot inhere in an indivisible substance.
B) Because length refers to composition by which parts move in a particular direction.
C) Because length is a divine attribute not applicable to atoms.
D) Because the atom is too small to have length.

Key Takeaway
The atom's possession of area was debated: for Abu Hashim it is intrinsic, for Abu Ali it is merely a result of composition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The scholars disagreed on whether the atom's area is intrinsic or from composition. This shows that even 'obvious' physical concepts can be understood differently. In your own faith, are there doctrines you hold as 'obvious' that might actually be subject to deeper disagreement? How can you remain humble without losing conviction?
?
Abu Hashim argued that the atom's area is part of its very nature (tahayyuz and jirm). When you reflect on your own beliefs about Allah's attributes, do you affirm them as intrinsic realities, or do you unconsciously reduce them to mere effects of other causes? How can this lesson deepen your awe of the divine nature?

Disputed Attributes of the Atom: A List of Questions
Enumeration of seven disputed attributes of the atom.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list the seven disputed questions about the atom's attributes that the author mentions, and understand why he dismisses them as fruitless.

Core Idea
Seven unresolved questions about the atom that even kalam scholars left unsettled.

After discussing extension, the author turns to a series of other disagreements among atomists: can an atom be described by directions? Can it be seen? Can it become as heavy as a mountain? How many atoms can meet it? Can God create it alone? Can motion and rest alternate in it? Can many accidents inhere in it? These questions were debated in longer works, but the author of this book dismisses them as having little benefit.

Guided Path
1
Read the list of seven questions
Identify the seven distinct questions the author enumerates. They are all introduced by 'هل' (whether).
These are not arguments but open problems.
2
Understand the author's attitude
Notice that the author says details are in 'lengthy works' and he does not care that his book lacks such fruitless discussions.
This reflects his methodological stance: avoid overly speculative topics.
3
Reflect on the purpose of listing
Ask why the author includes this list if he finds it useless. Possibly to acknowledge the debates and then set them aside.
This is a teaching moment about scholarly priorities.

A Direct Proof for Specific Forms
A simpler argument: we know water and fire differ essentially, so there must be a substantial form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the certainty of essential differences between substances proves the existence of specific forms.

Core Idea
Certainty about essential differences demands a substantial form.

This lesson presents a straightforward argument for specific forms. The author argues that we are absolutely certain that water and fire are essentially different, yet they share matter and corporeal form. This difference must come from a substantial constituent unique to each, which is the specific form. This proof relies on direct certainty, not on empirical observations, making it a foundational argument in philosophical theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the original Arabic once without translating. Let the structure sink in.
Notice the phrase 'نحن نقطع' — it asserts certainty.
2
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms: حقيقة, مادة, صورة جسمية, مقوم جوهري, صورة نوعية. Understand their meanings.
These terms are essential for the argument.
3
Follow the logical chain
Map out the premises: certainty of difference, shared matter and corporeal form, then the conclusion: necessary difference via substantial constituent.
The argument is concise: two premises lead to one conclusion.
4
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Consider why someone might think the difference is accidental. The passage shows why it must be substantial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبهذا يظهر أنه يكفي في إثبات الصور النوعية أن يقال: نحن نقطع باختلاف حقيقتي الماء والنار مع الاشتراك في المادة والصور الجسمية، فلا بد من الاختلاف بمقوم جوهري نسميه الصورة النوعية.
And by this it appears that it suffices for proving specific forms to say: We are certain of the difference between the realities of water and fire despite their sharing matter and corporeal forms, so there must be difference by a substantial constituent which we call the specific form.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents a simpler and direct proof for specific forms (الصور النوعية). The author states that we are certain (نقطع) that the reality (حقيقة) of water is different from the reality of fire, even though both share prime matter (المادة) and the corporeal form (الصورة الجسمية). Since matter and corporeal form are common to all bodies, the difference must be due to a substantial constituent (مقوم جوهري) that individuates each kind. This constituent is what we call the specific form (الصورة النوعية). The argument relies on our direct certainty of the essential differences between substances, and it does not depend on empirical effects. It is sufficient to establish the existence of specific forms without further complex reasoning. The phrase 'وبهدا يظهر' refers back to the previous discussion, but the proof stands on its own.

Key Terms

حقيقة
مادة
صورة جسمية
مقوم جوهري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The difference between water and fire is merely accidental (e.g., due to temperature or fluidity).
Correction: The author argues that the difference is substantial, because it persists despite sharing matter and corporeal form; accidents cannot account for the essential difference in reality.

Checkpoint

A) The specific form
B) The corporeal form
C) Prime matter
D) An accident

Key Takeaway
The certain essential difference between substances like water and fire proves the existence of specific forms as their substantial constituents.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you are absolutely certain that water and fire differ in their very realities, why do you often treat your own soul as if it were only a collection of accidental moods and thoughts? What does ignoring its substantial form say about your awareness of your own essence before Allah?
?
You just read that sharing matter and corporeal form does not prevent essential difference—so what does that mean for how you look at fellow human beings who share the same body type or ancestry? Do you see them as essentially different souls, or do you reduce them to accidental features?
?
If the certain difference between water and fire proves a substantial form, then what does your certainty about the difference between truth and falsehood prove about the nature of knowledge? How does your daily search for truth reflect—or fail to reflect—that reality?

First Argument: Succession of Accidents
Presents the first argument for substantial forms based on succession of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observed recurrence of accidents after their disappearance logically necessitates the existence of a stable substantial form that underlies them.

Core Idea
Accidents that return after vanishing reveal a hidden substantial form beneath.

In this passage, the author introduces the first of two philosophical arguments for the existence of substantial forms (الصور النوعية). He argues that the observed recurrence of accidents—such as heat returning to water after it is removed—cannot be explained by changeable accidents alone; rather, it requires a stable underlying principle. This argument uses the logical relationship of preparedness and succession to demonstrate that bodies possess a substantial form that persists through accidental changes.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Note the key terms: 'صور', 'أعراض', 'استعدادات'.
2
Identify the logical structure
The passage presents an analogy between forms and accidents. Find the premise, analogy, mechanism, and conclusion.
The conclusion is implicit: the succession proves substantial forms.
3
Reflect on the concept of preparedness
The author attributes the difference of accidents to differences in preparedness. What does this imply about the underlying form?
Preparedness is linked to the form's receptivity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أنه كما لا يمتنع تعاقب الصور على الإطلاق، لا يمتنع الأعراض التي يستند إليها ما يعود بعد الزوال، فيكون كل سابق معداً للاحق، ويرجع اختلافها إلى اختلاف الاستعدادات، وإن كان المبدأ واحداً، وقد يقال نحن نعلم بالضرورة أن ههنا آثاراً صادرة عن الأجسام كالإحراق للنار، والترطيب للماء، فلو لم يكن فيها إلا الهيولي والصورة الجسمية لما كان كذلك، فلا بد فيها من أمور هي مبادىء تلك الآثار ولا خفاء في أن الأجسام إنما تختلف بحسب آثارها المخصوصة بتنوع نوع، فتنوعها وتحصلها إنما يكون باعتبار تلك المبادىء، فتكون صوراً لا أعراضاً لامتناع تقوم الجوهر بالعرض. وحينئذ يندفع ما يقال : لم لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الآثار مستندة إلى الفاعل المختار؟ أو يكون لبعض المفارقات خصوصية بالقياس إلى بعض الأجسام دون بعض؟ أو يكون اختلاف الآثار عن المفارق بحسب اختلاف استعدادات الأجسام وهيوليتها؟ وبهذا يظهر أنه يكفي في إثبات الصور النوعية أن يقال: نحن نقطع باختلاف حقيقتي الماء والنار مع الاشتراك في [؟] والصور الجسمية، فلا بد من الاختلاف بمقوم جوهري نسميه الصورة النوعية.
The conclusion is that just as the succession of forms is not impossible absolutely, so likewise the accidents to which what returns after passing away is based are not impossible, so each preceding one is preparatory for the succeeding one, and their difference goes back to difference of preparedness, even if the principle is one. And it may be said: we know by necessity that there are effects emanating from bodies, like the burning of fire and the moistening of water. So if there were nothing in them except hyle and corporeal form, it would not be so. Thus there must be in them things that are principles of those effects, and there is no hiddenness in that bodies only differ according to their specific effects by a diversity of kind. So their diversity and actualization is only by consideration of those principles, so they are forms not accidents, because it is impossible for a substance to be constituted by an accident. And then what is said is repelled: why is it not permissible that those effects be based on the free agent? Or that some separate substances have a specificity with respect to some bodies without others? Or that the difference of effects from the separate be according to difference of the preparedness of bodies and their hyle? And by this it appears that it suffices for proving specific forms to say: we are certain of the difference of the reality of water and fire while sharing in [؟] and corporeal forms, so there must be difference by a substantial differentiator which we call specific form.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments for the existence of substantial forms (الصور النوعية). The first argument begins with ‘والحاصل أنه’ and is based on the succession of accidents. The author states that just as forms (presumably substantial forms) can succeed each other, so accidents which underlie what returns after passing away are also not impossible. Each preceding accident prepares for the succeeding one, and their variation is due to differences in preparedness, even if the principle (the first cause) is one. This argument uses the recurrence of accidents after their disappearance to posit a stable substrate (the form) that provides continuity.

The second argument is introduced with ‘وقد يقال’ and relies on the empirical observation that bodies have specific effects (e.g., fire burns, water moistens). If bodies consisted only of hyle (prime matter) and corporeal form, they would be undifferentiated and produce no such specific effects. Therefore, there must be additional principles (مبادىء) within bodies that are the sources of these effects. Since bodies differ by their specific effects, their diversity must be due to these principles. These principles must be forms (صور), not accidents, because a substance (الجوهر) cannot be constituted by an accident. The author then repels three possible objections: that effects could come directly from the free agent (الفاعل المختار), that separate substances could have special relations to particular bodies, or that the separate substance could act differently due to different preparations of bodies. The author concludes that it is sufficient to prove specific forms by observing the difference between water and fire despite their sharing in hyle and corporeal form, so there must be a substantial differentiator (مقوم جوهري) which is the specific form.

Key Terms
صور
أعراض
استعدادات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Accidents can exist and succeed each other without any substantial substrate.
Correction: The argument shows that accidents require a persisting substance (the substantial form) to inhere in, and their succession implies a stable underlying principle.

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what does the succession of accidents indicate?
A) The existence of a stable substantial form
B) Accidents are eternal
C) Bodies are composed of atoms
D) Change is an illusion

Key Takeaway
The succession of accidents demonstrates the need for a persisting substantial form as their substrate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have witnessed countless changes in your own body and life: moods, actions, thoughts coming and going. Do you truly believe that there is a stable self (your soul) that persists beneath all these transient accidents? If you knew that every change you go through is merely a new accident with no continuous substrate, how would that change your sense of identity and responsibility before Allah? Can you point to one accident that has returned in your life (e.g., a renewed intention to pray) and recognize the hidden substantial form (your soul) that made that return possible?
?
The argument shows that differences in accidents are due to differences in preparedness. In what area of your spiritual life have you neglected to prepare yourself (the substrate) for the accidents of guidance and repentance? What one step will you take today to adjust your preparedness so that the accident of khushu' (humility) returns to your prayer?
?
If the principle (the First Cause) is one, yet your actions vary, the difference is in your preparedness. Are you blaming external factors or Allah for your inconsistent state of faith, when the real cause is your own inner preparedness? Write down one change in your inner state that you can make right now to invite the return of a good accident you have lost.

Second Argument: Specific Effects and Refutation of Objections
Presents the second argument for substantial forms based on specific effects of bodies, with refutation of three objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinct specific effects of bodies (e.g., fire burns, water moistens) compel us to posit intrinsic substantial forms, and how three common objections based on divine agency or separate substances are refuted.

Core Idea
Different bodies have different essential effects, which forces us to posit intrinsic forms as their principles.

The author now presents a stronger argument grounded in empirical observation: fire burns, water moistens. If bodies consisted only of prime matter (hyle) and corporeal form, they would be indistinguishable and produce no such specific actions. Therefore, there must be additional internal principles that are the sources of these effects. The author then anticipates and refutes three objections, including the idea that a free agent (God) could directly cause the effects, or that separate substances (angels) could be responsible, or that differences in matter's preparedness alone could explain it. The conclusion is a concise formulation: the essential difference between water and fire, despite their sharing hyle and corporeal form, proves a substantial differentiator which is the specific form.

Guided Path
1
Read the second argument
Read from 'وقد يقال' to the end. Notice the empirical starting point: specific effects of bodies.
2
Trace the deduction
Follow the reasoning: if only hyle and corporeal form, then no specific effects → need additional principles → those must be forms, not accidents.
The key point is the impossibility of substance being constituted by accident.
3
Examine the three objections
List the three objections: free agent, separate substances, and preparedness. How does the author refute each? They are repelled by the preceding argument.
The author concludes the overall argument is sufficient.
4
Consider the final summary
The author states that the known difference between water and fire, despite shared hyle and corporeal form, proves a substantial differentiator.
This is a concise formulation of the entire argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والحاصل أنه كما لا يمتنع تعاقب الصور على الإطلاق، لا يمتنع الأعراض التي يستند إليها ما يعود بعد الزوال، فيكون كل سابق معداً للاحق، ويرجع اختلافها إلى اختلاف الاستعدادات، وإن كان المبدأ واحداً، وقد يقال نحن نعلم بالضرورة أن ههنا آثاراً صادرة عن الأجسام كالإحراق للنار، والترطيب للماء، فلو لم يكن فيها إلا الهيولي والصورة الجسمية لما كان كذلك، فلا بد فيها من أمور هي مبادىء تلك الآثار ولا خفاء في أن الأجسام إنما تختلف بحسب آثارها المخصوصة بتنوع نوع، فتنوعها وتحصلها إنما يكون باعتبار تلك المبادىء، فتكون صوراً لا أعراضاً لامتناع تقوم الجوهر بالعرض. وحينئذ يندفع ما يقال : لم لا يجوز أن تكون تلك الآثار مستندة إلى الفاعل المختار؟ أو يكون لبعض المفارقات خصوصية بالقياس إلى بعض الأجسام دون بعض؟ أو يكون اختلاف الآثار عن المفارق بحسب اختلاف استعدادات الأجسام وهيوليتها؟ وبهذا يظهر أنه يكفي في إثبات الصور النوعية أن يقال: نحن نقطع باختلاف حقيقتي الماء والنار مع الاشتراك في [؟] والصور الجسمية، فلا بد من الاختلاف بمقوم جوهري نسميه الصورة النوعية.
The conclusion is that just as the succession of forms is not impossible absolutely, so likewise the accidents to which what returns after passing away is based are not impossible, so each preceding one is preparatory for the succeeding one, and their difference goes back to difference of preparedness, even if the principle is one. And it may be said: we know by necessity that there are effects emanating from bodies, like the burning of fire and the moistening of water. So if there were nothing in them except hyle and corporeal form, it would not be so. Thus there must be in them things that are principles of those effects, and there is no hiddenness in that bodies only differ according to their specific effects by a diversity of kind. So their diversity and actualization is only by consideration of those principles, so they are forms not accidents, because it is impossible for a substance to be constituted by an accident. And then what is said is repelled: why is it not permissible that those effects be based on the free agent? Or that some separate substances have a specificity with respect to some bodies without others? Or that the difference of effects from the separate be according to difference of the preparedness of bodies and their hyle? And by this it appears that it suffices for proving specific forms to say: we are certain of the difference of the reality of water and fire while sharing in [؟] and corporeal forms, so there must be difference by a substantial differentiator which we call specific form.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage presents two arguments for the existence of substantial forms (الصور النوعية). The first argument begins with ‘والحاصل أنه’ and is based on the succession of accidents. The author states that just as forms (presumably substantial forms) can succeed each other, so accidents which underlie what returns after passing away are also not impossible. Each preceding accident prepares for the succeeding one, and their variation is due to differences in preparedness, even if the principle (the first cause) is one. This argument uses the recurrence of accidents after their disappearance to posit a stable substrate (the form) that provides continuity.

The second argument is introduced with ‘وقد يقال’ and relies on the empirical observation that bodies have specific effects (e.g., fire burns, water moistens). If bodies consisted only of hyle (prime matter) and corporeal form, they would be undifferentiated and produce no such specific effects. Therefore, there must be additional principles (مبادىء) within bodies that are the sources of these effects. Since bodies differ by their specific effects, their diversity must be due to these principles. These principles must be forms (صور), not accidents, because a substance (الجوهر) cannot be constituted by an accident. The author then repels three possible objections: that effects could come directly from the free agent (الفاعل المختار), that separate substances could have special relations to particular bodies, or that the separate substance could act differently due to different preparations of bodies. The author concludes that it is sufficient to prove specific forms by observing the difference between water and fire despite their sharing in hyle and corporeal form, so there must be a substantial differentiator (مقوم جوهري) which is the specific form.

Key Terms
هيولي
صورة جسمية
صورة نوعية
فاعل مختار
مفارقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The specific effects of bodies are due solely to external causes such as God's direct action or angels.
Correction: The author argues that such external agency cannot account for the intrinsic and constant nature of these effects; rather, there must be internal substantial forms as their principles.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the principles of specific effects must be forms rather than accidents?
A) Accidents cannot cause effects
B) Because a substance cannot be constituted by an accident
C) Because forms are eternal
D) Because accidents are only in the mind

Key Takeaway
The diversity of specific effects in bodies demands internal substantial forms as their principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the consistent effects of fire or water, do you ever consider that these are not just random events but reflect a deep, intrinsic nature placed by God? How does this awareness change your trust in the order of creation?
?
The author argues that the diversity of bodies is due to substantial forms, not mere accidents. In your own life, do you sometimes treat the unique qualities of people or things as mere externals, rather than recognizing their God-given essences? What would it take to see them with deeper sight?
?
The passage refutes the idea that effects could be solely from external causes. In your own life, do you often look for external explanations for your actions and states, rather than acknowledging your own inner disposition? How can you take responsibility for the 'effects' you produce?

The Similarity of Bodies and Their Atomistic Foundation
Definition of the similarity of bodies and its atomistic basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the similarity of bodies and how it is grounded in the doctrine of indivisible atoms.

Core Idea
All bodies share the same essence; differences are merely accidental.

The author now turns to the properties of bodies. The first and most foundational property is their similarity (tamathul). This lesson unpacks what this similarity means and why the mutakallimun base it on the concept of indivisible atoms.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the definition of similarity
Notice that the author states bodies are similar, not differing except by accidents. This is the first key point.
The term 'accidents' (a'rad) is crucial.
3
Understand the atomistic basis
The author explains that this similarity is because bodies are composed of similar indivisible atoms. Also note the shared properties of being distinguished and receiving accidents.
These two properties are said to be among the most specific attributes of the soul.
4
Reflect on the importance
The passage hints at theological implications. Consider why this principle might be foundational for proving a Chosen Agent and miracles.
This will be developed in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس في أحكام الأجسام فمنها أنها متماثلة لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض، ويجوز على كل ما يجوز على الآخر، ويبتني على ذلك استناد اختلاف العوارض إلى القادر المختار، وخرق السموات وكثير من خوارق العادات، وذلك لكونها من محض الجواهر الفردة المتماثلة، ولاشتراكها في التميز وقبول الأعراض، وهما من أخص صفات النفس وانقسام الجسم إليها . وقد يتوهم أن المراد بتماثلها الاتحاد في المفهوم المشترك بين الأنواع المختلفة، كالحيوان مثلاً، فيستدل بأن حد الجسم على اختلاف عباراتهم فيه واحد غير مشتمل على تنويع واختلاف الخواص إنما هو لاختلاف الأنواع . بعد الفراغ من بيان حقيقة الجسم أخذ في بيان أحكامه، فمنها أن الأجسام متماثلة أي متحدة الحقيقة ، وإنما الاختلاف بالعوارض ، وهذا أصل يبتني عليه كثير من قواعد الإسلام ، كإثبات القادر المختار ، وكثير من أحوال النبوة والمعاد، فإن اختصاص كل جسم بصفاته المعينة لا بد أن يكون بمرجح مختار، إذ نسبة المرجب إلى الكل على السواء، ولما جاز على كل جسم ما يجوز على الآخر كالبرد على النار، والحرق على الماء ثبت جواز ما نقل من المعجزات، وأحوال القيامة، ومبنى هذا الأصل عند المتكلمين على أن أجزاء الجسم ليست إلا الجواهر الفردة وانها متماثلة لا يتصور فيها اختلاف حقيقة ولا محيص لمن اعترف بتماثل الجواهر، واختلاف الأجسام بالحقيقة من جعل بعض الأعراض داخلة فيها
Fifth section: On the properties of bodies. Among them is that they are similar, not differing except by accidents, and whatever is permissible for one is permissible for the other. Upon this is based the attribution of difference in accidents to the Chosen Agent, and the rending of the heavens and many miraculous events. This is because they consist purely of similar indivisible atoms, and because they share in being distinguished and in receiving accidents—these two being among the most specific attributes of the soul and the division of the body into them. And it might be mistakenly thought that what is meant by their similarity is union in the common concept among different species, like 'animal' for example, and then it is inferred that the definition of body, despite the differences in their formulations, is one, not including diversification, and that the difference in properties is due to difference in species. After completing the clarification of the reality of the body, he proceeds to clarify its properties. Among them is that bodies are similar, i.e., unified in reality, and difference is only through accidents. This is a principle upon which many foundations of Islam are built, such as proving the Chosen Agent, and many matters of prophethood and the resurrection. For the specification of each body with its specific qualities must be by a choosing determiner, since the relation of the necessary to all is equal. And since whatever is permissible for one body is permissible for the other, like cold for fire and burning for water, the permissibility of what is reported of miracles and states of the resurrection is established. And the basis of this principle among the theologians (mutakallimun) is that the parts of a body are nothing but indivisible atoms, and that they are similar, with no difference in reality conceivable. And there is no escape for anyone who acknowledges the similarity of atoms, and the difference of bodies in reality is due to including some accidents in them.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a classification and proof. The author explains the fifth section on the properties of bodies. First, he states that bodies are similar (mutamathila), meaning they share the same reality and differ only by accidents (a'rad). From this it follows that whatever is possible for one body is possible for another. This principle underlies the need for a Chosen Agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar) to account for differences, as well as the possibility of miracles and heavenly occurrences. The basis for this similarity, according to the mutakallimun, is that bodies are composed of identical indivisible atoms (jawahir farda). Then the author addresses a potential misunderstanding: some might think that 'similarity' means sharing a common concept (like 'animal') across different species, and then argue that the definition of body is the same without diversification, and that differences in properties are due to different species. The author implies this is mistaken. He then reiterates: after clarifying the reality of the body, he now explains its properties. Again, bodies are similar in essence, differing only by accidents. This is a foundational principle for many Islamic doctrines: proving the Chosen Agent (since equal bodies require an external determiner), and establishing the possibility of miracles and the resurrection (since what is possible for one body is possible for another). The theologians ground this in atomism: all atoms are similar, and any difference in bodies comes from accidents included in them.

Key Terms
متماثلة
الجواهر الفردة
العوارض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what does it mean that bodies are similar?
A) They share the same material composition.
B) They differ only by accidents.
C) They are all divisible.
D) They are all created at the same time.

Key Takeaway
The similarity of bodies means they are identical in essence; all differences are due to accidents, and this is grounded in the atomic theory of the mutakallimun.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If all bodies are essentially the same, every difference you see—from the strength of a mountain to the fragility of a leaf—is an accident placed by Allah. Do you truly live as though nothing in creation has independent power? What would change if you acted on this truth daily?
?
The passage grounds miracles in the similarity of atoms. When you hear of a miracle, do you find yourself doubting, thinking it impossible? What does your hesitation reveal about your belief in Allah's absolute control over all accidents?
?
Your own body is a collection of similar atoms. Every attribute—health, strength, appearance—is an accident given by Allah. Are you more attached to these accidents or to the One who grants them? What would it take for you to detach from them now?

Theological Implications of Body Similarity and a Clarification
Theological implications of body similarity and clarification of a misunderstanding.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the similarity of bodies supports the proof of a Chosen Agent, miracles, and the resurrection, and why it is wrong to interpret similarity as a common concept.

Core Idea
Body similarity necessitates an external chooser and makes the miraculous possible.

The similarity of bodies is not just a metaphysical claim—it has profound theological consequences. This lesson explores how this principle is used to demonstrate the need for a freely choosing Creator and to justify the possibility of miracles. We also address a common misunderstanding that would undermine this principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for structure
First, read the complete Arabic passage and its translation. Note that it begins by addressing a potential misunderstanding and then restates the principle before drawing conclusions.
2
Identify the misunderstanding
Look for the phrase 'وقد يتوهم' ('it might be mistakenly thought'). Understand what the wrong interpretation is and why it is rejected.
The misunderstanding is that similarity means sharing a common concept like 'animal'.
3
Follow the theological reasoning
After restating the principle, the author lists its implications: proving the Chosen Agent and validating miracles and resurrection. Follow the logic carefully.
The key is that equal bodies need an external determiner, and what is possible for one body is possible for another.
4
Trace the atomistic foundation
The passage concludes by linking back to atomism. Notice how the similarity of atoms is the ultimate basis.
This ties the theological conclusions to the physical theory.

Fifth section: On the properties of bodies. Among them is that they are similar, not differing except by accidents, and whatever is permissible for one is permissible for the other. Upon this is based the attribution of difference in accidents to the Chosen Agent, and the rending of the heavens and many miraculous events. Commentary
