The Principle of the Similarity of Bodies and Its Role in Proving the Free Agent
Introduces the principle of body similarity and its foundations in atomism and theology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why all bodies are considered essentially similar in kalam, how this is grounded in atomist theory, and how it underpins proofs for a free creator and miracles.

Core Idea
Bodies are identical in essence; differences are only in accidents, pointing to a free agent.

This lesson establishes the foundational principle that all bodies are identical in reality (mutamathila) and only differ by accidents. This might seem like a minor metaphysical point, but it is the cornerstone of major Islamic theological arguments, including the necessity of a freely choosing agent to account for the variety in the world and the possibility of miracles.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then the translation. Note the author's systematic presentation from principle to basis to implications.
2
Identify the principle and its basis
The passage states that bodies are similar (متماثلة) and differ only by accidents. The basis is that bodies are composed of identical simple atoms (الجواهر الفردة المتماثلة).
This atomist foundation is key to the argument.
3
Understand the theological implications
The similarity of bodies leads to the need for a preponderator (مرجح) to account for differences in accidents, which proves the Free Agent. It also establishes the possibility of miracles, since what is possible for one body is possible for another.
4
Examine the weak arguments
The author mentions three additional arguments for similarity (equal in distinguishing features, common subject of division, confusion if equal in accidents) but dismisses them as weak (والكل ضعيف). Note why they are insufficient.
The main argument remains the atomist one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس في أحكام الأجسام فمنها أنها متماثلة لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض، ويجوز على كل ما يجوز على الآخر، ويبتني على ذلك استناد اختلاف العوارض إلى القادر المختار، وخرق السموات وكثير من خوارق العادات، وذلك لكونها من محض الجواهر الفردة المتماثلة، ولاشتراكها في التميز وقبول الأعراض، وهما من أخص صفات النفس وانقسام الجسم إليها. وقد يتوهم أن المراد بتماثلها الاتحاد في المفهوم المشترك بين الأنواع المختلفة، كالحيوان مثلاً، فيستدل بأن حد الجسم على اختلاف عباراتهم فيه واحد غير مشتمل على تنويع واختلاف الخواص إنما هو لاختلاف الأنواع. بعد الفراغ من بيان حقيقة الجسم أخذ في بيان أحكامه، فمنها أن الأجسام متماثلة أي متحدة الحقيقة، وإنما الاختلاف بالعوارض، وهذا أصل يبتني عليه كثير من قواعد الإسلام، كإثبات القادر المختار، وكثير من أحوال النبوة والمعاد، فإن اختصاص كل جسم بصفاته المعينة لا بد أن يكون بمرجح مختار، إذ نسبة المرجب إلى الكل على السواء، ولما جاز على كل جسم ما يجوز على الآخر كالبرد على النار، والحرق على الماء ثبت جواز ما نقل من المعجزات، وأحوال القيامة، ومبنى هذا الأصل عند المتكلمين على أن أجزاء الجسم ليست إلا الجواهر الفردة وانها متماثلة لا يتصور فيها اختلاف حقيقة ولا محيص لمن اعترف بتماثل الجواهر، واختلاف الأجسام بالحقيقة من جعل بعض الأعراض داخلة فيها، وقد يستدل بأن الأجسام متساوية في التميز، وقبول الأعراض، وذلك من صفات النفس، وبأن الجسم ينقسم إلى الفلكي والعنصري بما لهما من الأقسام، ومورد القسمة مشترك، وبأن الأجسام يلتبس بعضها ببعض على تقدير الاستواء في الأعراض، ولولا تماثلها في نفسها لما كان كذلك، والكل ضعيف. ومن أفاضل الحكماء من توهم أن المراد بتماثلها اتحادها في مفهوم الجسم، وإن كانت هي أنواعاً مختلفة مندرجة تحته فتمسك بأن الحد الدال على ماهية الجسم على اختلاف عباراتهم فيه واحد عند كل قوم من غير وقوع قسمة فيه، فلذلك اتفق الكل على تماثله فإن المختلفات إذا جمعت في حد واحد وقع فيه التقسيم ضرورة كما يقال الجسم هو القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة والمشتمل عليها، فيعم الطبيعي والتعليمي، ومنشأ هذا التوهم استبعاد أن يذهب عاقل إلى أن الماء والنار حقيقة واحدة لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض كالإنسان دون الفصول والمنوعات كالحيوان كيف ولم يسمع نزاع في أن الجسم جنس بعيد ثم قال : وقول النظام يتخلفها لتخالف خواصها إنما يوجب تخالف الأنواع، لا تخالف المفهوم من الحد.
The fifth section on the rulings of bodies: among them (the rulings) is that they are similar (mutamathila), not differing except by accidents (a'arid), and whatever is permissible for one is permissible for the other. And upon this is based the attribution of the difference of accidents to the Free Agent (al-Qadir al-Mukhtar), and the splitting of the heavens, and many supernatural occurrences (khawariq al-'adat). This is because they consist solely of similar simple atoms (al-jawahir al-fardah al-mutamathila), and because they share in being distinguished and in receiving accidents, which are among the most specific attributes of the soul and the division of the body into them. And it may be understood that what is meant by their similarity is unity in the common concept among different species, like animal for example, and then it is argued that the definition (hadd) of body, despite the differences in their expressions regarding it, is one, not containing division, and the difference in properties is only due to the difference of species. After completing the exposition of the reality of the body, he began to expound its rulings. Among them is that bodies are similar, i.e., identical in reality, and difference is only by accidents. This is a principle upon which many rules of Islam are based, such as proving the Free Agent, and many matters of prophethood and the resurrection. For the specification of each body with its particular attributes must be by a choosing preponderator (murajjih mukhtar), since the relationship of the necessitating factor (al-mujib) to all is equal. And when it is possible for every body what is possible for another, like cold on fire and burning on water, the possibility of what has been reported of miracles and states of the resurrection is established. The basis of this principle among the mutakallimun is that the parts of the body are nothing but simple atoms (al-jawahir al-fardah) and that they are similar, with no difference in reality conceivable. There is no escape for one who acknowledges the similarity of atoms from making some accidents enter into (the essence of) bodies that differ in reality. And it may be argued that bodies are equal in being distinguished and in receiving accidents, which are attributes of the soul; and that the body is divided into celestial and elemental with their divisions, and the subject of division is common; and that bodies are confused with each other given equality in accidents, and were they not similar in themselves, it would not be so. All of this is weak. And among the eminent philosophers (al-hukama') is one who imagined that what is meant by their similarity is their unity in the concept of body, even if they are different species subsumed under it. So he adhered to the fact that the definition indicating the quiddity (mahiyya) of body, despite the differences in their expressions regarding it, is one among each group without division occurring in it. Therefore, all agreed on its similarity. For different things, when gathered under one definition, division necessarily occurs in it, as it is said: 'Body is that which is receptive of the three dimensions and contains them' – it includes the natural and the mathematical. The origin of this misconception is the unlikelihood that a rational person would go so far as to say that water and fire are one reality, not differing except by accidents, like man without differentiae and differentiate like animal? How, and no dispute is heard that body is a remote genus. Then he said: 'And the statement of al-Nazzam that (bodies) differ due to difference of their properties only necessitates the difference of species, not the difference of the concept from the definition.'
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage introduces the first ruling concerning bodies (al-ajsam) in the fifth section of the book. It begins by stating that all bodies are similar (mutamathila) in their essence and only differ by accidents (a'arid). This is presented as a foundational principle for many Islamic doctrines, such as proving the existence of a free agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar) and the possibility of miracles and eschatological events. The reasoning is that since bodies are composed of identical simple atoms (jawahir fardah), any differentiation in their properties requires a choosing agent, as necessity applies equally to all. The author then addresses a potential misunderstanding: some might think 'similarity' means sharing a common concept (mafhum) like 'animal' across species. The mutakallimun's response is that bodies are alike in being receptive to accidents and in their divisibility, but these arguments are deemed weak. The passage then discusses an objection from some philosophers who interpret similarity as unity in the concept of 'body', which they argue is a single genus (jins) without division, but the author counters that this is a mistake, as different species would require division in the definition. The passage concludes with a reference to al-Nazzam, who claimed that differences in properties only indicate differences in species, not in the underlying concept of body.

Key Terms
متماثلة
عوارض
الجواهر الفردة
القادر المختار
المعجزات
تماثل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the principle that all bodies are essentially similar according to the mutakallimun?
A) Bodies share the same accidents.
B) Bodies are composed of identical simple atoms (al-jawahir al-fardah).
C) Bodies all have the same definition (hadd).
D) Bodies are all subject to the same physical laws.

Key Takeaway
The essential similarity of bodies, grounded in atomist theory, is a foundational principle that supports proving a free creator and the possibility of miracles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if the physical world around you is neutral and could be reshaped by the Free Agent at any moment, or do you rely on natural laws as if they are fixed? What would change in your daily life if you truly believed that the atoms of your body and your environment are identical and only God's choice gives them their properties?
?
When you observe differences in health, wealth, or success among people, do you attribute them to material causes like effort or genetics, or do you see the hand of the Free Agent? How would your gratitude or patience change if you reflected that each attribute is a direct choice of Allah?
?
If you truly internalized that miracles are not violations of nature but simply God assigning different accidents to the same atoms, would you still limit God's power over your life? Is there an area where you have unconsciously boxed in God's ability to intervene because you think 'that's impossible'?

Responding to Misconceptions about the Similarity of Bodies
Refutes philosopher's and al-Nazzam's objections to the similarity of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the common misconception that similarity of bodies means sharing a generic concept, and how the mutakallimun refute the philosopher's interpretation and al-Nazzam's objection.

Core Idea
Similarity is not a shared genus but identical reality of atoms; objections fail to grasp this.

The previous lesson established the principle of body similarity. Here, we address key objections. Some philosophers misinterpret 'similarity' as unity in the concept of body (like a genus), while al-Nazzam argues that differences in properties indicate different species. The author of the text explains these errors and defends the mutakallimun's position.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation, focusing on the philosopher's misunderstanding and al-Nazzam's objection.
2
Identify the philosopher's misinterpretation
The philosopher thinks 'similarity' means sharing the concept of 'body' (مفهوم الجسم) as a common genus, with bodies as different species under it.
3
Understand his argument from definition
He argues that the definition (حد) of body is the same for all bodies and doesn't involve division, so bodies must be similar in concept.
4
See the refutation
The author points out that this leads to absurdity: water and fire would be essentially the same, differing only by accidents like man without differentiate. He also notes that 'body' is a remote genus, not a species-defining concept.
5
Examine al-Nazzam's objection
Al-Nazzam claimed that differing properties (خواص) imply differing species, not differing concepts. The author dismisses this as only showing species difference, not undermining the similarity principle.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الخامس في أحكام الأجسام فمنها أنها متماثلة لا تختلف إلا بالعوارض، ويجوز على كل ما يجوز على الآخر، ويبتني على ذلك استناد اختلاف العوارض إلى القادر المختار، وخرق السموات وكثير من خوارق العادات، وذلك لكونها من محض الجواهر الفردة المتماثلة، ولاشتراكها في التميز وقبول الأعراض، وهما من أخص صفات النفس وانقسام الجسم إليها. وقد يتوهم أن المراد بتماثلها الاتحاد في المفهوم المشترك بين الأنواع المختلفة، كالحيوان مثلاً، فيستدل بأن حد الجسم على اختلاف عباراتهم فيه واحد غير مشتمل على تنويع واختلاف الخواص إنما هو لاختلاف الأنواع. بعد الفراغ من بيان حقيقة الجسم أخذ في بيان أحكامه، فمنها أن الأجسام متماثلة أي متحدة الحقيقة، وإنما الاختلاف بالعوارض، وهذا أصل يبتني عليه كثير من قواعد الإسلام، كإثبات القادر المختار، وكثير من أحوال النبوة والمعاد، فإن اختصاص كل جسم بصفاته المعينة لا بد أن يكون بمرجح مختار، إذ نسبة المرجب إلى الكل على السواء، ولما جاز على كل جسم ما يجوز على الآخر كالبرد على النار، والحرق على الماء ثبت جواز ما نقل من المعجزات، وأحوال القيامة، ومبنى هذا الأصل عند المتكلمين على أن أجزاء الجسم ليست إلا الجواهر الفردة وانها متماثلة لا يتصور فيها اختلاف حقيقة ولا محيص لمن اعترف بتماثل الجواهر، واختلاف الأجسام بالحقيقة من جعل بعض الأعراض داخلة فيها، وقد يستدل بأن ولولا تماثلها في نفسها لما كان كذلك، والكل ضعيف. ومن أفاضل الحكماء من توهم أن المراد بتماثلها اتحادها في مفهوم الجسم، وإن كانت هي أنواعاً مختلفة مندرجة تحته فتمسك بأن الحد الدال على ماهية الجسم على اختلاف عباراتهم فيه واحد عند كل قوم من غير وقوع قسمة فيه، فلذلك اتفق الكل على تماثله فإن المختلفات إذا جمعت في حد واحد وقع فيه التقسيم ضرورة كما يقال الجسم هو القابل للأبعاد الثلاثة والمشتمل عليها، Commentary
This passage introduces the first ruling concerning bodies (al-ajsam) in the fifth section of the book. It begins by stating that all bodies are similar (mutamathila) in their essence and only differ by accidents (a'arid). This is presented as a foundational principle for many Islamic doctrines, such as proving the existence of a free agent (al-qadir al-mukhtar) and the possibility of miracles and eschatological events. The reasoning is that since bodies are composed of identical simple atoms (jawahir fardah), any differentiation in their properties requires a choosing agent, as necessity applies equally to all. The author then addresses a potential misunderstanding: some might think 'similarity' means sharing a common concept (mafhum) like 'animal' across species. The mutakallimun's response is that bodies are alike in being receptive to accidents and in their divisibility, but these arguments are deemed weak. The passage then discusses an objection from some philosophers who interpret similarity as unity in the concept of 'body', which they argue is a single genus (jins) without division, but the author counters that this is a mistake, as different species would require division in the definition. The passage concludes with a reference to al-Nazzam, who claimed that differences in properties only indicate differences in species, not in the underlying concept of body.

مفهوم

النظام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The similarity of bodies means they share a common genus-concept (e.g., 'body') under which different species are differentiated by accidents.
Correction: The mutakallimun hold that similarity means identity of reality at the atomic level; the common concept of body does not capture this. Water and fire are not essentially different species but identical in their atomic constitution.

Checkpoint
What is the philosopher's main argument for interpreting body similarity as unity in the concept of body?
A) Bodies have the same accidents.
B) The definition of body is one and does not involve division into species.
C) All bodies are composed of atoms.
D) Body is a remote genus.

Key Takeaway
The similarity of bodies is not about sharing a concept but about identical atomic reality; objections based on generic unity or property differences are refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you observe vast differences among created things—like heat and cold, light and darkness—do you automatically assume they must be fundamentally different in essence? How might this assumption affect your understanding of Allah's power to create a single substance with diverse accidents?
?
Are you willing to accept that your 'logical' objections to Islamic teachings may stem from misunderstandings deeper than you think, just as the philosopher's objection stemmed from misinterpreting 'similarity'? What is one assumption you hold right now that might be preventing you from fully submitting to a revealed truth?

The Permanence of Bodies: A Necessary Judgment
Taftazani presents the doctrine of bodies' permanence by necessity and clarifies its meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Taftazani claims that bodies are permanent by rational necessity, not merely by sensory appearance, and how this relates to the possibility of annihilation.

Core Idea
Bodies are known to persist by necessity, yet they remain susceptible to annihilation.

In this passage, Taftazani begins by affirming that bodies have a property of permanence, but he immediately qualifies what this means. He argues that we know by necessity that objects remain the same over time, but this does not mean they are indestructible. Rather, this permanence is compatible with their being subject to annihilation, a key point in the theological discussion of creation and resurrection.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening claim
Taftazani states that bodies are permanent by the judgment of necessity, not by mere sensory persistence. Notice the distinction he makes.
2
Understand the meaning of 'necessity'
He clarifies that necessity means we intuitively know that our belongings retain their identity over time, even if accidents change.
This is not a claim of sensory perception but of rational intuition.
3
Consider the objection of renewal of similars
He addresses a potential objection: that what appears to be persistence could be a series of similar entities being replaced. He rejects this for bodies.
This concept is called 'tajaddud al-amthal'.
4
Note the connection to annihilation
He asserts that bodies are susceptible to annihilation because they are originated, and cites the Quranic verse.
This sets up the next lesson's discussion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها) أنها باقية بحكم الضرورة لا بمجرد البقاء في الحس، وقابلة للفناء لكونها حادثة على ما سيأتي، ولقوله تعالى كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه وهلاك البسيط لا يتصور إلا بفنائه، ولا يخفي أن الحدوث إنما يقتضي إمكان العدم بالذات، وهو لا ينافي امتناعه بالغير وهو المتنازع . فإن استروح إلى أن الحكم هو الإمكان حتى يثبت ما به الامتناع كان ذكر الحدوث مستدركا . أنا أي من أحكام الأجسام أنها باقية زمانين وأكثر بحكم الضرورة بمعنى نعلم بالضرورة أن كتبنا وثيابنا وبيوتنا ودوابنا هي بعينها التي كانت من غير تبدل في الذوات، بل إن كان ففي العوارض والهيئات، لا بمعنى أن الحس يشاهدها باقية ليرد الاعتراض بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك بتجدد الأمثال كما في الأعراض، وقد يفهم من البقاء الدوام وامتناع الفناء، وعليه يحمل ما قال في التجريد أن الضرورة قاضية ببقاء الأجسام وبين بأن غاية أمرها التفرق والانقسام، وهو لا يوجب الانعدام. وأنت خبير بأن دعوى الضرورة في ذلك في غاية الفساد. كيف وقد صرح بجوازه في بحث المعاد واستدل على جواز العدم تارة بالحدوث، فإن العدم السابق كالعدم اللاحق لعدم التمايز ، وقد جاز الأول فكذا الثاني، وتارة بالإمكان، فإن معناه جواز كل من الوجود والعدم نظراً إلى الذات. وأجيب بأن هذا لا ينافي الامتناع بالغير على ما هو المتنازع ، فإنه يجوز أن يكون الشيء في ذاته قابلاً للعدم السابق واللاحق جميعاً، ويمتنع أحدهما أو كلاهما لعلة، والحاصل أن الحدوث لا ينافي الأبدية كما في النفس الناطقة على رأي أرسطو، والإمكان لا ينافي الأبدية والأزلية كما في القدماء لزمانية دون الذاتية على رأي الفلاسفة (ويمكن) أن يقال : إنه لما كان الحكم بجواز العدم نفي ذلك الحكم حتى يقوم دليل الامتناع . لا يقال على هذا لا حاجة إلى اعتبار الحدوث لأن الجسم ممكن جائز الوجود والعدم قديماً كان أو حديثاً لأنا نقول هو مراعاة لطرق المتكلمين حيث
He said: 'And among them (the properties of bodies) is that they are permanent by the judgment of necessity, not merely by persistence in sense, and they are susceptible to annihilation because they are originated (haditha) as will come, and due to His saying the Exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (Quran 28:88), and the annihilation of a simple (thing) cannot be conceived except by its passing away. And it is not hidden that origination (huduth) only necessitates the possibility (imkan) of non-existence in essence, and it does not contradict its impossibility (imtina') by another, which is the point of dispute. For if one finds solace in the judgment being possibility until what necessitates impossibility is established, then the mention of origination is redundant.' I say: That is, from the judgments of bodies is that they persist for two or more moments by the judgment of necessity, meaning we know by necessity that our books, clothes, houses, and animals are the very same ones that were, without change in the essences; rather, if (change occurs) it is in the accidents and states. Not in the sense that the sense perceives them persisting, so that the objection could be raised that it is possible that this is by renewal of similars (tajaddud al-amthal) as in accidents. And permanence might be understood as perpetuity (dawam) and impossibility of annihilation, and upon this is carried what was said in al-Tajrid that necessity judges the permanence of bodies, and he explained that the utmost of their affair is dispersion and division, which does not necessitate non-existence. And you are well aware that the claim of necessity in this is extremely corrupt. How so, when he explicitly allowed it in the discussion of resurrection, and he argued for the permissibility of non-existence sometimes by origination, for prior non-existence is like posterior non-existence due to lack of distinctness, and the first was possible, so likewise the second; and sometimes by possibility, for its meaning is the permissibility of both existence and non-existence with respect to the essence. And it was answered that this does not contradict impossibility by another, which is the point of dispute, for it is possible that a thing is in its essence receptive to prior and posterior non-existence both, while one or both are impossible due to a cause. The upshot is that origination does not contradict eternity (abadiyya) as in the rational soul according to Aristotle, and possibility does not contradict eternity and beginninglessness (azaliyya) as in the eternal temporal things (qudama' zamaniyya) excluding the essential according to the philosophers. And it is possible to say: Since the judgment of the permissibility of non-existence negates that judgment until a proof of impossibility is established. It should not be said that on this there is no need to consider origination because body is possible, permissible of existence and non-existence whether it is eternal or originated, for we say it is out of consideration for the methods of the theologians, where...
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage discusses the property of bodies that they are permanent by necessity but also susceptible to annihilation because they are originated. The author begins by quoting an unnamed source (possibly from al-Tajrid) that bodies are permanent by a judgment of necessity, not merely by sensory persistence, and they are capable of annihilation due to their origination and the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face.' He notes that origination only entails possibility of non-existence in essence, not actual non-existence, and it does not contradict impossibility by an external factor, which is the real point of dispute. If one argues that the judgment is mere possibility until impossibility is proven, then mentioning origination is superfluous. Then the author clarifies that the intended meaning of 'permanence by necessity' is that we know necessarily that things like books and clothes remain identical over time, without change in essences; if change occurs, it is only in accidents. This is not to claim that sense perception shows them persisting, because that would be open to the objection of 'renewal of similars' (tajaddud al-amthal) as in the case of accidents. Some understand permanence as perpetuity (dawam) and impossibility of annihilation, and that is what is meant in al-Tajrid when it says necessity judges permanence, and the author of al-Tajrid explains that bodies only undergo dispersion and division, which does not entail annihilation. The author strongly criticizes the claim of necessity in this matter, calling it extremely corrupt. He points out that the same theologian explicitly allowed the possibility of annihilation in the discussion of resurrection, and argued for the permissibility of non-existence based on origination (since prior non-existence was possible, so is posterior) and based on possibility (since the essence admits both existence and non-existence). The response to this is that this does not contradict impossibility by another factor, which is the real point of dispute. A thing may be in its essence receptive to both prior and posterior non-existence, but one or both may be impossible due to a cause. The conclusion is that origination does not contradict perpetuity (as in the rational soul for Aristotle), and possibility does not contradict perpetuity and beginninglessness (as in the temporal eternal things of the philosophers, excluding the essential). Finally, a possible answer is given: the judgment of permissibility of non-existence is negated until a proof of impossibility is established. If it is objected that this removes the need to consider origination, since the body is possible whether eternal or originated, the response is that it is out of consideration for the methods of the theologians. The text ends abruptly.

Key Terms
بقاء (baqa')
ضرورة (darura)
تجدد الأمثال (tajaddud al-amthal)
فناء (fana')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that 'permanence by necessity' means bodies are absolutely indestructible and cannot be annihilated.
Correction: Taftazani clarifies that permanence by necessity is a rational judgment of identity over time, not a claim of indestructibility. He explicitly states that bodies are susceptible to annihilation due to their origination.

Checkpoint
According to Taftazani, in what sense are bodies 'permanent by the judgment of necessity'?
A) We perceive them continuously without change through the senses.
B) We know by rational intuition that they remain identical in essence over time.
C) They are absolutely eternal and cannot cease to exist.
D) They are only permanent in accidents.

Key Takeaway
Bodies are known to persist by rational necessity, but this does not preclude their susceptibility to annihilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You trust that your body and belongings remain the same from moment to moment. But this passage says that permanence is only known by necessity, not by sense, and that they are susceptible to annihilation. How does this truth affect your attachment to the material things you hold dear? Will you still cling to them as if they will last forever, or will you prepare for their passing?
?
When you look at your own body in the mirror, do you assume it is fixed and unchanging? Yet the renewal of similars could be happening every instant. If that were true, would your identity still be the same? What does this teach you about relying on Allah alone for your continued existence?
?
The passage mentions that origination only gives possibility of non-existence, not actual annihilation. But the Quran says everything perishes except Allah. Do you live as if that is true? Is your heart attached to anything in a way that would leave a void if it were taken away? What would it take for you to truly believe that nothing besides Allah is permanent?

Allah's Existence
advanced
12 min
The Possibility of Annihilation: Arguments and Responses
Taftazani criticizes the necessity claim and presents arguments for annihilation with responses.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for the permissibility of non-existence of bodies and the responses that uphold the possibility of eternity.

Core Idea
Contingency allows non-existence, but an external cause can necessitate perpetual existence.

Taftazani now critically examines the claim that bodies are permanent by necessity, pointing out its weakness. He presents arguments based on origination and possibility to show that non-existence is permissible, and then explains how the response—that impossibility by another does not contradict essential possibility—resolves the dispute. This lesson unpacks the dialectic.

Guided Path
1
Read the criticism of necessity
Taftazani calls the claim of necessity 'extremely corrupt' and notes that even its proponent allowed annihilation in the case of resurrection.
This sets up the need for a different approach.
2
Understand the argument from origination
The argument is that prior non-existence was possible before creation, so posterior non-existence after creation should also be possible, as there is no distinction.
This is based on symmetry.
3
Understand the argument from possibility
Possibility (imkan) means the essence equally permits existence and non-existence, so non-existence is always a rational option.
4
Follow the response
The response is that essential possibility does not prevent impossibility by an external cause. A thing may be receptive to both prior and posterior non-existence, yet be kept in existence perpetually by a cause.
This is the key to reconciling contingency with perceived permanence.
5
Note the final methodological point
Taftazani concludes that origination does not contradict eternity, and possibility does not contradict beginninglessness or perpetuity. The judgment of permissibility is negated until proof of impossibility is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (ومنها) أنها باقية بحكم الضرورة لا بمجرد البقاء في الحس، وقابلة للفناء لكونها حادثة على ما سيأتي، ولقوله تعالى كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه وهلاك البسيط لا يتصور إلا بفنائه، ولا يخفي أن الحدوث إنما يقتضي إمكان العدم بالذات، وهو لا ينافي امتناعه بالغير وهو المتنازع . فإن استروح إلى أن الحكم هو الإمكان حتى يثبت ما به الامتناع كان ذكر الحدوث مستدركا . أنا أي من أحكام الأجسام أنها باقية زمانين وأكثر بحكم الضرورة بمعنى نعلم بالضرورة أن كتبنا وثيابنا وبيوتنا ودوابنا هي بعينها التي كانت من غير تبدل في الذوات، بل إن كان ففي العوارض والهيئات، لا بمعنى أن الحس يشاهدها باقية ليرد الاعتراض بأنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك بتجدد الأمثال كما في الأعراض، وقد يفهم من البقاء الدوام وامتناع الفناء، وعليه يحمل ما قال في التجريد أن الضرورة قاضية ببقاء الأجسام وبين بأن غاية أمرها التفرق والانقسام، وهو لا يوجب الانعدام. وأنت خبير بأن دعوى الضرورة في ذلك في غاية الفساد. كيف وقد صرح بجوازه في بحث المعاد واستدل على جواز العدم تارة بالحدوث، فإن العدم السابق كالعدم اللاحق لعدم التمايز ، وقد جاز الأول فكذا الثاني، وتارة بالإمكان، فإن معناه جواز كل من الوجود والعدم نظراً إلى الذات. وأجيب بأن هذا لا ينافي الامتناع بالغير على ما هو المتنازع ، فإنه يجوز أن يكون الشيء في ذاته قابلاً للعدم السابق واللاحق جميعاً، ويمتنع أحدهما أو كلاهما لعلة، والحاصل أن الحدوث لا ينافي الأبدية كما في النفس الناطقة على رأي أرسطو، والإمكان لا ينافي الأبدية والأزلية كما في القدماء لزمانية دون الذاتية على رأي الفلاسفة (ويمكن) أن يقال : إنه لما كان الحكم بجواز العدم نفي ذلك الحكم حتى يقوم دليل الامتناع . لا يقال على هذا لا حاجة إلى اعتبار الحدوث لأن الجسم ممكن جائز الوجود والعدم قديماً كان أو حديثاً لأنا نقول هو مراعاة لطرق المتكلمين حيث
He said: 'And among them (the properties of bodies) is that they are permanent by the judgment of necessity, not merely by persistence in sense, and they are susceptible to annihilation because they are originated (haditha) as will come, and due to His saying the Exalted: 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (Quran 28:88), and the annihilation of a simple (thing) cannot be conceived except by its passing away. And it is not hidden that origination (huduth) only necessitates the possibility (imkan) of non-existence in essence, and it does not contradict its impossibility (imtina') by another, which is the point of dispute. For if one finds solace in the judgment being possibility until what necessitates impossibility is established, then the mention of origination is redundant.' I say: That is, from the judgments of bodies is that they persist for two or more moments by the judgment of necessity, meaning we know by necessity that our books, clothes, houses, and animals are the very same ones that were, without change in the essences; rather, if (change occurs) it is in the accidents and states. Not in the sense that the sense perceives them persisting, so that the objection could be raised that it is possible that this is by renewal of similars (tajaddud al-amthal) as in accidents. And permanence might be understood as perpetuity (dawam) and impossibility of annihilation, and upon this is carried what was said in al-Tajrid that necessity judges the permanence of bodies, and he explained that the utmost of their affair is dispersion and division, which does not necessitate non-existence. And you are well aware that the claim of necessity in this is extremely corrupt. How so, when he explicitly allowed it in the discussion of resurrection, and he argued for the permissibility of non-existence sometimes by origination, for prior non-existence is like posterior non-existence due to lack of distinctness, and the first was possible, so likewise the second; and sometimes by possibility, for its meaning is the permissibility of both existence and non-existence with respect to the essence. And it was answered that this does not contradict impossibility by another, which is the point of dispute, for it is possible that a thing is in its essence receptive to prior and posterior non-existence both, while one or both are impossible due to a cause. The upshot is that origination does not contradict eternity (abadiyya) as in the rational soul according to Aristotle, and possibility does not contradict eternity and beginninglessness (azaliyya) as in the eternal temporal things (qudama' zamaniyya) excluding the essential according to the philosophers. And it is possible to say: Since the judgment of the permissibility of non-existence negates that judgment until a proof of impossibility is established. It should not be said that on this there is no need to consider origination because body is possible, permissible of existence and non-existence whether it is eternal or originated, for we say it is out of consideration for the methods of the theologians, where...

Division of Existence and the Possibility of Annihilation
Sanusi establishes the division of existence and provides Quranic evidence for annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between eternal and originated existence and the scriptural basis for the possibility of annihilation of bodies.

Core Idea
Annihilation occurs in originated things, not merely possible ones.

This lesson lays the foundation for the debate on annihilation. The author begins by clarifying the division of existence and establishing that non-existence applies to created things. Using Quranic verses, he shows that annihilation is not only possible but promised.

Guided Path
1
Understanding the division
The passage distinguishes between eternal and originated existence, not the usual necessary/possible. Focus on why this distinction matters for annihilation.
The author uses 'qadim' and 'hadith' to set the stage.
2
Scriptural evidence
The author cites Quranic verses (e.g., 'Everything is perishable except His Face') to demonstrate that annihilation applies to all created things.
Reflect on how the verses are used as proof for the possibility of annihilation.
3
Annihilation in composites vs simples
Note that in composite bodies, destruction may mean decomposition, but in simple substances (atoms, matter/form), it must be absolute non-existence.
This sets up the later discussion on bodies.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقسمون الوجود إلى القديم والحادث لا الواجب والممكن وبيان لجواز حصول العدم بالفعل وذلك في الحدوث دون مجرد الإمكان.
وقد يستدل بنحو قوله تعالى كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه كل من عليها فان.
وغير ذلك من العمومات مع القطع بأن الهلاك والفناء في المركبات، وإن جاز أن يكون بانحلال التركيب وزوال الصور لكن في البسائط وأجزاء الجسم من الجواهر الفردة أو الهيولي والصورة لا يتصور إلا بالانعدام
قال (وحين اقتضت) شبهة امتناع بقاء الأعراض المنافاة بين البقاء وصحة الفناء. واعتبر حيث مثلها في الأجسام اعتبر النظام دليل قبول الفناء، فالتزم عدم البقاء، والكرامية ضرورة البقاء، فالتزموا امتناع الفناء وقد عرفت الجواب مع إمكان الفرق بأن الأعراض مشروطة بالجواهر المشروطة بها، فتدور بخلاف الجواهر، فإنه يجوز أن يبقيها الله تعالى بأعراض متعاقبة يحتاج إليها الجواهر ويفنيها بلا واسطة أو بعدم خلق تلك الأعراض أو العرض الذي هو الفناء واحداً أو متعدداً على اختلاف المذاهب، وتمسكت الفلاسفة في امتناع فنائها بأصولهم الفاسدة من أنها مستندة إلى القديم إيجاباً، ومفتقرة إلى مادة لا تقبل العدم، لاستحالة تسلسل المواد أو لا تجرد من الصورة لما مر.
يعني أن ما ذكر في عدم بقاء الأعراض من أنها لو بقيت لامتنع فناؤها لما كان جارياً في الأجسام أيضاً، على ما سبق. اعتبر النظام قيام الدليل على صحة فنائها، فالتزم أنها لا تبقى زمانين وإنما تتجدد بتجدد الأمثال كالأعراض قولاً بانتفاء الملزوم لانتفاء اللازم والكرامية قالوا قضاء الضرورة ببقائها، فالتزموا امتناع
فنائها قولاً بثبوت اللازم لثبوت الملزوم، وقد سبق في بحث امتناع بقاء العرض بطلان دليل هذه الملازمة، فاندفع ما ذكره الفريقان مع إمكان البعد عن النقض بأنه يجوز أن تفني الأجسام بعد بقائها، بأن لا يخلق الله تعالى فيها الأعراض التي يكون بقاء الجسم محتاجاً إليها مشروطاً بها كالأكوان وغيرها على ما ذهب إليه القاضي وإمام الحرمين أو بأن لا يخلق فيها العرض الذي هو البقاء كما قال الكعبي، أو بأن يخلق فيها عرضاً هو الفناء إما متعدداً كما قال أبو علي: أنه تعالى يخلق لكل جوهر فناء، وإما غير متعدد كما قال غيره. إن فناء واحداً يكفي لإفناء كل الأجسام، وزعم بعضهم أن قول النظام بعدم بقاء الأجسام مبني على أن الجسم عنده مجموع أعراض والعرض غير باق، وقد نبهناك على أن ليس مذهبه أن الجسم عرض، بل إن مثل اللون والطعم والرائحة من الأعراض أجسام قائمة بأنفسها، وأما الفلاسفة فلا نزاع لهم في فناء الأجسام بزوال الصور النوعية، والهيئات التركيبية، وإنما النزاع في فنائها بالكلية، أعني الهيولي والصورة الجسمية، ومبني ذلك عندهم على اعتقاد أزليته المستلزمة لأبديته، فإن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه وسيرد عليك شبههم بأجوبتها.
They divide existence into eternal and originated, not into necessary and possible, and it is a clarification of the possibility of actual non-existence, and that is in originatedness (ḥudūth) not mere possibility (imkān). And one may argue with such as His saying, the Most High: 'Everything is perishable except His Face,' 'Everyone upon it is passing away.' And other general verses, with the certainty that annihilation and perishing occur in composite things, even if it may be by dissolution of composition and removal of forms, but in simple things and parts of the body—whether atoms (jawāhir fard) or matter and form—it is not conceivable except by non-existence. He said: '(When there arose) the argument of the impossibility of survival of accidents, contradicting survival and the validity of annihilation.' And he considered where similar examples are applied to bodies; al-Nazzām considered it a proof of the genuineness of annihilation, so he denied survival, and the Karāmiyya considered survival necessary, so they affirmed the impossibility of annihilation. You have already known the answer, along with the possibility of a distinction: accidents are conditioned by substances (jawāhir), which themselves condition them, so they are interdependent, unlike substances, for it is possible for God Almighty to preserve them with successive accidents that substances need, and to annihilate them without mediation, or by not creating those accidents, or the accident which is annihilation—whether one or multiple—according to differing schools. The philosophers relied for the impossibility of their annihilation on their corrupt principles: that they are traced back to the Eternal necessarily, and are dependent on matter that does not admit non-existence, due to the impossibility of an infinite regress of matters or the non-existence of privation from form, as previously mentioned. He means: what was mentioned concerning the non-survival of accidents—that if they survived, their annihilation would be impossible—would also apply to bodies, as before. Al-Nazzām considered the existence of proof for the validity of their annihilation, so he held that they do not last two moments, but are renewed by renewal of similars, like accidents, saying: the non-existence of the necessary (al-malzūm) implies the non-existence of the consequent (al-lāzim). And the Karāmiyya said: necessity [of perception] decrees their survival, so they affirmed the impossibility of their annihilation, saying: the establishment of the consequent implies the establishment of the necessary. And it has already preceded in the discussion on the impossibility of survival of an accident the invalidity of the proof of this correlation, so what both groups mentioned is repelled, along with the possibility of avoiding the refutation: it is permissible for Him to annihilate bodies after their survival by His not creating in them the accidents that the body's survival needs and is conditioned by—such as locations (akwān) and others—as al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn held; or by His not creating in them the accident which is survival, as al-Kaʿbī said; or by His creating in them an accident which is annihilation—either multiple, as Abū ʿAlī said: that He creates for each atom an annihilation, or not multiple, as others said: that one annihilation suffices for the annihilation of all bodies. And some claimed that al-Nazzām's denial of the survival of bodies is based on [the view] that body in his view is a collection of accidents, and an accident does not survive, but we have already alerted you that his doctrine is not that body is an accident, but that such things as color, taste, and smell are accidents that are self-subsisting bodies. As for the philosophers, they have no dispute concerning the annihilation of bodies by the removal of specific forms and compositional structures; the dispute is only concerning their complete annihilation, i.e., matter (hayūlā) and physical form (ṣūra jismiyya). That is based on their belief in its eternity, which entails everlastingness; for whatever is established as eternal is impossible to become non-existent. Their arguments, together with their answers, will come to you.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage refutes arguments against the possibility of annihilation of bodies, addressing positions of al-Nazzām, the Karāmiyya, and the Philosophers. It begins by distinguishing the division of existence into eternal and originated, clarifying that non-existence occurs in originated things, not merely possible ones. Then it mentions scriptural evidence for annihilation (Qur'anic verses like 'Everything is perishable except His Face' and 'Everyone upon it is passing away'). The author explains that destruction in composite things can be understood, but in simple things (atoms, matter/form) annihilation must be true non-existence. The core argument: the impossibility of survival of accidents does not apply to bodies because accidents are conditioned by substances, whereas substances can be preserved by God through successive accidents or annihilated directly. Al-Nazzām, applying the same reasoning as for accidents, denied survival of bodies (they renew moment by moment), while the Karāmiyya insisted on survival based on necessary perception. The author points out that the previous refutation of the argument for impossibility of accident survival also applies to both groups. Then he offers a way to avoid the refutation: God can annihilate bodies after keeping them by not creating necessary accidents (al-Qāḍī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn), by not creating the accident of survival (al-Kaʿbī), or by creating a separate accident of annihilation (Abū ʿAlī and others). The author corrects a misunderstanding about al-Nazzām's view: he does not hold that body is an accident, but that some accidents are self-subsisting bodies. Finally, the philosophers' denial of complete annihilation (matter and physical form) is based on their belief in eternity, which will be refuted later.

Allah's Existence
intermediate
6 min
Refuting al-Nazzām and the Karāmiyya on Body Annihilation
Sanusi refutes al-Nazzām and Karāmiyya on body annihilation and clarifies God's power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the arguments for and against the survival of bodies after creation and how the author refutes both extreme positions.

Core Idea
Accidents and substances differ: bodies can be preserved by God's power, not by inherent necessity.

The passage engages with two deviant views: al-Nazzām's denial of bodily survival (holding they renew each moment) and the Karāmiyya's insistence on necessary survival. The author refutes both by distinguishing between the nature of accidents and substances, and by showing that the same reasoning does not apply to bodies.

Guided Path
1
Identifying the two positions
Al-Nazzām says bodies do not survive two moments; Karāmiyya say they must survive. Note their reasoning from analogy and necessity.
2
The author's refutation
The author points to a previous argument: the impossibility of accident survival does not apply to bodies because accidents are conditioned by substances.
Substances can be preserved by God with successive accidents.
3
Possible Divine actions
God can annihilate bodies by not creating necessary accidents, by not creating survival, or by creating an accident of annihilation. This shows God's absolute power.
Three specific mechanisms are mentioned from different scholars.
4
Clarification on al-Nazzām's view
The author corrects a misconception: al-Nazzām did not consider body itself an accident; rather, some accidents are self-subsisting bodies.
This clarifies an important point about al-Nazzām's ontology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقسمون الوجود إلى القديم والحادث لا الواجب والممكن وبيان لجواز حصول العدم بالفعل وذلك في الحدوث دون مجرد الإمكان.
وقد يستدل بنحو قوله تعالى كل شيء هالك إلا وجهه كل من عليها فان.
وغير ذلك من العمومات مع القطع بأن الهلاك والفناء في المركبات، وإن جاز أن يكون بانحلال التركيب وزوال الصور لكن في البسائط وأجزاء الجسم من الجواهر الفردة أو الهيولي والصورة لا يتصور إلا بالانعدام
قال (وحين اقتضت) شبهة امتناع بقاء الأعراض المنافاة بين البقاء وصحة الفناء. واعتبر حيث مثلها في الأجسام اعتبر النظام دليل قبول الفناء، فالتزم عدم البقاء، والكرامية ضرورة البقاء، فالتزموا امتناع الفناء وقد عرفت الجواب مع إمكان الفرق بأن الأعراض مشروطة بالجواهر المشروطة بها، فتدور بخلاف الجواهر، فإنه يجوز أن يبقيها الله تعالى بأعراض متعاقبة يحتاج إليها الجواهر ويفنيها بلا واسطة أو بعدم خلق تلك الأعراض أو العرض الذي هو الفناء واحداً أو متعدداً على اختلاف المذاهب، وتمسكت الفلاسفة في امتناع فنائها بأصولهم الفاسدة من أنها مستندة إلى القديم إيجاباً، ومفتقرة إلى مادة لا تقبل العدم، لاستحالة تسلسل المواد أو لا تجرد من الصورة لما مر.
يعني أن ما ذكر في عدم بقاء الأعراض من أنها لو بقيت لامتنع فناؤها لما كان جارياً في الأجسام أيضاً، على ما سبق. اعتبر النظام قيام الدليل على صحة فنائها، فالتزم أنها لا تبقى زمانين وإنما تتجدد بتجدد الأمثال كالأعراض قولاً بانتفاء الملزوم لانتفاء اللازم والكرامية قالوا قضاء الضرورة ببقائها، فالتزموا امتناع
فنائها قولاً بثبوت اللازم لثبوت الملزوم، وقد سبق في بحث امتناع بقاء العرض بطلان دليل هذه الملازمة، فاندفع ما ذكره الفريقان مع إمكان البعد عن النقض بأنه يجوز أن تفني الأجسام بعد بقائها، بأن لا يخلق الله تعالى فيها الأعراض التي يكون بقاء الجسم محتاجاً إليها مشروطاً بها كالأكوان وغيرها على ما ذهب إليه القاضي وإمام الحرمين أو بأن لا يخلق فيها العرض الذي هو البقاء كما قال الكعبي، أو بأن يخلق فيها عرضاً هو الفناء إما متعدداً كما قال أبو علي: أنه تعالى يخلق لكل جوهر فناء، وإما غير متعدد كما قال غيره. إن فناء واحداً يكفي لإفناء كل الأجسام، وزعم بعضهم أن قول النظام بعدم بقاء الأجسام مبني على أن الجسم عنده مجموع أعراض والعرض غير باق، وقد نبهناك على أن ليس مذهبه أن الجسم عرض، بل إن مثل اللون والطعم والرائحة من الأعراض أجسام قائمة بأنفسها، وأما الفلاسفة فلا نزاع لهم في فناء الأجسام بزوال الصور النوعية، والهيئات التركيبية، وإنما النزاع في فنائها بالكلية، أعني الهيولي والصورة الجسمية، ومبني ذلك عندهم على اعتقاد أزليته المستلزمة لأبديته، فإن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه وسيرد عليك شبههم بأجوبتها.
They divide existence into eternal and originated, not into necessary and possible, and it is a clarification of the possibility of actual non-existence, and that is in originatedness (ḥudūth) not mere possibility (imkān). And one may argue with such as His saying, the Most High: 'Everything is perishable except His Face,' 'Everyone upon it is passing away.' And other general verses, with the certainty that annihilation and perishing occur in composite things, even if it may be by dissolution of composition and removal of forms, but in simple things and parts of the body—whether atoms (jawāhir fard) or matter and form—it is not conceivable except by non-existence. He said: '(When there arose) the argument of the impossibility of survival of accidents, contradicting survival and the validity of annihilation.' And he considered where similar examples are applied to bodies; al-Nazzām considered it a proof of the genuineness of annihilation, so he denied survival, and the Karāmiyya considered survival necessary, so they affirmed the impossibility of annihilation. You have already known the answer, along with the possibility of a distinction: accidents are conditioned by substances (jawāhir), which themselves condition them, so they are interdependent, unlike substances, for it is possible for God Almighty to preserve them with successive accidents that substances need, and to annihilate them without mediation, or by not creating those accidents, or the accident which is annihilation—whether one or multiple—according to differing schools. The philosophers relied for the impossibility of their annihilation on their corrupt principles: that they are traced back to the Eternal necessarily, and are dependent on matter that does not admit non-existence, due to the impossibility of an infinite regress of matters or the non-existence of privation from form, as previously mentioned. He means: what was mentioned concerning the non-survival of accidents—that if they survived, their annihilation would be impossible—would also apply to bodies, as before. Al-Nazzām considered the existence of proof for the validity of their annihilation, so he held that they do not last two moments, but are renewed by renewal of similars, like accidents, saying: the non-existence of the necessary (al-malzūm) implies the non-existence of the consequent (al-lāzim). And the Karāmiyya said: necessity [of perception] decrees their survival, so they affirmed the impossibility of their annihilation, saying: the establishment of the consequent implies the establishment of the necessary. And it has already preceded in the discussion on the impossibility of survival of an accident the invalidity of the proof of this correlation, so what both groups mentioned is repelled, along with the possibility of avoiding the refutation: it is permissible for Him to annihilate bodies after their survival by His not creating in them the accidents that the body's survival needs and is conditioned by—such as locations (akwān) and others—as al-Qāḍī and Imām al-Ḥaramayn held; or by His not creating in them the accident which is survival, as al-Kaʿbī said; or by His creating in them an accident which is annihilation—either multiple, as Abū ʿAlī said: that He creates for each atom an annihilation, or not multiple, as others said: that one annihilation suffices for the annihilation of all bodies. And some claimed that al-Nazzām's denial of the survival of bodies is based on [the view] that body in his view is a collection of accidents, and an accident does not survive, but we have already alerted you that his doctrine is not that body is an accident, but that such things as color, taste, and smell are accidents that are self-subsisting bodies. As for the philosophers, they have no dispute concerning the annihilation of bodies by the removal of specific forms and compositional structures; the dispute is only concerning their complete annihilation, i.e., matter (hayūlā) and physical form (ṣūra jismiyya). That is based on their belief in its eternity, which entails everlastingness; for whatever is established as eternal is impossible to become non-existent. Their arguments, together with their answers, will come to you.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage refutes arguments against the possibility of annihilation of bodies, addressing positions of al-Nazzām, the Karāmiyya, and the Philosophers. It begins by distinguishing the division of existence into eternal and originated, clarifying that non-existence occurs in originated things, not merely possible ones. Then it mentions scriptural evidence for annihilation (Qur'anic verses like 'Everything is perishable except His Face' and 'Everyone upon it is passing away'). The author explains that destruction in composite things can be understood, but in simple things (atoms, matter/form) annihilation must be true non-existence. The core argument: the impossibility of survival of accidents does not apply to bodies because accidents are conditioned by substances, whereas substances can be preserved by God through successive accidents or annihilated directly. Al-Nazzām, applying the same reasoning as for accidents, denied survival of bodies (they renew moment by moment), while the Karāmiyya insisted on survival based on necessary perception. The author points out that the previous refutation of the argument for impossibility of accident survival also applies to both groups. Then he offers a way to avoid the refutation: God can annihilate bodies after keeping them by not creating necessary accidents (al-Qāḍī, Imām al-Ḥaramayn), by not creating the accident of survival (al-Kaʿbī), or by creating a separate accident of annihilation (Abū ʿAlī and others). The author corrects a misunderstanding about al-Nazzām's view: he does not hold that body is an accident, but that some accidents are self-subsisting bodies. Finally, the philosophers' denial of complete annihilation (matter and physical form) is based on their belief in eternity, which will be refuted later.

Key Terms
النظام
الكرامية
بقاء
فناء
أعراض
جواهر
ملازمة
لازم
ملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Nazzām held that bodies are collections of accidents and therefore do not survive.
Correction: The author clarifies that al-Nazzām held that some accidents (like color) are bodies standing by themselves, not that body is an accident.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how did al-Nazzām view the survival of bodies?
A) Bodies survive permanently.
B) Bodies do not last two moments and are renewed like accidents.
C) Bodies are annihilated only by dissolution of composition.
D) Bodies are eternal and cannot be annihilated.

Key Takeaway
Bodies can be annihilated by God's will through various means, and the refutation of opponents hinges on the ontological distinction between accidents and substances.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your very existence at this moment depends on God willing it, what would change if you truly internalized that? How would you speak, act, and prioritize differently starting today?
?
The philosophers assumed matter must be eternal and thus cannot be destroyed. Have you ever assumed something about yourself or your world is 'just the way it is' without considering that God could change or end it in an instant? What might that be?
?
Al-Nazzām and the Karāmiyya both made intellectual errors because they overextended a valid principle. In what area of your faith are you at risk of taking a sound teaching to an extreme, ignoring the balancing truths of revelation?

The Impossibility of a Body Without Accidents Does Not Compel God
Presents and refutes the objection that requiring accidents compels God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the impossibility of a body being free from accidents does not undermine God's free choice or power.

Core Idea
Inability to do the impossible is not impotence.

The objection claims that if a body cannot exist without accidents, then God is forced to create them, contradicting His freedom. The answer is a classic kalam principle: impossibility is not a defect in power. Let's examine how this argument works.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the objection from those who argue for the possibility of a body being free from opposites. Note how they claim it would compel God.
The objection is the first sentence of the passage.
2
Understand the response
Read the author's answer, focusing on the analogy of the necessary condition and its necessary consequence.
The key phrase: 'inability with respect to the impossible does not necessitate incapacity.'
3
Reflect on the principle
Consider why the impossibility of a body without accidents does not conflict with divine omnipotence.
This is a standard argument in kalam against objections that misuse 'compulsion'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتج القائلون بجواز خلو الجسم عن الضدين في الجملة الأول: أنه لو لم يجز لكان الباري تعالى مضطراً عند خلق الجسم إلى خلق العرض، وهو ينافي الاختيار. والجواب: أن عدم القدرة على المحال كوجود الملزوم بدون اللازم لا يوجب العجز وسلب الاختيار.
And those who affirm the permissibility of a body being free from the two opposites argued, first: that if it were not permissible, then God most high would be compelled when creating a body to create an accident, which contradicts choice. The answer: that inability with respect to the impossible, like the existence of the necessary condition without the necessary consequence, does not necessitate incapacity or negation of choice.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from those who claim that a body can be free from opposites (i.e., accidents). Their first proof is that if a body necessarily required an accident, then God would be compelled to create that accident whenever He creates a body, which would contradict His free choice (ikhtiyār). The author provides a succinct answer: inability to do the impossible does not indicate impotence or lack of choice. Just as it is not a failure of power that God cannot create a necessary condition without its consequence, similarly the impossibility of a body without an accident is not a defect in divine power.

Key Terms
جواز خلو الجسم عن الضدين
الباري تعالى
مضطر
الاختيار
المحال
الملزوم
اللازم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If something is impossible for God, then God is not all-powerful.
Correction: Inability to do the logically impossible does not diminish omnipotence; it is not a true object of power.

Checkpoint
What is the response to the objection that requiring accidents would compel God to create them?
A) God is indeed compelled; it is a necessity of His nature.
B) The objection is invalid because God can refrain from creating bodies altogether.
C) Inability to create the impossible is not a failure of power or choice.
D) The objection only applies to some accidents, not all.

Key Takeaway
Inability to bring about an impossible scenario is not a limitation of divine power or choice.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt that God 'should' have done something different in your life, implying that His choices were limited? How does this lesson challenge that feeling? Will you now submit to the fact that God's power and choice are perfect, even when you don't understand His actions?
?
This passage teaches that inability to do the impossible is not a flaw. Reflect on an area where you expect God to act in a way that is logically contradictory. Can you release that expectation and instead trust that God's power is complete? What specific step can you take today to surrender that assumption?

Second Proof: First Body Must Be Free
Second proof: reductio on impossibility of first body.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the kalām argument that if bodies always require accidents (conjunction/separation), then God could not have created the first body, and why that conclusion is impossible.

Core Idea
If bodies always need accidents, no first body could exist.

This lesson presents the second proof for the permissibility of a body being free from accidents. The argument is a reductio ad absurdum: if bodies cannot exist without being paired with opposite accidents (conjunction and separation), then God could not have created the first body in time. Since that consequence is clearly false, the original premise must be rejected.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic sentence once, then the literal translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'. The argument is compact.
2
Identify the premise and consequence
The premise is 'if it were not permissible for a body to be free from conjunction and separation'. The consequence is 'then it would not be permissible for God to create a first body in time'.
3
Recognize the absurdity
The consequence is labeled 'absolutely false' (qaṭʿī al-buṭlān). This is the key: we know from revelation and reason that God did create the first body. So the consequence is false.
4
Draw the conclusion
Since a false consequence follows from the premise, the premise itself must be false. Therefore, it is permissible for a body to be free from conjunction and separation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثاني: أنه لو لم يجز خلو الجسم عن الاجتماع والافتراق لما جاز أن يخلق الله تعالى جسماً هو أول الأجسام بحسب الزمان واللازم قطعى البطلان.
Second: that if a body's being free from conjunction and separation were not permissible, then it would not be permissible for God most high to create a body that is the first of bodies according to time – and the necessary consequence is absolutely false.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is the second proof from those who affirm the permissibility of a body being free from opposites. The argument is that if a body cannot exist without the accidents of conjunction (ijtimāʿ) and separation (iftirāq), then God could not create a first body, because before any body existed, there was no conjunction or separation to attach to it. But it is agreed that God created the first body, so the premise must be false. The author states that the conclusion (lāzim) is clearly false (qaṭʿī al-buṭlān), implying that the premise is therefore unacceptable. Note: There is no explicit answer given; the proof is presented as a self-contained reductio ad absurdum.

Key Terms
خلو الجسم عن الاجتماع والافتراق
أول الأجسام
بحسب الزمان
اللازم قطعى البطلان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That this proof assumes the world had a beginning in time and that the argument only works if one already accepts temporal finitude.
Correction: The proof does not assume temporal finitude; it takes the fact of a first body as given by revelation, and uses that to show that the alternative (bodies always requiring accidents) leads to a contradiction. The argument is valid regardless of one's view on the world's eternity, as long as one accepts that a first body exists.

Checkpoint
What does the second proof argue would be impossible if bodies cannot be free from conjunction and separation?
A) God could not create a body with a specific color.
B) God could not create a body without motion.
C) God could not create the first body in time.
D) Bodies could not undergo change.

Key Takeaway
The second proof shows that if bodies required accidents, no first body could be created; since a first body exists, bodies must be able to exist free from opposites.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If bodies can exist without any accidents, what does that say about your own reliance on accidental qualities (like status, wealth, health) for your identity? Are you willing to let go of those and trust that your essence is secure in God's hands?
?
The proof shows that God's creative power is not bound by our usual expectations of how things must be. In what area of your life are you assuming that God cannot act because of ‘necessary’ conditions? Will you pray for God to break that assumption today?
?
The first body was created free from any prior cause. How does that challenge your deep-seated belief that everything must have a prior physical cause? Are you ready to submit to a Creator who acts without being constrained by your logic?

The Ladder Proof: Finitude of Spatial Dimensions
Taftazani presents the ladder proof for finitude of dimensions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the logical structure of the 'ladder proof' (al-burhan al-sullami) for the finitude of spatial dimensions, including the geometric reasoning and the response to a key objection.

Core Idea
Infinite dimensions lead to a contradiction: an infinite distance bounded between two barriers.

After establishing the first proof for the finitude of dimensions, the author presents a second, geometric argument known as the 'ladder proof'. This argument uses the properties of two lines emanating from a point to show that spatial extension cannot be infinite, because that would entail an impossible scenario. The passage also addresses a potential objection and clarifies the logical force of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the key terms: 'خطان' (two lines), 'مثلث متساوي الأضلاع' (equilateral triangle), 'برهان سلمي' (ladder proof).
The passage is dense; take your time to follow the geometric reasoning.
2
Identify the hypothesis and the contradiction
The argument hypothesizes two lines from a point with increasing distance. If extended infinitely, the distance would also be infinite, yet it remains bounded by the two lines—a contradiction.
The 'barriers' are the lines themselves.
3
Examine the clearer formulation
The author restates the argument using an equilateral triangle (60° angle) to make the relationship between the sides and the distance more straightforward.
In an equilateral triangle, the distance between the two lines equals the length of the lines themselves at every stage.
4
Understand the objection and response
The objection claims the argument assumes an endpoint for extension (a 'last distance'). The response shows that the contradiction itself entails the necessity of an endpoint, so it is not a petition (circular reasoning).
This strengthens the proof by showing it does not beg the question.
5
Reflect on the summary
The summary ties the argument to the impossibility of an infinite bounded distance, concluding that dimensions must be finite.
The phrase 'إمكان عدم القدماء' may be a textual corruption; focus on the core logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثاني) إنا نفرض من نقطة خطين يزداد البعد بينهما على نسبة زيادة امتدادهما بحيث توجد كل زيادة مع المزيد عليه في بعد، فلو امتدا إلى غير النهاية يلزم لضرورة المحافظة على النسبة لوجود بعد مشتمل على الزيادات الغير المتناهية زائد على البعد الأول بقدرها مع انحصاره بين الحاجزين والأوضح أن يفرض كون البعد دائماً بقدر الخطين بأن تجعل الزاوية ثلثي قائمة والمثلث متساوي الأضلاع فيلزم بالضرورة من عدم تناهيهما وجود بعد بينهما غير متناه . فإن قيل : هذا يتوقف على أن يكون للامتداد طرف فيتحقق بعد هو آخر الأبعاد وهو مصادرة . قلنا : لا بل يستلزمه وهو خلف. هذا هو البرهان (٣) السلمي وحاصله أنه لو كانت الأبعاد غير متناهية لزم إمكان عدم القدماء، تناهي المحصور بين حاصرين وهو محال.
He said (the second): We hypothesize from a point two lines such that the distance between them increases in proportion to the increase of their extension, such that every increase exists with that to which it is added in a distance. So if they extended to infinity, it would necessarily follow, for the necessity of preserving the proportion, the existence of a distance containing infinite increases beyond the first distance by their amount, while it is bounded between two barriers. And it is clearer to hypothesize that the distance is always equal to the two lines by making the angle two-thirds of a right angle and the triangle equilateral, so it necessarily follows from their non-finiteness the existence of a distance between them that is infinite. If it is said: This depends on the extension having an endpoint so that a distance is realized which is the last of the distances, and that is a petition. We say: No, rather it entails it, and it is a contradiction. This is the ladder proof (al-burhan al-sullami), and its summary is that if dimensions were infinite, it would entail the possibility of the non-existence of the ancients, the finitude of what is bounded between two barriers, which is impossible.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage is a proof (burhan) presented by the author, likely Taftazani, for the finitude of spatial dimensions, known as the ladder proof (al-burhan al-sullami). The argument begins by hypothesizing two lines emanating from a single point, such that the distance between them increases proportionally to their extension. If the lines extended infinitely, the distance would also increase infinitely, but since the distance is bounded by the two lines themselves (the barriers), this would result in an infinite distance bounded between two barriers—a contradiction. The author offers a clearer formulation by making the angle two-thirds of a right angle (i.e., 60°) and the triangle equilateral, so that the distance equals the lines at every stage. The proof then addresses an objection: the argument assumes an endpoint for the extension. The reply is that the contradiction itself shows that the extension must have an endpoint (the impossibility of an infinite bounded distance). The summary states that if dimensions were infinite, then something finite bounded between two barriers would also have to be infinite—an impossibility, hence dimensions are finite.

Key Terms
برهان سلمي
أبعاد
تناهي
خطان
مثلث متساوي الأضلاع
مصادرة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes what it wants to prove (that dimensions are finite) by requiring an endpoint.
Correction: The argument does not assume an endpoint; rather, the contradiction (an infinite bounded distance) logically entails that an endpoint must exist, because an infinite distance cannot be bounded. So the conclusion follows necessarily, not by assumption.

Checkpoint
What geometric figure does the proof use to clarify the argument?
A) A right triangle
B) An equilateral triangle
C) A square
D) A circle

Key Takeaway
The ladder proof demonstrates that infinite dimensions are impossible because they would entail an infinite distance bounded between two barriers, which is logically contradictory.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If dimensions are finite, then the universe had a beginning. How does this confront your daily assumption that time will always continue for you? What would change if you truly lived as if your time is finite?
?
The proof shows that infinite regress is impossible. In what areas of your life do you rely on 'more time' or 'unlimited chances'? What is one change you can commit to today because time is finite?
?
Taftazani argues that a contradiction forces the conclusion. Are there beliefs you hold that, when examined, lead to contradictions? Will you allow logic to correct your worldview, even if it's uncomfortable?

Ibn Sina's Objection and Its Refinement
Taftazani presents Ibn Sina's objection and a refined response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Ibn Sina's objection to the triangle proof for God's existence and how it was refined to address the objection.

Core Idea
Infinite increase is not the same as an actually infinite distance.

This passage presents a critical exchange in kalam cosmology. Ibn Sina raises a subtle objection against the typical proof: showing that distances increase without limit does not prove an actual infinite, which is the real impossibility. In response, a refined argument is given using equal increments to derive an actual infinite distance. Understanding this dialectic sharpens one's grasp of arguments for divine existence.

Guided Path
1
Read Ibn Sina's objection
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Ibn Sina's point: infinite increase (no maximum) is not impossible; only an actually infinite distance is impossible.
Notice that Ibn Sina distinguishes between potential and actual infinity.
2
Understand the refined formulation
The refined argument connects opposite points, assumes equal increments, and deduces that infinite equal increases lead to an actually infinite distance.
Equal increments are assumed to avoid issues with decreasing increments that might not be divisible.
3
Reflect on the author's note
The passage ends with a note that the formulation is lengthy and the entailment is debatable.
This shows that even refined arguments are subject to scrutiny.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعترض عليه ابن سينا بأن اللازم منه ازدياد البعد إلى غير النهاية، بمعنى أنه لا ينتهي إلى بعد لا يكون فوقه بعد أزيد منه ، وهو ليس بمحال ، وإنما المحال وجود بعد بينهما يمتد طوله إلى غير النهاية ، وهو ليس بلازم، فقرره بأنا نصل بين نقطتين متقابلتين من الخطين المفروضين خطاً ونقسم بالبعد الأصل، وامتداد الخطين حينئذ بالامتداد الأصل، فلكون تزايد الأبعاد بحسب تزايد الامتداد لزم من عدم تناهي الامتداد وجود زيادات على البعد الأصل غير متناهية، لأن نسبة زيادة البعد على البعد الأصل نسبة زيادة الامتداد على الامتداد (٢) الأصل، وإذ قد أمكن تساوي الزيادات، فلنفرضها كذلك ، ولكون كل زيادة مع المزيد عليه موجودة في بعد لزم وجود بعد مشتمل على الزيادات المتساوية الغير المتناهية، لأن ذلك معنى حصول كل زيادة مع المزيد عليه، ولزم كونه غير متناه، لأن زيادة الأجزاء المقدارية بالفعل إلى غير النهاية، توجب عدم تناهي المقدار المشتمل عليها بحكم الضرورة، أو بحكم امتناع التداخل . وإنما فرض الزيادات متساوية احترازاً عما إذا كانت متناقصة، فإن انقسام المقدار ربما ينتهي إلى ما لا يقبل الانقسام بالفعل، فلا يلزم وجود البعد الغير المتناهي أو لا يظهر، وإما في صورة الزائد: فلاخفاء في أن الزائد مثل وزيادة فاللزوم فيه أظهر، ولما كان في هذا التقرير تطويل مع كون استلزام عدم تناهي الزيادات لوجود بعد غير متناه محل بحث ونظر.
And Ibn Sina objected to it that what follows from it is the increase of distance to infinity, meaning that it does not end at a distance beyond which there is no greater distance, and that is not impossible. Rather, the impossible is the existence of a distance between them whose length extends to infinity, and that does not follow. So he formulated it (or someone formulated it) as: We connect between two opposite points of the hypothesized two lines a line and we divide by the original distance, and the extension of the two lines then is by the original extension. Because the increase of distances in proportion to the increase of extension, it follows from the non-finiteness of extension the existence of increases upon the original distance that are infinite, because the ratio of the increase of distance over the original distance is the ratio of the increase of extension over the original extension. And since it is possible that the increases are equal, let us hypothesize them as such. And because every increase together with that to which it is added exists in a distance, it follows the existence of a distance containing the equal infinite increases, because that is the meaning of the occurrence of every increase together with that to which it is added. And it follows that it is infinite, because the increase of quantitative parts actually to infinity necessitates the non-finiteness of the quantity containing them by necessity, or by the impossibility of overlap. And the equal increases are hypothesized only as a precaution against the case when they are decreasing, for the division of quantity may end at what does not accept actual division, so the existence of the infinite distance does not follow or is not apparent. And as for the case of the increasing, there is no concealment that the increased is like the like and more, so the entailment is more apparent. And since in this formulation there is prolixity while the entailment of the non-finiteness of increases for the existence of an infinite distance is a point of investigation and reflection.

Commentary
This passage contains Ibn Sina's objection to the triangle proof and a refined formulation designed to meet it. Ibn Sina argues that the original proof only shows that the distance between the lines increases without end (i.e., it has no maximum), which is not impossible in itself. The real impossibility would be an actually infinite distance, which the original proof does not establish. In response, a refined version is presented: by connecting opposite points on the two lines and considering the original distance as a unit, the ratio of increase is maintained. If the extension is infinite, then there must be infinitely many equal increments added to the original distance. Since each increment exists with that to which it is added, this results in a distance containing infinitely many equal increments—an actually infinite distance. The author notes that the increments must be equal to avoid problems with division into indivisible parts. The passage ends by acknowledging that this formulation is lengthy and that the entailment of an infinite distance from infinite increases is still debatable.

Correction: Ibn Sina's objection shows that infinite increase (no maximum) is not necessarily impossible; only an actually infinite distance is impossible. The refined argument attempts to derive an actual infinite from equal increments.
