Suhrawardi's One-Line Proof for Finite Dimensions
Suhrawardi's reformulation of the proof for finitude of dimensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Suhrawardi's concise argument that dimensions must be finite by considering the distance between two lines.

Core Idea
If two lines extend infinitely, the distance between them would be infinite, but finite cannot equal infinite.

Suhrawardi, the master of illumination, offers a remarkably compact proof for the finitude of dimensions. He asks us to imagine two lines and the distance between them. By a simple assumption about their relationship, he shows that infinite extension leads to a direct contradiction. This lesson unpacks his elegant reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Understand the hypothesis
Suhrawardi hypothesizes that the distance between two lines equals their extension. This is a crucial assumption for the proof.
The hypothesis is mathematical: distance = extension at every point.
3
Apply infinite extension
If the lines extend infinitely, then by the hypothesis the distance must also be infinite. But is that possible?
4
Spot the contradiction
The distance between two diverging lines at any finite point is finite. So if the lines are infinitely extended, the distance would be both finite and infinite, which is impossible. The conclusion is that the lines cannot extend infinitely.
The contradiction shows that the assumption of infinite extension is false.
5
Reflect on the proof's elegance
Notice how a simple hypothesis and basic logic lead to a powerful conclusion about the nature of dimensions.
This proof is a classic example of proof by contradiction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لخص صاحب الإشراق في بعض تصانيفه البرهان بأنا نفرض بعد ما بين الخطين دائماً بقدر امتدادهما، فلو امتدا إلى غير النهاية كان ما بينهما غير متناه ضرورة(٣)، إذ المتناهي لا يكون مساوياً لغير المتناهي وعلى قدره، وهذا اللزوم واضح لا
The master of illumination (Suhrawardi) summarized in some of his works the proof as: We hypothesize the distance between the two lines always equal to their extension. So if they extended to infinity, what is between them would be infinite necessarily, for the finite cannot be equal to the infinite and of its measure. And this entailment is clear, and it is not...
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents Suhrawardi's concise formulation of the proof for the finitude of dimensions. He proposes to hypothesize that the distance between the two diverging lines is always equal to the length of the lines themselves. If the lines extended infinitely, the distance would also be infinite. But since the distance is bounded by the lines and is finite at any finite extension, the only way it could be equal to the infinite extension is if it itself is infinite, which is contradictory because the finite cannot equal the infinite. This direct contradiction shows that the lines cannot extend infinitely. The author considers this entailment obvious.

Correction: The equality assumption is a deliberate hypothesis for proof by contradiction; it is permissible to assume any consistent condition to test a claim.

C) That it is half their extension
D) That it is constant

Key Takeaway
Suhrawardi's proof shows that the assumption of infinite dimensions leads to a logical contradiction using the simple idea that a finite quantity cannot equal an infinite one.

?

The Ancient Proof of Finitude and Ibn Sina's Objection
Presents the ancients' proof of finitude and Ibn Sina's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the classical argument for the finitude of bodies and Ibn Sina's critical objection based on the distinction between potential and actual infinite.

Core Idea
Infinity can mean endless increase or an actual infinite magnitude — Ibn Sina showed the confusion between them.

The ancient theologians used a geometric argument to prove that bodies must be finite: if two lines diverge infinitely, the distance between them becomes infinite, which is impossible. This argument seemed solid, but Ibn Sina raised a subtle objection. He distinguished between a distance that increases without bound (potential infinite) and a distance that is actually infinite (completed infinite). Only the latter is impossible, and the argument only proves the former. This objection reshaped how infinity is treated in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that the passage has two clear parts: the ancient argument and Ibn Sina's objection.
2
Identify the key terms
Find the Arabic terms for 'enclosed', 'boundaries', 'impossible', 'necessary', 'increase', and 'infinite'. Understanding these will help you follow the logic.
The term 'غير النهاية' means 'infinity'.
3
Understand the geometric illustration
Imagine two straight lines drawn from a single point, like the legs of a triangle. As they extend, the distance between them increases. The ancients claim that if they extend infinitely, that distance becomes infinite, which is impossible.
4
Grasp Ibn Sina's distinction
Ibn Sina argues that what follows is only an endless increase (potential infinite), not an actually infinite distance. He says the impossible would be a distance that is already infinite in length, which does not necessarily follow.
This is the key insight: potential vs actual infinity.
5
Reflect on the implications
Ask yourself: If the argument only shows a potential infinite, does it still prove that bodies must be finite? How does this affect later theological proofs?
This connects to broader debates about the eternity of the world.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القدماء ، تناهي المحصور بين حاصرين وهو محال وجه اللزوم على ما نقل عن [؟] أنا تخرج من نقطة خطين كساقي مثلث، ولاخفاء في أنهما كلما يمتدان يزداد البعد بينهما، فلو امتدا إلى غير النهاية كان زيادة البعد بينهما إلى غير النهاية . واعترض عليه ابن سينا (۱) بأن اللازم منه ازدياد البعد إلى غير النهاية، بمعنى أنه لا ينتهي إلى بعد لا يكون فوقه بعد أزيد منه ، وهو ليس بمحال ، وإنما المحال وجود بعد بينهما يمتد طوله إلى غير النهاية ، وهو ليس بلازم
The ancients: the finitude of what is enclosed between two boundaries is impossible. The aspect of necessity according to what is transmitted [?]: we draw two lines from a point like the legs of a triangle, and it is clear that the more they extend, the greater the distance between them. So if they extend to infinity, the distance between them would increase to infinity. And Ibn Sina (1) objected that what follows is an increase of distance to infinity, meaning that it does not terminate at a distance beyond which there is no greater distance, and that is not impossible; the impossible is the existence of a distance between them whose length extends to infinity, and that does not follow.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents a classical argument for the finitude of bodies, followed by a crucial objection from Ibn Sina. The ancient argument proceeds as follows: from a single point, imagine two lines diverging like the legs of a triangle. As they extend, the distance between them increases. If they were to extend infinitely, this distance would increase without bound, which is considered impossible because an actual infinite distance would exist. The argument assumes that the distance 'between them' must be a single actual magnitude. Ibn Sina objects by distinguishing two senses of 'infinite distance': (a) a distance that keeps increasing without ever reaching a maximum (which is merely potential and not impossible), and (b) a distance that is actually infinite in length (which would be impossible). He argues that the argument only proves (a), not (b), so the impossibility of an actual infinite distance does not follow. The objection highlights a subtle but critical distinction in the concept of infinity.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Confusing potential infinite (endless increase) with actual infinite (a completed magnitude).
Correction: The ancients assumed that an endless increase implies an actual infinite magnitude, but Ibn Sina showed that an endless increase is merely potential and not impossible.

Checkpoint

A) The proof incorrectly assumes the lines are straight.
B) The proof only shows a potential infinite distance, not an actual one.
C) The proof assumes the lines are finite from the start.
D) The proof relies on an unproven geometric lemma.

Key Takeaway
Ibn Sina's objection reveals that the ancient proof only establishes a potential infinite, not an actual one, and thus does not prove the impossibility of infinite extension.

Ibn Sina's distinction shows that an endless process does not create an actual infinite. Will you examine your life today to identify where you are chasing potential infinity while craving actual completion?
?

?
Ibn Sina's objection forced a distinction between potential and actual infinity. In your worship, do you confuse the endless repetition of rituals with actual closeness to Allah? Commit this week to one act where you seek quality over quantity.

Taftazani's Revised Proof: The Equal Increments Assumption
Taftazani revises the proof to avoid Ibn Sina's objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani revises the classical proof of finitude by assuming equal increments to guarantee an actual infinite magnitude, circumventing Ibn Sina's objection.

Core Idea
Equal increments turn potential infinity into actual infinity.

In this dense passage, Taftazani refines the standard proof that bodies must be finite. He introduces a crucial assumption—that the increments of distance between two lines are equal—to avoid a subtle objection from Ibn Sina. By carefully setting up the geometry and reasoning with ratios, Taftazani shows that an infinite extension would necessarily yield an actual infinite magnitude, which is impossible. This lesson will guide you through each step of his argument and the reasoning behind the equal increments assumption.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Try to follow the geometric reasoning without getting lost in the technical terms.
Notice the phrase 'زيادات متساوية' (equal increases) — this is the key innovation.
2
Identify the geometric setup
Understand the two parallel lines, the connecting line between opposite points, and the original distance used as a unit. Taftazani uses these to define the extension of the lines.
The 'original distance' becomes the measuring unit.
3
Follow the ratio argument
Taftazani argues that if the extension is infinite, the number of equal increments (each proportional to the original distance) must be infinite. This is based on the ratio of increases.
The ratio of increase in distance equals the ratio of increase in extension.
4
Understand the equal increments assumption
He explicitly assumes the increments are equal to avoid the problem of decreasing increments. Without this, the division might terminate at indivisible points, preventing an actual infinite.
This is a precaution against the case where increments decrease.
5
Grasp the conclusion
With equal increments, the total distance contains an actual infinite number of quantitative parts, which is impossible. Thus the extension must be finite.
The impossibility of overlap ensures the parts are distinct.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقرره بأنا نصل بين نقطتين متقابلتين من الخطين المفروضين خطاً ونقسم بالبعد الأصل، وامتداد الخطين حينئذ بالامتداد الأصل، فلكون تزايد الأبعاد بحسب تزايد الامتداد لزم من عدم تناهي الامتداد وجود زيادات على البعد الأصل غير متناهية، لأن نسبة زيادة البعد على البعد الأصل نسبة زيادة الامتداد على الامتداد (۲) الأصل، وإذ قد أمكن تساوي الزيادات، فلنفرضها كذلك ، ولكون كل زيادة مع المزيد عليه موجودة في بعد لزم وجود بعد مشتمل على الزيادات المتساوية الغير المتناهية، لأن ذلك معنى حصول كل زيادة مع المزيد عليه، ولزم كونه غير متناه، لأن زيادة الأجزاء المقدارية بالفعل إلى غير النهاية، توجب عدم تناهي المقدار المشتمل عليها بحكم الضرورة، أو بحكم امتناع التداخل . وإنما فرض الزيادات متساوية احترازاً عما إذا كانت متناقصة، فإن انقسام المقدار ربما ينتهي إلى ما لا يقبل الانقسام بالفعل، فلا يلزم وجود البعد الغير المتناهي أو لا يظهر، وإما في صورة الزائد: فلاخفاء في أن الزائد مثل وزيادة فاللزوم فيه أظهر
He revised it by saying: we connect two opposite points of the two supposed lines with a line, and we take the original distance as a unit, and the extension of the two lines then is measured by the original extension. Since the increases in distance are proportional to the increase in extension, it follows from the non-finiteness of extension that there are infinite increases upon the original distance. Because the ratio of the increase in distance to the original distance is the ratio of the increase in extension to the original extension. And since equal increases are possible, let us suppose them so. And since each increase together with what is increased exists in a distance, it follows that there exists a distance containing infinite equal increases. That is because this is the meaning of each increase together with what is increased. And it follows that it is infinite, because the increase of quantitative parts ad infinitum necessarily entails the non-finiteness of the magnitude containing them, either by necessity or by the impossibility of overlap. And he supposed the increases equal only as a precaution against the case when they are decreasing, for the division of a magnitude may end at what does not accept actual division, so the existence of an infinite distance does not follow or is not apparent. And in the case of increase: it is clear that the increase is like 'like and more', so the consequence is more evident.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents Taftazani's revision of the proof, designed to overcome Ibn Sina's objection. The key move is to consider the distance between corresponding points on the two lines and to assume that the increments of that distance are equal. By fixing an original distance as a unit, he argues that the total extension of the lines is measured by multiples of that unit. If the extension is infinite, there must be an infinite number of such units. Since each unit exists in the distance between the lines, the total distance must contain an infinite number of actual parts, yielding an actual infinite magnitude. Taftazani also notes that if the increments were decreasing (not equal), the argument might fail because the division could terminate at indivisible points. By assuming equality, he ensures that the parts are actually distinct and infinite in number, which necessitates an infinite whole. The phrase 'like and more' (مثل وزيادة) indicates that in the increasing case, the consequence is even clearer.

Key Terms
زيادات
بعد أصل
امتداد
تناهي
تساوي الزيادات
أجزاء مقدارية
امتناع التداخل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A decreasing series of increments can still yield an actual infinite magnitude.
Correction: Taftazani shows that if increments decrease, they may terminate at indivisible points, preventing an actual infinite. Hence equality is needed.

Checkpoint

A) To simplify the geometry.
B) To avoid the possibility that the distances decrease and lead to indivisible points that would block the actual infinite.
C) Because it is the only way to measure the distance.
D) To make the proof match Euclid's axioms.

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's revised proof shows that an actual infinite magnitude necessarily follows from infinite extension only if the increments of distance are assumed equal, avoiding the subtlety of potential infinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The precaution against decreasing increments shows that a path of diminishing returns leads to a dead end. What would it take to restore equal increments of consistency?
?
The proof deems an actual infinite magnitude absurd. Yet you treat your time and ambitions as if they are infinite, never facing the reality of finitude. Suhrawardi's Two Proofs of Infinite Distance
Suhrawardi's two proofs for infinite distance between infinite lines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Suhrawardi's two proofs—the simple distance-equality argument and the geometric triangle argument—for the necessity of an infinite distance given infinite lines.

Core Idea
Infinite lines imply infinite distances—geometry proves what logic already demands.

Suhrawardi (the 'author of al-Ishraq') offers a crisp argument: if the gap between two diverging lines always matches their length, infinite length means infinite gap. But because the assumption might seem obscure, he also provides a geometric proof using a 60° triangle and Euclidean axioms. Together, these arguments establish a key premise for proving the existence of an infinite body, which is used in arguments for a Necessary Being.

Guided Path
1
Read Suhrawardi's simplified proof
Read the first part of the passage where Suhrawardi argues that if the distance between two lines equals their extension, then if the lines are infinite, the distance must also be infinite. Note the claim that this is obvious except to the obstinate.
The argument relies on the principle that the finite cannot equal the infinite.
2
Examine the geometric alternative
Read the second part which constructs a triangle with a vertex angle of 60°. Follow the reasoning that equal angles lead to equal sides, so the distance (base) equals the legs, hence infinite legs imply infinite base.
Euclid's theorem that equal angles imply equal sides is key.
3
Compare both proofs
Notice that both proofs reach the same conclusion: infinite lines -> infinite distance. The first is simpler but assumes the distance always equals the lines; the second removes that assumption using geometry.
This shows how different premises can converge on a single truth.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولما كان في هذا التقرير تطويل مع كون استلزام عدم تناهي الزيادات لوجود بعد غير متناه محل بحث ونظر. لخص صاحب الإشراق في بعض تصانيفه البرهان بأنا نفرض بعد ما بين الخطين دائماً بقدر امتدادهما، فلو امتدا إلى غير النهاية كان ما بينهما غير متناه ضرورة(۳)، إذ المتناهي لا يكون مساوياً لغير المتناهي وعلى قدره، وهذا اللزوم واضح لا يمكن منعه إلا مكابرة ()، لكن لما كان في إمكان المفروض نوع خفاء قرره بعضهم بأنا نفرض زاوية مبدأ الخطين ثلثي قائمة وللزوم تساوي الزاويتين الحادثتين من الخط الواصل بين كل نقطتين متقابلتين من ساقي المثلث، ولزوم كون زواياه مساوية لقائمتين، لزم أن يكون كل من الزاويتين ثلثي قائمة، ولزم من تساوي زوايا المثلث تساوي أضلاعه كل ذلك لما بينه إقليدس (۲) فيلزم من عدم تناهي الخطين عدم تناهي ما بينهما
Since this exposition is lengthy and the implication of the non-finiteness of increases for the existence of an infinite distance is a matter of investigation and scrutiny, the author of al-Ishraq (Suhrawardi) summarized in some of his works the proof by saying: we suppose the distance between the two lines always equals their extension. So if they extend to infinity, what is between them would be infinite by necessity (3), for the finite cannot be equal to the infinite nor proportionate to it. This implication is clear and cannot be denied except by obstinacy (). But since there is some obscurity in the possibility of the supposition, some explained it by saying: we suppose the angle of the vertex of the two lines is two-thirds of a right angle, and because of the equality of the two angles formed by the line connecting every two opposite points on the legs of the triangle, and because the sum of its angles must equal two right angles, it follows that each of the two angles is two-thirds of a right angle, and from the equality of the angles of the triangle follows the equality of its sides—all of this according to what Euclid (2) demonstrated. So from the non-finiteness of the two lines follows the non-finiteness of what is between them.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage presents two proofs for the same conclusion. First, Suhrawardi simplifies: if the distance between two diverging lines always equals the length of the lines themselves, then if the lines are infinite, the distance must be infinite, because a finite magnitude cannot equal an infinite one. He calls this obvious except to the obstinate. Second, since the assumption that the distance always equals the lines may be questioned, another proof is given using a triangle with a vertex angle of 60° (two-thirds of a right angle). Using Euclidean geometry, it is shown that if two sides (the diverging lines) are infinite, the third side (the distance between them) must also be infinite. The argument relies on the fact that the triangle will have equal angles and therefore equal sides, so the distance is always equal to the sides, hence infinite if they are infinite.

Key Terms
صاحب الإشراق
متناهي
غير متناه
مساوٍ
مكابرة
زاوية ثلثي قائمة
إقليدس
تساوي الزوايا والأضلاع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The geometric proof only works for a triangle with a 60° vertex angle.
Correction: Euclid's theorem that equal angles imply equal sides holds for any equiangular triangle. The 60° choice is convenient but not essential; the proof generalizes to any equiangular triangle.

Checkpoint
What is the key Euclidean principle used in the geometric proof?
A) The Pythagorean theorem.
B) That equal angles imply equal sides in a triangle.
C) That the sum of angles in a triangle is 180 degrees.
D) That parallel lines never meet.

Key Takeaway
Suhrawardi demonstrates that if two lines extend infinitely, the distance between them must also be infinite, whether through a simple equality argument or a geometric proof using Euclidean theorem.

Suhrawardi provides two proofs for the same conclusion—one simple and one more detailed. Are you satisfied with simple evidences of faith, or do you seek deeper understanding? What one simple proof about Allah's existence do you need to meditate on today to strengthen your certainty?

The proof assumes a construction that leads to necessary conclusions. In your life, what assumptions do you hold that, if followed consistently, would compel you to change a belief or action? Are you willing to follow the logic even if it requires sacrifice?

Suhrawardi's Shield Proof
Suhrawardi presents his shield proof to establish the angle and the consequence of infinite distance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the geometric construction of Suhrawardi's shield proof and how it attempts to show that infinite lines entail an infinite distance.

Core Idea
Six equilateral triangles in a circle force the distance between infinite lines to be infinite.

Suhrawardi attempted to find a method that would guarantee the vertex angle is exactly two-thirds of a right angle (60°), which is crucial for the parity argument. He invented a clever geometric construction using a shield (turs) shape. In this lesson, we examine his shield proof and its geometric reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to get the flow of the proof. Note the key terms like برهان ترسي and مثلث متساوي الزوايا والأضلاع.
2
Visualize the construction
Imagine a circle with six radii dividing it into six equal sectors. Each central angle is 60°. Connect opposite points to form equilateral triangles.
3
Understand the implication
The proof claims that if the two lines are extended infinitely, the distance between them (the chord) also extends infinitely. This is the core of the argument against infinite lines.
4
Note the dismissal
Suhrawardi dismisses any doubt as lack of geometric feeling. This attitude is important for the objection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحاول صاحب الإشراق سلوك طريق يوجب كون زاوية مبدأ الخطين ثلثي قائمة، فاخترع البرهان الترسي، وتقريره : أنا نخرج من مركز جسم (۳) مستدير كالترس مثلاً ستة خطوط قاسمـة له إلى ستة أقسام متساوية، فيكون كل من الزوايا الست ثلثي قائمة وكذا كل من الزاويتين الحادثتين من الخط الواصل بين كل نقطتين متقابلتين من كل ضلعين، فيصير كل مثلثاً متساوي الزوايا والأضلاع، ويلزم من امتداد الخطين إلى غير النهاية، قسم امتداد بعدما بينهما إلى غير النهاية . ومن تردد في لزوم تساوي الزوايا والأضلاع ، وجوز كون وتر زاوية مبدأ الخطوط الستة أقل من الضلعين أو أكثر فلعدم شعوره بالهندسة. واعترض على هذه البيانات بأنها إنما تفيد زيادة الأبعاد والاتساعات فيما بين الخطين إلى غير النهاية لا وجود سعة وبعد ممتد إلى غير النهاية، وإنما يلزم ذلك لو كان هناك بعد هو آخر الأبعاد يساوي الخطين اللذين هما ساقا المثلث فلا يتصور ذلك إلا بانقطاعهما وتناهيهما، فيكون إثبات التناهي بذلك مصادرة على المطلوب(4). ولو سلّم إنما لزم من المجموع المفروض ، وهو لا يستلزم (٥) استحالة لا تناهي الخطين
Suhrawardi attempted to follow a method that would necessitate that the angle of the vertex of the two lines is two-thirds of a right angle, so he invented the 'ters proof' (al-burhan al-tursi). Its explanation: we draw from the center of a round body (3), like a shield (turs), for example, six lines dividing it into six equal sections. So each of the six angles is two-thirds of a right angle, and likewise each of the two angles formed by the line connecting every two opposite points of each pair of sides, so it becomes an equilateral and equiangular triangle. And from the extension of the two lines to infinity follows the extension of what is between them to infinity. And whoever hesitates about the necessity of equality of angles and sides and allows that the chord of the angle of the vertex of the six lines is less or more than the sides, it is due to his lack of feeling for geometry. And an objection was raised against these data: they only yield an increase of distances and spaciousness between the two lines to infinity, not the existence of a spaciousness and distance extending to infinity. That would only follow if there were a distance that is the final of distances equal to the two lines that are the legs of the triangle, which cannot be conceived except by their termination and finitude. So proving finitude by that is a petition of principle (musadara) (4). And even if conceded, it only follows from the supposed aggregate, and it does not entail (5) the impossibility of the infinitude of the two lines.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage presents Suhrawardi's ingenious 'shield proof' (برهان ترسي), which aims to force the vertex angle to be exactly 60° by inscribing six equilateral triangles in a circle. By constructing six radii from the center, six 60° angles are formed. Connecting opposite points yields equilateral triangles, ensuring that the distance between the two original lines equals the sides. Therefore, if the sides are infinite, the distance is infinite. The author dismisses anyone who doubts the geometry as ignorant. Then a powerful objection is raised: all these proofs (البيانات) only show that the distance increases without bound (potential infinity) as the lines extend, not that there is an actually infinite distance. To get an actual infinite distance, one would need a 'final distance' equal to the infinite lines, but that would require the lines to be finite to have a final distance—thus the argument assumes what it tries to prove (petitio principii). Furthermore, even if the aggregate distance were infinite, that does not imply the impossibility of infinite lines. This objection encapsulates the core skeptical stance against the cosmological argument from the impossibility of an actual infinite.

Key Terms
برهان ترسي
زاوية ثلثي قائمة
مثلث متساوي الزوايا والأضلاع
وتر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The shield proof is a standalone argument for the existence of God.
Correction: The proof is only a geometric construction to establish the angle measure, not a complete argument itself. It is part of a larger argument for a finite universe.

Checkpoint
What does the shield proof construct to force the vertex angle to be 60°?
A) A square inscribed in a circle
B) Six radii dividing a circle into equal sectors
C) A tangent line to the shield
D) A perpendicular bisector

Key Takeaway
Suhrawardi's shield proof uses six radii and equilateral triangles to argue that infinite lines would produce an infinite distance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Final Objection: Potential vs. Actual Infinity
The objector challenges the shield proof on two grounds: confusion of potential and actual infinity, and begging the question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the shield proof only shows potential infinity (increase without bound) and not actual infinity, and that it commits a petitio principii.

Core Idea
The proof assumes what it tries to prove: that the infinite distance would be a final finite distance.

After presenting the shield proof, the text raises a powerful objection. The objection argues that all these geometric proofs only demonstrate that the distance increases without limit (potential infinity), not that there exists an actually infinite distance. Furthermore, to conclude impossibility, the proof would need a 'final distance' equal to the infinite sides, which requires the sides to be finite—thus the argument assumes what it wants to prove. This objection is crucial for the debate.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic of the objection carefully. Identify the two main points: the difference between potential and actual infinity, and the charge of begging the question.
2
Understand the potential vs. actual distinction
The objection states that the proof only shows an increase without bound (potential infinity), not an actually infinite distance. This is a key philosophical distinction.
3
Analyze the petitio principii claim
The objection says that to get an actual infinite distance, you would need a 'final distance' equal to the infinite lines, but that final distance would require the lines to be finite (to have an end), so the argument assumes finitude.
4
Consider the concession
Even if the aggregate distance were infinite, that does not prove the impossibility of infinite lines. The objection concludes that the proof fails.

Answering the Objection: The Proofs Determine the Nature of the Beyond
Objection and answer: the two arguments only prove existence, not nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two arguments for the finitude of the world, when combined with known premises, establish that the beyond is an infinite body and not pure non-existence.

Core Idea
The arguments refute pure non-existence and, with premises, show the beyond is an infinite body.

This passage addresses an objection against earlier arguments that aimed to prove something beyond the world. The objector claims these arguments only show existence, not nature. The answer shows that with additional premises, the arguments actually determine that the beyond is a body and infinite. Let's follow the reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and answer
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: an objection followed by a reply.
The objection is introduced with 'فإن قيل' and the answer with 'قلنا'.
2
Identify the objection's two points
The objector claims the arguments only show existence, not that it is a body or dimension, and even if conceded, not that it is infinite.
3
Trace the answer's reasoning
The answer first says the arguments refute the view that it is pure non-existence. Then it uses premises about the edge of the world and the impossibility of void to conclude it is a body and infinite.
Notice the premises: 'ما يلاقي طرف العالم لا يكون إلا خلاء' and 'إذا بين استحالة الخلاء تعين أنه جسم'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الأولان لا يفيدان، سوى أن وراء العالم أمراً له تحقق ما من غير دلالة على أنه جسم أو بعد، ولو سلّم فلا دلالة على أنه غير متناه . قلنا يفيد أن بطلان رأى من زعم أنه عدم محض ، ثم يدلان على تمام المطلوب بمعونة مقدمات معلومة، مثل أن ما يلاقي طرف العالم لا يكون إلا خلاء ، وهو بعد أو جسم، وهو ذو بعد بل إذا بين استحالة الخلاء تعين أنه جسم ، ولا يكون متناهياً وإلا لكان له طرف فيعود الكلام، ويثبت أن ما وراءه ليس عدماً محضاً.
If it is said: The first two do not benefit except that behind the world there is something that has some existence, without indicating that it is a body or a dimension, and even if conceded, there is no indication that it is infinite. We say: They benefit the falsehood of the view of one who claims that it is pure non-existence, then they indicate the complete required by means of known premises, such as that what meets the edge of the world is nothing but void, and it is either a dimension or a body, and it is possessed of dimension; rather, if the impossibility of void is shown, it is determined that it is a body, and it is not finite, otherwise it would have an edge and the argument would return, and it is established that what is behind it is not pure non-existence.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its answer concerning two earlier arguments (likely about the finitude of the world). The objector claims the arguments only show that something exists beyond the world, but do not specify whether it is a body or dimension, nor that it is infinite. The answer explains that the arguments do benefit: they first refute the notion that the beyond is pure non-existence. Then, by appealing to known premises—such as that what touches the edge of the world must be void, and void is either a dimension or a body—they show that if void is impossible, the beyond must be a body. Moreover, it cannot be finite, because if it had an edge, the same reasoning would apply. Thus, the arguments establish that the beyond is not non-existence and leads to a body that is infinite.

Refutation of the Claim That Motion as a Universal Can Be Eternal
Al-Taftazani refutes the objection that the universal essence of motion could be eternal with three arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Taftazani's refutation of the claim that the universal essence of motion could be eternal, including the three arguments against it.

Core Idea
Motion's nature as change and its dependence on particulars prevent it from being eternal.

The objection that motion as a universal might be eternal challenges the classical argument that anything subject to change must be temporal. Al-Taftazani responds with three decisive arguments: one from motion's essential nature, one from the dependence of universals on particulars, and one from the impossibility of an infinite regress. This passage shows how kalam reasoning dismantles a sophisticated counterargument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the objection
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Identify the initial objection: that the universal essence of motion could be eternal even if particular motions are not.
The objection targets the proof that whatever is not devoid of accidents is temporal.
2
Understand the first argument: motion's essence
Focus on the first answer: motion is change, and being preceded (masbuqiyya) is a necessary concomitant of its essence, so it cannot be eternal.
This argument cuts off the possibility at the definitional level.
3
Understand the second argument: universal-particular relation
The second answer: a universal exists only within its particulars. If motion were eternal, its particulars would have to be eternal, which is false.
This refutes the idea of an eternal universal with temporal particulars.
4
Understand the third argument: infinite regress
The third answer: an infinite succession of events is impossible, as shown by mental application (tatbiq) comparing two infinite series, which would require an unpreceded starting point.
This is a standard kalam argument against infinite temporal regress.
5
Synthesize the three arguments
Review how each argument addresses a different aspect: essence, instantiation, and infinite series. Together they decisively refute the objection.
This step helps consolidate the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل: امتناع أزلية الحركات الجزئية لا يوجب امتناع أزلية ماهيتها الكلية فيجوز أن يكون كل حركة مسبوقة بحركة لا إلى بداية، ويكون الجسم متحركاً أزلاً وأبداً بمعنى أنه لا يقرر زمان إلا وفيه شيء من جزئيات الحركة، وبهذا يقع القدح في أن ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث، لأن ذلك إنما هو على تقدير تناهي الحوادث، فالعمدة الوثقى في هذا الباب أن تبين امتناع تعاقب حوادث لا نهاية لها. أجيب: أولاً بأن حقيقة الحركة هي التغير من حال إلى حال. فالمسبوقية المنافية للأزلية من لوازم ماهيتها. وثانياً: بأن الكلي لا يوجد إلا في ضمن الجزئي، فقدم الحركة مع حدوث كل من الجزئيات غير معقول. وثالثاً: بأن تعاقب الحوادث لا إلى بداية سواء كانت حركات أو غيرها باطل بالتطبيق بحسب فرض العقل بين جملة من الآن وجملة من الطوفان كما مر، ولأنا إذا اعتبرنا سلسلة من هذا الحادث المسبوق لزم اشتمالها على سابق غير مسبوق، تحقيقاً لتكافؤ ما يشتمل عليه كل حادث من السابقية والمسبوقية المتضايفين].
For it is said: The impossibility of the eternity of particular motions does not necessitate the impossibility of the eternity of their universal essence, so it is possible that every motion is preceded by a motion without beginning, and that the body is moving eternally and perpetually, meaning that no time is fixed except that there is in it some particular motion, and by this the objection is made against [the premise] that whatever is not devoid of accidents is temporal, because that is only on the assumption of the finitude of accidents. So the strongest foundation in this chapter is to demonstrate the impossibility of an infinite succession of events. The answer: First, the reality of motion is change from state to state; being preceded (al-masbuqiyya), which negates eternity, is among the necessary concomitants of its essence. Second, the universal does not exist except within the particular, so the pre-eternity of motion together with the temporality of each of its particulars is inconceivable. Third, the succession of events without a beginning, whether motions or other things, is invalid by application according to the mind's supposition between a set from now and a set from the Flood, as has passed, and because if we consider a chain from this accident that is preceded, it must include an unpreceded predecessor, to realize the equivalence of what every accident includes of precedence and being preceded, which are correlatives.
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and a three-part answer regarding the possibility of eternal motion. The objector argues that although individual motions cannot be eternal, the universal essence of motion might be eternal, meaning that every motion is preceded by another motion without a beginning. This would undermine the proof that whatever is not devoid of accidents is temporal, since the proof assumes a finite series of accidents. The answer is given in three points: First, motion by its very nature is change from one state to another, and being preceded is an essential property that precludes eternity. Second, a universal cannot exist except as instantiated in particulars, so if motion were eternal, its particulars would also have to be eternal, which is false. Third, an infinite regress of events is impossible, as shown by mental application comparing an infinite series from now with an infinite series from the Flood—such a series would necessarily contain an unpreceded starting point, which contradicts the definition of an infinite regress. The commentary follows the author's order, first restating the objection, then explaining each of the three refutations in sequence.

Key Terms
الحركة
الأزلية
الكلي
الجزئي
تعاقب الحوادث
المسبوقية
التغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A universal (like motion) can be eternal even if its particulars are temporal.
Correction: A universal does not exist independently of its particulars; if motion were eternal, its particulars would have to be eternal as well, which is false.

Checkpoint
What is the first argument given in the answer to refute the claim that the universal essence of motion could be eternal?
A) Motion is a change from state to state, and being preceded is essential to it, negating eternity.
B) The universal exists independently of particulars and can be eternal.
C) The succession of events can be infinite.
D) Motion is not a real accident but a relation.

Key Takeaway
Motion cannot be eternal because its essence requires being preceded, its universal is dependent on temporal particulars, and an infinite regress of events is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You believe in Allah as the Eternal, yet you experience constant change in yourself—your body, your thoughts, your desires. If even the universal idea of change cannot be eternal, what does that force you to confront about the permanence of anything besides Allah?
?
The universal exists only in its particular instances. Where do you see the 'universals' of your life—like your identity, your faith, your goals—actually being real? Are you living as if they are real only in your mind, or are they manifest in the particular moments of your day?
?
Al-Taftazani argues that an infinite regress of events is impossible. Think of a habit or pattern in your life that you assume will just continue forever—what is the 'first motion' that started it? Are you avoiding confronting its beginning because that would require you to acknowledge your own responsibility?

The Syllogism for the Temporality of Bodies
Al-Taftazani states the syllogism and declares the major premise evident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the overall structure of the famous proof for the temporality of bodies: a syllogism with a self-evident major premise and a minor premise supported by two arguments.

Core Idea
Bodies are temporal because they cannot exist without accidents, and whatever cannot exist without accidents is temporal.

Al-Taftazani begins by stating the central argument for the createdness of material bodies. The proof is a simple syllogism: bodies are always accompanied by accidents, and anything always accompanied by accidents must itself be temporal. This lesson unpacks that syllogism and the self-evident nature of its major premise, setting the stage for the detailed arguments that follow.

Guided Path
1
Read the syllogism
Read the opening sentence that states the syllogism: bodies are not devoid of accidents, and whatever is not devoid of accidents is temporal. Notice how the conclusion is implied.
This is the core of the proof.
2
Understand the major premise
Al-Taftazani asserts that the major premise is evident (ẓāhir). Reflect on why it is considered self-evident: if something always has temporal qualities, it cannot be eternal itself.
The major premise is an axiom in kalām.
3
Preview the minor premise
The minor premise 'bodies are not devoid of accidents' will be proven by two separate arguments. This lesson only introduces them; subsequent lessons will examine each in detail.
Prepare to study the two arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشهور في الاستدلال على حدوث الأجسام أنها لا تخلو عن الحوادث، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث. أما الكبرى فظاهر، وأما الصغرى فلوجهين: أحدهما أن الأجسام لا تخلو عن الأعراض كما مر، والأعراض كلها حادثة. إذ لو كانت قديمة لكانت باقية بما تقرر من أن القدم ينافي العدم. والأزلية تستلزم الأبدية لكن اللازم باطل لما سبق من أدلة امتناع بقاء الأعراض على الإطلاق. وثانيهما: أن الأجسام لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون لأن الجسم لا يخلو عن الكون في الحيز، وكل كون في الحيز إما حركة أو سكون لأنه إن كان مسبوقاً بكون في غير ذلك الحيز فحركة وإلا فسكون لما سبق من أنه لا معنى للحركة والسكون سوى هذا، ثم كل من الحركة والسكون حادث. أما الحركة فلوجهين: أحدهما: أنها تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير لكونها تغيراً من حال إلى حال، وكوناً بعد كون وهذا سبق زماني حيث لم يجامع فيه السابق المسبوق، والمسبوق بالغير سبقاً زمانياً مسبوق بالعدم، لأن معنى عدم مجامعة السابق المسبوق، أن يوجد السابق، ولا يوجد المسبوق، والمسبوقية بالعدم هو معنى الحدوث ههنا. وثانيهما: أن الحركة في معرض الزوال قطعاً لكونها تغيراً وتقضياً على التعاقب والزوال، أعني طريان العدم ينافي القدم، لأن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه، فما جاز عدمه انتفى قدمه وأما السكون فلأنه وجودي جائز الزوال، ولا شيء من القديم
The famous [proof] in demonstrating the temporality of bodies is that they are not devoid of accidents, and everything that is not devoid of accidents is temporal. As for the major premise, it is evident. As for the minor premise, it is [supported] by two aspects: First, bodies are not devoid of accidents, as was mentioned, and all accidents are temporal. For if they were eternal, they would be persistent, based on the established [principle] that pre-eternity negates non-existence. And eternity necessitates perpetuity, but the consequent is false due to what has preceded of proofs of the impossibility of the persistence of accidents absolutely. Second, bodies are not devoid of motion and rest because a body is not devoid of being-in-a-locus (al-kawn fi al-hayyiz), and every being-in-a-locus is either motion or rest, for if it is preceded by a being in a different locus, it is motion; otherwise it is rest, based on what has preceded that there is no meaning of motion and rest other than this. Then each of motion and rest is temporal. As for motion, [it is temporal] for two reasons: First, it necessitates being preceded by another, because it is a change from state to state and a being after a being, and this is a temporal priority where the prior does not coexist with the posterior, and what is preceded by another with temporal priority is preceded by non-existence, because the meaning of the non-coexistence of the prior and the posterior is that the prior exists while the posterior does not, and being preceded by non-existence is the meaning of temporality (al-huduth) here. Second, motion is definitely subject to passing away, because it is a change and a successive passing away, i.e., the occurrence of non-existence negates pre-eternity, for whatever is proven to be pre-eternal is impossible to cease, so whatever is possible to cease is not pre-eternal. As for rest, it is because it is existent and subject to passing away, and nothing of the eternal [can be subject to passing away].
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the famous proof for the temporality of bodies (huduth al-ajsam). The proof is structured as a syllogism: bodies are not devoid of accidents (minor premise), and whatever is not devoid of accidents is temporal (major premise), therefore bodies are temporal. The major premise is self-evident. The minor premise is supported by two arguments. The first argument: bodies are not devoid of accidents (a'rad), and all accidents are temporal. This is because if accidents were eternal, they would persist forever (since eternity negates non-existence), but it has been proved that accidents do not persist. The second argument: bodies are not devoid of motion and rest because a body always occupies a locus (kawn fi al-hayyiz). Every locus-occupation is either motion (if preceded by a different locus) or rest (if not). Then each of motion and rest is proven to be temporal. Motion is temporal for two reasons: (1) it requires being preceded by another state, involving temporal priority and non-coexistence, which means it is preceded by non-existence—the definition of temporality. (2) Motion is inherently subject to cessation (zawal), and anything subject to cessation cannot be eternal. Rest is temporal because it is an existent that can cease, and eternal things cannot cease. The commentary follows the author's order: first stating the overall proof, then explaining each part of the minor premise and the sub-proofs for motion and rest.

Key Terms
حدوث الأجسام
الحوادث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the status of the major premise in the famous proof?
A) It is obscure and requires extensive proof.
B) It is evident (ẓāhir) and does not need proof.
C) It is derived from the minor premise.
D) It is accepted only by some scholars.

Key Takeaway
The famous proof for body's temporality is a syllogism with a self-evident major premise: whatever is never free of accidents is temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The author says the major premise is 'evident'—that everything subject to change is temporal. Is this truly evident in your soul? Do you live with the certainty that every object you own, every body you see, will perish? What would change if you held this conviction without doubt?
?
If you accept that bodies are temporal, then your own body is temporal. Does this truth shape how you use your body in worship and obedience? Are there parts of your daily routine that treat your body as if it were permanent? Confess one area where you need to realign.

First Argument: Accidents Are Temporal
Al-Taftazani presents the first argument for the minor premise using the temporality of accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first argument for the minor premise: that bodies are not devoid of accidents, and that all accidents are temporal.

Core Idea
Accidents are temporal because if they were eternal they would persist forever, but they do not.

The first argument to establish the minor premise draws on the previously established fact that bodies always have accidents. Then it proves that accidents themselves are temporal: if an accident were eternal, it would have to persist forever (since eternity negates non-existence), but it has been demonstrated that accidents do not persist. Therefore, accidents are temporal, and since bodies always have accidents, bodies must be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Recall that bodies always have accidents
The argument begins by referencing a prior conclusion: bodies are never without accidents (a'rāḍ). This is taken as given here.
This was established earlier in the course.
2
Understand the proof that accidents are temporal
Al-Taftazani argues by contradiction: if accidents were eternal (qadīma), they would persist forever, because eternity implies non-existence cannot occur. But it is impossible for accidents to persist, so they cannot be eternal.
The impossibility of persistence of accidents is a key kalām doctrine.
3
Apply the syllogism
Since bodies always have accidents, and accidents are temporal, then bodies themselves must be temporal (by the major premise). This completes the first proof of the minor premise.
This connects back to the overall syllogism.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشهور في الاستدلال على حدوث الأجسام أنها لا تخلو عن الحوادث، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث. أما الكبرى فظاهر، وأما الصغرى فلوجهين: أحدهما أن الأجسام لا تخلو عن الأعراض كما مر، والأعراض كلها حادثة. إذ لو كانت قديمة لكانت باقية بما تقرر من أن القدم ينافي العدم. والأزلية تستلزم الأبدية لكن اللازم باطل لما سبق من أدلة امتناع بقاء الأعراض على الإطلاق. وثانيهما: أن الأجسام لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون لأن الجسم لا يخلو عن الكون في الحيز، وكل كون في الحيز إما حركة أو سكون لأنه إن كان مسبوقاً بكون في غير ذلك الحيز فحركة وإلا فسكون لما سبق من أنه لا معنى للحركة والسكون سوى هذا، ثم كل من الحركة والسكون حادث. أما الحركة فلوجهين: أحدهما: أنها تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير لكونها تغيراً من حال إلى حال، وكوناً بعد كون وهذا سبق زماني حيث لم يجامع فيه السابق المسبوق، والمسبوق بالغير سبقاً زمانياً مسبوق بالعدم، لأن معنى عدم مجامعة السابق المسبوق، أن يوجد السابق، ولا يوجد المسبوق، والمسبوقية بالعدم هو معنى الحدوث ههنا. وثانيهما: أن الحركة في معرض الزوال قطعاً لكونها تغيراً وتقضياً على التعاقب والزوال، أعني طريان العدم ينافي القدم، لأن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه، فما جاز عدمه انتفى قدمه وأما السكون فلأنه وجودي جائز الزوال، ولا شيء من القديم
The famous [proof] in demonstrating the temporality of bodies is that they are not devoid of accidents, and everything that is not devoid of accidents is temporal. As for the major premise, it is evident. As for the minor premise, it is [supported] by two aspects: First, bodies are not devoid of accidents, as was mentioned, and all accidents are temporal. For if they were eternal, they would be persistent, based on the established [principle] that pre-eternity negates non-existence. And eternity necessitates perpetuity, but the consequent is false due to what has preceded of proofs of the impossibility of the persistence of accidents absolutely. Then each of motion and rest is temporal. As for motion, [it is temporal] for two reasons: First, it necessitates being preceded by another, because it is a change from state to state and a being after a being, and this is a temporal priority where the prior does not coexist with the posterior, and what is preceded by another with temporal priority is preceded by non-existence, because the meaning of the non-coexistence of the prior and the posterior is that the prior exists while the posterior does not, and being preceded by non-existence is the meaning of temporality (al-huduth) here. This passage presents the famous proof for the temporality of bodies (huduth al-ajsam). The proof is structured as a syllogism: bodies are not devoid of accidents (minor premise), and whatever is not devoid of accidents is temporal (major premise), therefore bodies are temporal. The major premise is self-evident. The minor premise is supported by two arguments. The first argument: bodies are not devoid of accidents (a'rad), and all accidents are temporal. Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that if accidents are temporal, the body could still be eternal by constantly acquiring new accidents.
Correction: The argument does not rely on the persistence of a single accident; it uses the fact that bodies are never free of any accidents, and since each accident is temporal, the body must be temporal as a whole.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Everything you own, every relationship, even your own body—all are made of accidents that are constantly being replaced. If you really knew that nothing in this world can stay the same for two moments, would you still cling to them as if they were permanent? What would change in your daily attachments if you truly believed this?
?
The argument proves that the only truly eternal Being is Allah. Your life on earth is a fleeting accident. Are you living as if this moment is just another step toward an eternal meeting with your Creator? Or are you so absorbed in the temporary that you forget the Everlasting?
?
The principle that 'pre-eternity negates non-existence' means that what is eternal cannot cease to be. Apply this to your own actions: does your faith in Allah—the only Eternal—reflect that same certainty? Do you act as if His promises are more permanent than the world around you? If not, what is holding you back from that conviction today?

Second Argument: Motion and Rest Are Temporal
Al-Taftazani presents the second argument, focusing on motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second argument for the minor premise: that bodies always have motion or rest, and both are temporal.

Core Idea
Motion and rest are always present in bodies, and both are temporal by their nature.

The second argument for the minor premise approaches from a more fundamental level: bodies always occupy a locus (kawn fi al-hayyiz), which is either motion or rest. Then each is shown to be temporal—motion because it involves change and cessation, rest because it is a contingent existent. This reinforces the conclusion that bodies, being inseparable from temporal qualities, must themselves be temporal.

Guided Path
1
Understand that bodies always have a locus
The argument starts by asserting that every body occupies a locus (al-kawn fi al-hayyiz). This is a necessary condition of being a body.
This is a definitional point in kalām physics.
2
Distinguish motion and rest
Occupying a locus is either motion (if preceded by a different locus) or rest (if not). This differentiation is crucial for the proof.
Motion and rest are the two modes of being in a locus.
3
Prove motion is temporal
Motion is temporal for two reasons: (1) it is preceded by another state (change), implying non-existence before; (2) it is subject to cessation, which cannot be true of the eternal.
Both reasons show motion is inherently temporal.
4
Prove rest is temporal
Rest is temporal because it is an existent that can cease, and whatever can cease cannot be eternal.
This is a simpler argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The famous [proof] in demonstrating the temporality of bodies is that they are not devoid of accidents, and everything that is not devoid of accidents is temporal. As for the major premise, it is evident. As for the minor premise, it is [supported] by two aspects: First, bodies are not devoid of accidents, as was mentioned, and all accidents are temporal. For if they were eternal, they would be persistent, based on the established [principle] that pre-eternity negates non-existence. And eternity necessitates perpetuity, but the consequent is false due to what has preceded of proofs of the impossibility of the persistence of accidents absolutely. Second, bodies are not devoid of motion and rest because a body is not devoid of being-in-a-locus (al-kawn fi al-hayyiz), and every being-in-a-locus is either motion or rest, for if it is preceded by a being in a different locus, it is motion; otherwise it is rest, based on what has preceded that there is no meaning of motion and rest other than this. Then each of motion and rest is temporal. As for motion, [it is temporal] for two reasons: First, it necessitates being preceded by another, because it is a change from state to state and a being after a being, and this is a temporal priority where the prior does not coexist with the posterior, and what is preceded by another with temporal priority is preceded by non-existence, because the meaning of the non-coexistence of the prior and the posterior is that the prior exists while the posterior does not, and being preceded by non-existence is the meaning of temporality (al-huduth) here. Second, motion is definitely subject to passing away, because it is a change and a successive passing away, i.e., the occurrence of non-existence negates pre-eternity, for whatever is proven to be pre-eternal is impossible to cease, so whatever is possible to cease is not pre-eternal. As for rest, it is because it is existent and subject to passing away, and nothing of the eternal [can be subject to passing away].
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the famous proof for the temporality of bodies (huduth al-ajsam). The proof is structured as a syllogism: bodies are not devoid of accidents (minor premise), and whatever is not devoid of accidents is temporal (major premise), therefore bodies are temporal. The major premise is self-evident. The minor premise is supported by two arguments. The first argument: bodies are not devoid of accidents (a'rad), and all accidents are temporal. This is because if accidents were eternal, they would persist forever (since eternity negates non-existence), but it has been proved that accidents do not persist. The second argument: bodies are not devoid of motion and rest because a body always occupies a locus (kawn fi al-hayyiz). Every locus-occupation is either motion (if preceded by a different locus) or rest (if not). Then each of motion and rest is proven to be temporal. Motion is temporal for two reasons: (1) it requires being preceded by another state, involving temporal priority and non-coexistence, which means it is preceded by non-existence—the definition of temporality. (2) Motion is inherently subject to cessation (zawal), and anything subject to cessation cannot be eternal. Rest is temporal because it is an existent that can cease, and eternal things cannot cease. The commentary follows the author's order: first stating the overall proof, then explaining each part of the minor premise and the sub-proofs for motion and rest.

Key Terms
الحركة
السكون
الكون في الحيز
المسبوقية
الزوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the second reason given for motion being temporal?
A) Motion is always preceded by rest.
B) Motion is subject to passing away (zawāl).
C) Motion is an accident that persists.
D) Motion is the same as change of locus.

Key Takeaway
The second argument shows that motion and rest—inseparable from bodies—are temporal, reinforcing that bodies themselves must be temporal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every movement you make—from waking up to lying down—is a new creation from Allah. When you last moved or rested, did you feel His constant recreation of you? If not, how can you cultivate that awareness starting today?
?
The proof says whatever can cease cannot be eternal. Your own life is full of changes: breath after breath, thought after thought. Are you living as if your current state will last? What specific habit or attachment do you need to let go of, knowing that all but Allah passes away?
?
You were non-existent before this moment. Does this truth humble you? What one action will you take today to show gratitude to the One who brought you from non-existence into existence and sustains you every instant?

The Standard Proof for the Temporality of Bodies
Al-Taftazani presents the standard proof for the temporality of bodies and the first proof for the minor premise using accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the standard kalam proof for the temporality of bodies, including its major and minor premises and the first proof for the minor premise using accidents.

Core Idea
Bodies cannot be eternal because they depend on changing accidents.

This passage presents the classic argument of Islamic theology for the createdness of the physical world. Al-Taftazani outlines the well-known proof that bodies are temporal because they are never free from temporals (accidents). Since the major premise is self-evident, the focus shifts to establishing that bodies are always accompanied by temporal accidents, which themselves cannot be eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical structure of the argument.
2
Identify the two premises
Find the major premise (the general rule) and the minor premise (the specific claim about bodies). Write them down in your own words.
3
Analyze the first proof for the minor premise
Focus on the justification that accidents are temporal. Understand the reasoning: if accidents were eternal, they would be permanent, but permanence is impossible for accidents.
This connects to earlier lessons on the impossibility of accidents lasting.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider why this proof is foundational in kalam. It establishes that the physical world is not eternal, leading to the need for a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشهور في الاستدلال على حدوث الأجسام أنها لا تخلو عن الحوادث، وكل ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث . أما الكبرى فظاهر، وأما الصغرى فلوجهين : أحدهما أن الأجسام لا تخلو عن الأعراض كما مر، والأعراض كلها حادثة. إذ لو كانت قديمة لكانت باقية بما تقرر من أن القدم ينافي العدم. والأزلية تستلزم الأبدية لكن اللازم باطل لما سبق من أدلة امتناع بقاء الأعراض على الإطلاق.
The well-known [method] in deriving proof for the temporality of bodies is that they are never free from temporals, and whatever is never free from temporals is temporal. As for the major premise, it is evident. As for the minor premise, it is established by two aspects: one of them is that bodies are never free from accidents, as has passed, and all accidents are temporal. For if they were eternal, they would be permanent, since it has been established that eternity precludes non-existence. And eternality entails perpetuity, but the consequent is false due to what has preceded of the proofs of the impossibility of the subsistence of accidents absolutely.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents the standard kalam proof for the temporality of bodies (huduth al-ajsam). It states the famous argument: bodies are never free from temporals (hawadith), and whatever is never free from temporals is itself temporal. The major premise is considered self-evident. The minor premise is supported by two proofs; the first proof is given here: bodies are never free from accidents (a'rad), and all accidents are temporal. The justification for accidents being temporal is that if they were eternal (qadima), they would be permanent (baqiya), because eternity precludes non-existence. Furthermore, eternity (azaliyya) entails perpetuity (abadiyya), but that is false because it has already been proven that accidents cannot persist for two moments. This argument relies on the principle that accidents do not subsist beyond their moment.

Key Terms
حدوث الأجسام
حوادث
أعراض
قدم
عدم
أزلية
أبدية
بقاء الأعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that 'temporality of bodies' is the same as 'createdness in time', but the argument here proves that bodies cannot be eternal, not necessarily that they have a temporal beginning.
Correction: The proof establishes that bodies are temporal (hadith) in the sense that they are not eternal (qadim). It does not directly prove that they were created at a specific moment; that requires additional argument.

Checkpoint
What is the major premise in the standard proof for the temporality of bodies?
A) Bodies are never free from accidents.
B) Whatever is never free from temporals is temporal.
C) Accidents are all temporal.
D) Bodies are subject to change.

Key Takeaway
The standard proof for the temporality of bodies rests on the impossibility of accidents being eternal, thus establishing that the physical world is not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This argument shows that your own body, with its constant accidents, cannot be eternal. If your physical existence is temporary, what are you investing your time and energy in that will outlast it? What one change can you make today to build for what is eternal?
?
The proof says that if accidents were eternal, they would have to be permanent, but permanence is impossible. Think about a change you have been resisting in your life (a habit, a worldview, a relationship). Are you treating something as if it were permanent when it is not? What would it mean to accept its temporality?
?
The major premise—'whatever is never free from temporals is temporal'—is presented as self-evident. But do you live as if this world is temporary? Examine your attachments: your comfort, your reputation, your plans. Where are you acting as if they are eternal? What step can you take today to realign your priorities with the temporal reality of this life?

The Second Proof: Motion and Rest as Temporal Indicators
Al-Taftazani defines motion/rest and proves both temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how motion and rest are defined in terms of being in a location, and the rational proofs that both are temporal (hadith).

Core Idea
Motion and rest are always present in bodies, and both prove temporality.

Al-Taftazani presents the second proof for the temporality of bodies by examining the universal phenomena of motion and rest. Before we can accept the conclusion, we must understand the precise definitions of motion and rest as relational concepts rooted in location (hayyiz). This lesson walks through those definitions and the two-part proof for motion's temporality, followed by the proof for rest.

Guided Path
1
Understand the criterion for motion and rest
Read the definitions carefully, noting how motion is defined as a being in a location preceded by a different location, and rest as otherwise.
2
Follow the two proofs for motion's temporality
Notice the first proof based on change and temporal priority, and the second based on motion's passing-away nature.
3
Grasp the proof for rest's temporality
See why rest is considered existent and capable of ceasing, and why this implies it cannot be eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما: أن الأجسام لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون لأن الجسم لا يخلو عن الكون في الحيز ، وكل كون في الحيز إما حركة أو سكون لأنه إن كان مسبوقاً بكون في غير ذلك الحيز فحركة وإلا فسكون لما سبق من أنه لا معنى للحركة والسكون سوى هذا ، ثم كل من الحركة والسكون حادث . أما الحركة فلوجهين : أحدهما : أنها تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير لكونها تغيراً من حال إلى حال، وكوناً بعد كون وهذا سبق زماني حيث لم يجامع فيه السابق المسبوق، والمسبوق بالغير سبقاً زمانياً مسبوق بالعدم، لأن معنى عدم مجامعة السابق المسبوق، أن يوجد السابق، ولا يوجد المسبوق، والمسبوقية بالعدم هو معنى الحدوث ههنا . وثانيهما: أن الحركة في معرض الزوال قطعاً لكونها تغيراً وتقضياً على التعاقب والزوال، أعني طريان العدم ينافي القدم، لأن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه، فما جاز عدمه انتفى قدمه وأما السكون فلأنه وجودي جائز الزوال، ولا شيء من القديم كذلك لما مرّ، وإنما قيد بالوجودي لأن عدم الحادث قديم يزول إلى الوجود إذ دليل امتناع عدم القديم . وهو أنه إما واجب أو مستند إليه بطريق الإيجاب، إنما قام في الموجود أما كون السكون وجودياً فلأنه من الأكوان، وأما كونه جائز الزوال فلأن كل جسم قابل للحركة . أما أولاً فلعدم نزاع الخصم في ذلك. وأما ثانياً فلأن الأجسام متماثلة فيجوز على كل منها ما يجوز على الآخر فإذا جاز الحركة على البعض بحكم المشاهدة جاز على الكل. وأما ثالثاً فلأن الأجسام إما بسائط وإما مركبات . فالبسائط يجوز على كل من أجزائها المتشابهة الحصول في الآخر، وما ذاك إلا بالحركة والمركبات يجوز على كل من بسائطها المقاسمة أن يكون تماسها الذي وقع بجزء من هذا يقع بسائر أجزائه المتشابهة حيز وذلك بالحركة . واعترض على ما ذكر في بيان امتناع خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون بأنه لو صح لزم أن يكون الجسم في أول السكون متحركاً أو ساكناً واللازم باطل قطعاً لاقتضاء كل منهما المسبوقية بكون آخر. وبأنا لا نسلّم أن الكون في حيز إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالكون في ذلك الحيز كان حركة ، وإنما يلزم لو كان مسبوقاً بالكون في حيز آخر ، وهذا في الأزل محال ، لأن الأزلية تنافي المسبوقية بحسب الزمان . وأجيب بأن الكلام في الكون المسبوق بكون آخر للقطع بأن الكون الذي لا كون قبله حادث قطعاً وفيه المطلوب، وعلى هذا فالمنع ساقط لأن معنى الكلام أن الكون إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالكون في ذلك الحيز ، بل في حيز آخر كان حركة ، وما ذكر من أن هذا ينافي الأزلية باطل، لأن الأزل ليس عبارة عن حالة زمانية لا حالة قبلها ليكون الكون فيه كوناً لا كون قبله، بل معناه نفي أن يكون الشيء بحيث يكون له أول وحقيقته الاستمرار في الأزمنة المقررة الماضية، بحيث لا يكون له بداية، كما أن حقيقة الأبدية هو الاستمرار في الأزمنة الآتية لا إلى نهاية .
And the second [proof]: that bodies are never free from motion and rest, because the body is never free from being (kaun) in a location (hayyiz), and every being in a location is either motion or rest. For if it is preceded (masbuq) by a being in a different location, it is motion; otherwise it is rest, since it has preceded that there is no meaning for motion and rest other than this. Then each of motion and rest is temporal (hadith). As for motion, [it is temporal] for two reasons: one of them: that it necessitates being preceded by something else because it is a change from one state to another, and a being after being, and this is temporal priority where the prior does not coexist with the posterior, and that which is preceded by something else temporally is preceded by non-existence, because the meaning of non-coexistence of the prior and posterior is that the prior exists while the posterior does not, and being preceded by non-existence is the meaning of temporality here. The second: that motion is definitely subject to passing away because it is a change and a successive occurrence and passing away, meaning the intrusion of non-existence contradicts eternity, because whatever is established as eternal is impossible to cease to exist, so whatever is possible to cease to exist negates its eternity. And as for rest, it is existent and possible to pass away, and nothing eternal is like that, as has preceded. It is qualified by 'existent' because the non-existence of the temporal is eternal, ceasing into existence. Indeed, the proof of the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal is that it is either necessary (wajib) or dependent upon it by way of necessitation, and this only stands in the existent. As for rest being existent, it is because it is among the locations (akwan). As for it being possible to pass away, it is because every body is capable of motion. As for firstly, because the opponent does not dispute that. As for secondly, because bodies are similar, so whatever is possible for one is possible for another; since motion is observed in some, it is possible for all. As for thirdly, because bodies are either simple (basait) or composite (murakkabat). For simples, each of their similar parts can occur in the other, and that is only by motion. For composites, each of their simple parts that are contiguous can have the contact that occurred with a part of this one occur with the rest of its similar parts in a location, and that is by motion. And an objection was raised against what was mentioned in demonstrating the impossibility of the body being free from motion and rest: that if it were valid, it would necessitate that the body at the beginning of rest would be either moving or resting, and the consequent is absolutely false because each of them requires being preceded by another being. And [the objector says] we do not concede that a being in a location, if it is not preceded by a being in that same location, is motion; it would only necessitate if it were preceded by a being in a different location, and this is impossible in eternity (al-azal) because eternality (azaliyya) is incompatible with precedence with respect to time. It was answered: the discussion is about a being preceded by another being, because it is certain that a being that has no being before it is temporal, and in it is the desired [conclusion]. On this basis, the objection falls because the meaning of the statement is that a being, if it is not preceded by a being in that same location but rather in a different location, is motion. What was mentioned about this contradicting eternality is false, because the eternal (al-azal) is not a temporal state with no state before it such that the being in it is a being with no being before it. Rather, its meaning is the negation that a thing has a beginning, and its reality is continuity in the determined past times such that it has no beginning, just as the reality of perpetuity (al-abad) is continuity in future times without end.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage provides the second proof for the minor premise of the standard argument for the temporality of bodies. It argues that bodies are never free from motion (haraka) and rest (sukun), and that both are temporal. First, it defines motion and rest in terms of 'being in a location' (kaun fi al-hayyiz): if the being in a location is preceded by a being in a different location, it is motion; otherwise it is rest. Then it proves that motion is temporal by two arguments: (1) motion requires being preceded by something else because it is a change from one state to another, and temporal priority implies non-existence of the posterior, which is the meaning of temporality; (2) motion is subject to passing away because it is a successive occurrence, and anything that can cease to exist cannot be eternal. Rest is also temporal because it is an existent thing that can cease to exist, and nothing eternal can be like that. The passage also addresses a possible objection. The objector argues that if we define motion as being preceded by a different location, then at the first moment of rest the body would have to be either moving or resting, which is absurd, and also that this definition cannot apply in eternity because eternity precludes priority. The response clarifies that the argument deals with a being that is preceded by another being; a being with no prior being is itself temporal by consensus. Furthermore, eternity (al-azal) is not a temporal state with nothing before it but rather the negation of having a beginning, i.e., infinite past duration. This refutes the objection.

حركة
سكون
كون في الحيز
سبق زماني
مسبوقية
عدم
قدم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first proof for motion's temporality, what is the meaning of al-huduth (temporality) in this context?
A) Coming into existence after non-existence
B) Being preceded by non-existence due to temporal priority
C) Having a beginning in time
D) Being subject to change

Key Takeaway
Motion and rest are temporal because they involve being preceded by non-existence and are possible to cease, contradicting eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that everything you see — your body, your surroundings — is constantly changing or resting, and both are temporal. If you truly believed that nothing in this world is eternal, would you still cling to it as if it will last forever? What would you change about your attachments today?
?
The proof shows that rest is possible to pass away because every body can move. Are you 'at rest' in your faith, assuming it will remain unchanged without effort? What if your spiritual 'rest' is actually an accident that can cease — what are you doing to prevent that?
?
You rely on reason to understand God's existence, but do you live as if reason alone is enough? This proof demands that you accept the temporality of your own existence. If you truly internalized that you are not eternal, how would your daily choices reflect that urgency?
