Defending the Proof: Objection on the Beginning of Rest and the Meaning of Eternity
Al-Taftazani refutes an objection about the beginning of rest and defines eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that the definition of motion fails at the first moment of rest and how the response clarifies the correct understanding of eternity (al-azal).

Core Idea
Eternity does not mean a timeless moment but infinite past duration.

Having established that motion and rest are temporal, al-Taftazani anticipates a critical objection: if motion is defined as a being preceded by a different location, then at the first instant of rest the body would have to be either moving or resting, which is impossible. Moreover, the objector argues that the definition cannot apply in eternity because eternity precludes any temporal precedence. This lesson unpacks the objection and the detailed response that redefines the nature of eternity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector points out a logical problem at the first moment of rest and challenges the applicability of the definition in eternity.
2
Follow the response: focusing on the type of being
The response restricts the argument to beings that are preceded by another being; a being with no prior being is itself temporal.
3
Learn the correct meaning of al-azal
The response clarifies that eternity is not a temporal state with no before, but the negation of having a beginning, i.e., infinite past duration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وثانيهما: أن الأجسام لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون لأن الجسم لا يخلو عن الكون في الحيز ، وكل كون في الحيز إما حركة أو سكون لأنه إن كان مسبوقاً بكون في غير ذلك الحيز فحركة وإلا فسكون لما سبق من أنه لا معنى للحركة والسكون سوى هذا ، ثم كل من الحركة والسكون حادث . أما الحركة فلوجهين : أحدهما : أنها تقتضي المسبوقية بالغير لكونها تغيراً من حال إلى حال، وكوناً بعد كون وهذا سبق زماني حيث لم يجامع فيه السابق المسبوق، والمسبوق بالغير سبقاً زمانياً مسبوق بالعدم، لأن معنى عدم مجامعة السابق المسبوق، أن يوجد السابق، ولا يوجد المسبوق، والمسبوقية بالعدم هو معنى الحدوث ههنا . وثانيهما: أن الحركة في معرض الزوال قطعاً لكونها تغيراً وتقضياً على التعاقب والزوال، أعني طريان العدم ينافي القدم، لأن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه، فما جاز عدمه انتفى قدمه وأما السكون فلأنه وجودي جائز الزوال، ولا شيء من القديم كذلك لما مرّ، وإنما قيد بالوجودي لأن عدم الحادث قديم يزول إلى الوجود إذ دليل امتناع عدم القديم . وهو أنه إما واجب أو مستند إليه بطريق الإيجاب، إنما قام في الموجود أما كون السكون وجودياً فلأنه من الأكوان، وأما كونه ثالثاً فلأن الأجسام إما بسائط وإما مركبات . فالبسائط يجوز على كل من أجزائها المتشابهة الحصول في الآخر، وما ذاك إلا بالحركة والمركبات يجوز على كل من بسائطها المقاسمة أن يكون تماسها الذي وقع بجزء من هذا يقع بسائر أجزائه المتشابهة حيز وذلك بالحركة . واعترض على ما ذكر في بيان امتناع خلو الجسم عن الحركة والسكون بأنه لو صح لزم أن يكون الجسم في أول السكون متحركاً أو ساكناً واللازم باطل قطعاً لاقتضاء كل منهما المسبوقية بكون آخر. وبأنا لا نسلّم أن الكون في حيز إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالكون في ذلك الحيز كان حركة ، وإنما يلزم لو كان مسبوقاً بالكون في حيز آخر ، وهذا في الأزل محال ، لأن الأزلية تنافي المسبوقية بحسب الزمان . وأجيب بأن الكلام في الكون المسبوق بكون آخر للقطع بأن الكون الذي لا كون قبله حادث قطعاً وفيه المطلوب، وعلى هذا فالمنع ساقط لأن معنى الكلام أن الكون إن لم يكن مسبوقاً بالكون في ذلك الحيز ، بل في حيز آخر كان حركة ، وما ذكر من أن هذا ينافي الأزلية باطل، لأن الأزل ليس عبارة عن حالة زمانية لا حالة قبلها ليكون الكون فيه كوناً لا كون قبله، بل معناه نفي أن يكون الشيء بحيث يكون له أول وحقيقته الاستمرار في الأزمنة المقررة الماضية، بحيث لا يكون له بداية، كما أن حقيقة الأبدية هو الاستمرار في الأزمنة الآتية لا إلى نهاية .
And the second [proof]: that bodies are never free from motion and rest, because the body is never free from being (kaun) in a location (hayyiz), and every being in a location is either motion or rest. For if it is preceded (masbuq) by a being in a different location, it is motion; otherwise it is rest, since it has preceded that there is no meaning for motion and rest other than this. Then each of motion and rest is temporal (hadith). As for motion, [it is temporal] for two reasons: one of them: that it necessitates being preceded by something else because it is a change from one state to another, and a being after being, and this is temporal priority where the prior does not coexist with the posterior, and that which is preceded by something else temporally is preceded by non-existence, because the meaning of non-coexistence of the prior and posterior is that the prior exists while the posterior does not, and being preceded by non-existence is the meaning of temporality here. The second: that motion is definitely subject to passing away because it is a change and a successive occurrence and passing away, meaning the intrusion of non-existence contradicts eternity, because whatever is established as eternal is impossible to cease to exist, so whatever is possible to cease to exist negates its eternity. And as for rest, it is existent and possible to pass away, and nothing eternal is like that, as has preceded. It is qualified by 'existent' because the non-existence of the temporal is eternal, ceasing into existence. Indeed, the proof of the impossibility of the non-existence of the eternal is that it is either necessary (wajib) or dependent upon it by way of necessitation, and this only stands in the existent. As for rest being existent, it is because it is among the locations (akwan). As for it being possible to pass away, it is because every body is capable of motion. As for firstly, because the opponent does not dispute that. As for secondly, because bodies are similar, so whatever is possible for one is possible for another; since motion is observed in some, it is possible for all. As for thirdly, because bodies are either simple (basait) or composite (murakkabat). For simples, each of their similar parts can occur in the other, and that is only by motion. For composites, each of their simple parts that are contiguous can have the contact that occurred with a part of this one occur with the rest of its similar parts in a location, and that is by motion. And an objection was raised against what was mentioned in demonstrating the impossibility of the body being free from motion and rest: that if it were valid, it would necessitate that the body at the beginning of rest would be either moving or resting, and the consequent is absolutely false because each of them requires being preceded by another being. And [the objector says] we do not concede that a being in a location, if it is not preceded by a being in that same location, is motion; it would only necessitate if it were preceded by a being in a different location, and this is impossible in eternity (al-azal) because eternality (azaliyya) is incompatible with precedence with respect to time. It was answered: the discussion is about a being preceded by another being, because it is certain that a being that has no being before it is temporal, and in it is the desired [conclusion]. On this basis, the objection falls because the meaning of the statement is that a being, if it is not preceded by a being in that same location but rather in a different location, is motion. What was mentioned about this contradicting eternality is false, because the eternal (al-azal) is not a temporal state with no state before it such that the being in it is a being with no being before it. Rather, its meaning is the negation that a thing has a beginning, and its reality is continuity in the determined past times such that it has no beginning, just as the reality of perpetuity (al-abad) is continuity in future times without end.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage provides the second proof for the minor premise of the standard argument for the temporality of bodies. It argues that bodies are never free from motion (haraka) and rest (sukun), and that both are temporal. First, it defines motion and rest in terms of 'being in a location' (kaun fi al-hayyiz): if the being in a location is preceded by a being in a different location, it is motion; otherwise it is rest. Then it proves that motion is temporal by two arguments: (1) motion requires being preceded by something else because it is a change from one state to another, and temporal priority implies non-existence of the posterior, which is the meaning of temporality; (2) motion is subject to passing away because it is a successive occurrence, and anything that can cease to exist cannot be eternal. Rest is also temporal because it is an existent thing that can cease to exist, and nothing eternal can be like that. The passage also addresses a possible objection. The objector argues that if we define motion as being preceded by a different location, then at the first moment of rest the body would have to be either moving or resting, which is absurd, and also that this definition cannot apply in eternity because eternity precludes priority. The response clarifies that the argument deals with a being that is preceded by another being; a being with no prior being is itself temporal by consensus. Furthermore, eternity (al-azal) is not a temporal state with nothing before it but rather the negation of having a beginning, i.e., infinite past duration. This refutes the objection.

Key Terms
أزل
مسبوقية
كون في الحيز
سبق زماني

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity (al-azal) means a timeless moment with no state before it.
Correction: The response clarifies that al-azal is not a temporal state at all, but the negation of having a beginning, meaning infinite past duration.

Checkpoint
According to the response, what is the correct understanding of al-azal?
A) A temporal state with no state before it
B) The negation of having a beginning, i.e., infinite past duration
C) The simultaneous existence of all moments
D) The state of being before all temporal things

Key Takeaway
The objection against the motion/rest proof fails because the correct definition of eternity is not a timeless instant but the absence of a beginning, allowing the proof to stand.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah has always existed without beginning, what does that mean for the urgency of your relationship with Him? How are you spending the limited days you have been given?
?
The objector tried to limit Allah's attributes by his own finite understanding. Where in your life have you resisted submitting to a truth about Allah because it did not fit your limited framework? Will you repent and submit today?

Objection Against Eternity of Motion and First Response
Al-Taftazani presents the philosophers' objection and answers with two proofs against eternal motion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' objection to the kalam argument for the temporal origination of the world and the first response proving that the essence of motion cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
Eternal motion would require an eternal particular, which is impossible.

In this passage, al-Taftazani presents a sophisticated objection from the philosophers: they argue that the standard kalam proof only applies to individual motions, not to motion as a continuous succession. He then begins his response by giving two proofs that the very essence of motion cannot be eternal, because eternity contradicts being preceded, and because universals only exist in particulars.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully. The philosophers claim that the kalam proof only addresses each particular motion, but they assert that motion as a whole is eternal in the sense of an endless succession.
2
Examine the first proof
The first proof uses the idea that eternity (al-azaliyya) is incompatible with being preceded (al-masbuqiyya). Since motion is essentially change from one state to another, it must be preceded, so it cannot be eternal.
Notice the logical form: eternity contradicts being preceded; being preceded is a necessary concomitant of motion; therefore eternity contradicts motion.
3
Examine the second proof
The second proof argues that if the essence of motion were eternal, then at least one particular motion would have to be eternal, because universals only exist in particulars. But no particular motion can be eternal, so the essence cannot be eternal.
This proof relies on the metaphysical principle that a universal exists only in its particulars.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل ما ذكرتم من دليل امتناع الأزلية إنما يقوم في كل جزئي من جزئيات الحركة، ولا يدفع مذهب الحكماء، وهو أن كل حركة مسبوقة بحركة أخرى لا إلى بداية والفلك متحرك أزلاً وأبداً بمعنى أنه لا يقرر زمان إلا وفيه شيء من جزئيات الحركة، وهذا معنى كون ماهية الحركة أزلية. وحينئذ يرد المنع على الكبرى أيضاً، أي لا نسلم أن ما لا يخلو عن الحوادث فهو حادث، وإنما يلزم لو كانت تلك الحوادث متناهية، فلا بد من بيان امتناع تعاقب الحوادث من غير بداية ونهاية على ما هو رأيهم في حركات الأفلاك وأوضاعها. أجيب: أولاً بإقامة البرهان على امتناع أن تكون ماهية الحركة أزلية وذلك من وجهين: أحدهما أن الأزلية تنافي المسبوقية ضرورة، والمسبوقية من لوازم ماهية الحركة وحقيقتها لكونها عبارة عن التغير من حال إلى حال ومنافي اللازم مناف للملزوم ضرورة. وثانيهما: بأن ماهية الحركة لو كانت قديمة أي موجودة في الأزل لزم أن يكون شيء من جزئياتها أزلياً، إذ لا تحقق للكلي إلا في ضمن الجزئي لكن اللازم باطل بالاتفاق.
If it is said: What you mentioned of the proof of the impossibility of eternity only holds for every particular of the particulars of motion, and does not refute the doctrine of the philosophers, which is that every motion is preceded by another motion without beginning, and the sphere is moving eternally past and future, meaning that no time is determined except that in it there is some particular of motion, and this is the meaning of the essence of motion being eternal. Then the denial also applies to the major premise, i.e., we do not accept that whatever is not free from contingent events is itself contingent, for that would only follow if those events were finite, so it is necessary to demonstrate the impossibility of the succession of events without beginning or end according to their view regarding the motions of the spheres and their positions. It is answered: First, by establishing the proof of the impossibility of the essence of motion being eternal, and that in two ways: One of them is that eternity necessarily contradicts being preceded, and being preceded is among the necessary concomitants of the essence of motion and its reality because it is an expression of change from one state to another, and that which contradicts the necessary concomitant is necessarily contradictory to that which it is concomitant with. Second: That if the essence of motion were eternal, i.e., existing in eternity, then some particular of its particulars would be eternal, for the universal has no existence except within the particular, but the consequent is false by agreement.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage presents an objection from the philosophers and the first part of the response. The objection argues that the classical proof for the temporal origination of the world (based on the impossibility of an infinite regress of events) only applies to each individual motion, but the philosophers claim that motion as a whole is eternal in the sense that at any time there is some motion occurring, even if each motion is preceded by another. They therefore reject the major premise that 'whatever is not free from contingent events is itself contingent' unless the events are finite, and they demand a proof against the endless succession of events. The author then gives the first answer, which consists of two proofs that the essence of motion cannot be eternal. The first proof is that eternity (al-azaliyya) is incompatible with being preceded (al-masbuqiyya), and being preceded is an essential property of motion because motion is change from one state to another. Since the necessary concomitant is contrary, what it accompanies is also contrary. The second proof is that if the essence of motion were eternal, then some particular instance of motion would have to be eternal, because a universal exists only within a particular. But that particular motion would then be eternal, which is impossible by consensus.

ماهية الحركة
مسبوقية
قديمة
كلي
جزئي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the eternity of motion as a whole is different from the eternity of each particular motion.
Correction: The author clarifies that the universal depends on the particular; if the essence of motion were eternal, at least one particular motion would have to be eternal, which is impossible.

Checkpoint
Why does the author argue that the essence of motion cannot be eternal?
A) Because motion is a contingent event itself
B) Because motion necessarily involves being preceded by another state, which contradicts eternity
C) Because the philosophers admit that motion is finite
D) Because the sphere is not eternal

Key Takeaway
The essence of motion cannot be eternal because eternity contradicts being preceded (essential to motion) and because an eternal universal would require an eternal particular.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that everything about you—your actions, your thoughts, your very existence—has a beginning. Yet do you live as if your time is infinite? How does al-Taftazani's proof that eternity contradicts 'being preceded' expose the illusion of tomorrow? What will you change today to stop postponing what you owe your Creator?
?
The universal 'prayer' only exists in the particular acts of prayer you perform day by day. If you claim to love prayer in general but neglect each specific prayer time, have you not fallen into the very error the philosophers made—imagining a universal without its particulars? What specific prayer will you guard this week to prove your love is real?
?
If the universe cannot sustain an eternal motion, how much less can your soul afford an eternal postponement of repentance? You are not infinite; every moment you delay is a moment that will never return. What sin have you been telling yourself you will 'eventually' leave? By what concrete step will you begin leaving it today?

First Proof: Movement as Effect of a Free Agent
Presents a proof for temporal origination from movement being the effect of a free agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the transient nature of movement demonstrates that it is the effect of a free agent, thereby establishing the temporal origination of bodies.

Core Idea
Movement's transient parts require a free agent, not a necessitating cause.

This lesson presents the first proof for the temporal origination of bodies, one of the foundational arguments in kalam. The proof hinges on the nature of movement: since each part of movement is fleeting, it cannot be the effect of a necessitating cause but must come from a free agent. This argument ties directly to the broader discussion of the universe's dependence on a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the logical flow of the proof sink in.
Notice the compressed style typical of kalam texts.
2
Identify the major and minor premises
The argument has two premises: a major (all effects of a free agent are originated) and a minor (movement is an effect of a free agent). Locate each in the text.
The major premise is referenced as 'previously established'.
3
Understand the justification for the minor premise
The minor premise is justified by the transient nature of movement's parts. Each part is passing away, so it cannot come from a necessitating cause. Grasp why this implies a free agent.
Contrast between necessitating cause (effect persists) and free agent (effect can cease).
4
Reflect on the conclusion and infinite succession
The conclusion is that the whole movement is the effect of the free agent. The passage also mentions an infinite succession of events conditioned by the passing away of the previous. Think about how this fits the argument.
This connects to the broader kalam framework of temporal origination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منها أن الحركة أثر الفاعل المختار، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو حادث مسبوق بالعدم. أما الكبرى فلما تقدم . وأما الصغرى فلأن كل جزء يفرض من الحركة فهو على الزوال والانقضاء ضرورة كونها غير متقررة الأجزاء، ولا شيء من الزائل بأثر للموجب لامتناع انتفاء المعلول مع بقاء علته الموجبة، وإذا كان كل جزء من الحركة أثراً للفاعل المختار كانت الحركة أثراً له لأن الموجود لكل جزء من أجزاء الشيء موجد له ضرورة، وقد سبق الكلام على ذلك في بحث استناد الحادث إلى الموجب القديم. وأنه يجوز ذلك بشرط حادث فغاية الأمر لزوم تعاقب حوادث غير متناهية يكون حدوث اللاحق منها مشروطاً بانقضاء السابق.
And among them is that movement is the effect of the free agent, and everything that is such is originated (hadith) preceded by non-existence. As for the major premise, it is as previously. As for the minor premise, because every part that is assumed of movement is in the state of passing away and ending, necessarily because it is not having parts that persist, and nothing that is passing away is an effect of the necessitating cause (al-mujib) due to the impossibility of the effect ceasing while its necessitating cause remains. And if every part of movement is an effect of the free agent, then the movement as a whole is an effect of him, because that which brings into existence every part of the parts of a thing is the bringer-into-existence of it necessarily. And the discussion on this has already preceded in the research on the dependence of the originated being on the eternal necessitating cause, and that it is permissible under a temporal condition, so the ultimate result is the necessity of the succession of infinite temporal events, the occurrence of the later being conditioned by the passing away of the earlier.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage is the first proof for the temporal origination (huduth) of bodies, presented as one of several arguments. It begins by stating that movement is the effect of a free agent (al-fa'il al-mukhtar), and everything that is an effect of a free agent is temporally originated and preceded by non-existence. The argument is structured into a major premise: 'everything that is the effect of a free agent is originated' (established earlier), and a minor premise: 'movement is the effect of a free agent'. The minor premise is justified by the nature of movement: every assumed part of a movement is passing away and ceasing. Since movement consists of parts that do not persist, no part of it can be the effect of a necessitating cause (al-mujib), because the effect cannot cease while its cause remains. Therefore, each part must be from a free agent, who can bring about a new effect at each instant. Since each part is from the free agent, the whole movement is also from him. The author notes that this has been discussed earlier regarding the dependence of the originated on the eternal cause, and it is permissible with a temporal condition, leading to an infinite succession of events where each later event is conditioned by the passing away of the earlier.

Key Terms
الحركة (movement)
الفاعل المختار (free agent)
الموجب (necessitating cause)
حادث (originated)
أثر (effect)
مسبوق بالعدم (preceded by non-existence)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Movement can be eternal if it is the effect of a necessary cause.
Correction: The argument shows that because movement's parts are transient, they cannot come from a necessitating cause (which would require the effect to persist). Hence, movement requires a free agent and cannot be eternal.

Checkpoint
What is the minor premise in the first argument for the temporal origination of bodies?
A) Every effect of a free agent is temporally originated.
B) Movement is the effect of a free agent.
C) Each part of movement is passing away.
D) The necessitating cause cannot produce transient phenomena.

Key Takeaway
Movement's transient nature proves it is the effect of a free agent, making it temporally originated and not eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Every moment of your life, including this very breath, is brought into existence by Allah's free choice. If He were to cease willing it, it would vanish. How does this realization change your awareness of your dependence on Him right now?
?
The argument shows that nothing transient can be caused by a necessary cause—it requires a free agent who chooses each instant. Acknowledge one area in your life where you have acted as if things happen by necessity (e.g., routine, health, provisions). What would it look like to consciously attribute each moment of that area to Allah's free will and thank Him anew?
?
If every part of your movement—every blink, every step—is directly created by Allah, what does that imply about your accountability for how you use these moments? Write down one specific action you will take today to use a movement (e.g., a word spoken, a gesture) consciously for His sake.

The Simpler Proof Against Eternal Bodies
Refutes philosophers' claim of eternal celestial spheres and presents a simpler proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the simpler kalam argument that an eternal body would necessitate either an eternal accident or an infinite succession of accidents, both of which are impossible, thus proving that no body can be eternal.

Core Idea
Every eternal body must have an eternal accident or endless accidents — both impossible.

The author now offers a concise argument that avoids prior complexities about the existential nature of rest or whether bodies can be free from motion and rest. Instead, it focuses on the necessary attachment of the accident of state (kawn) to any body that occupies space. This simple formulation powerfully refutes the eternity of any body, including the celestial spheres.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the structure: first a reference to refuting various philosophical positions, then the simpler proof presented in a disjunctive form.
The passage ends mid-sentence; the core argument is complete.
2
Understand the key term 'kawn'
Kawn (الكون) is the accident of being in a state or location. The author states that its occurrence for a body is necessary: whenever the intellect imagines a body and its occupation of space (tahayyuz), it judges that kawn exists for it.
This is a foundational principle in kalam.
3
Follow the disjunctive argument
The argument: If any body were eternal, then either its kawn is eternal (option 1) or kawns succeed one another without beginning (option 2). Both are impossible. Therefore, no body can be eternal.
The impossibility of an eternal kawn and an infinite regress of accidents are established earlier in the text.
4
Reflect on the simplicity
The author notes this proof is more concise because it does not require proving that rest is an accident or that a body is never free from motion and rest. It relies solely on the necessary connection between body and kawn.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إبطال ما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة من قدم الأفلاك
قال (ولولا القصد) إلى نفي ما ذهب إليه البعض من قدم الفلكيات وسرمدية الحركات، والبعض من قدم أجسام صغار لا تنقسم فعلاً مع سكونها أزلاً، ثم عروض الحركة لها أو بالعكس، لكفى ما قيل إن ثبوت الكون للجسم ضروري، فقدمه يستلزم قدم الكون أو تعاقب الأكوان من غير بداية، وكلاهما محال لما مر]
يريد أن القوم حاولوا بهذا الدليل التصريح بنفي ما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة من قدم الأفلاك بحركاتها بمعنى أن كل حركة مسبوقة بأخرى من غير بداية، وبعضهم من أن مواد العالم أجسام صغار أزلية لا تقبل الانقسام بالفعل، وهي في الأزل ساكنة تعرض لها الحركة فتتكون المركبات من اجتماعها، وبعضهم من أنها متحركة تتصادم، فتسكن فتتكون الأفلاك والعناصر، وإلا فله تقرير أخصر لا يفتقر إلى بيان أن السكون وجودي وأن الجسم لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون، فإن للحركة أجزاء مسبوقاً بعضها بالبعض، وهو أنه لو كان شيء من الأجسام قديماً لزم إما أن كونه قديماً وإما تعاقب الأكوان من غير بداية وكلاهما محال . أما اللزوم فلأن حصول الكون للجسم ضروري، فإن العقل إذا تصوره وتصور التحيز جزم بثبوته له ، فإن كان شيء من أكوانه قديماً فذاك، وإلا كان كل كون مسبوقاً
Refutation of what the philosophers (al-falasifa) have held regarding the eternity of the celestial spheres. He said (And without the intention) to negate what some have held regarding the eternity of celestial bodies and the perpetuity of movements, and some regarding the eternity of small bodies that are not actually divisible, while being at rest eternally, then movement occurs to them or vice versa, sufficient is what was said that the establishment of al-kawn (accident of being in a place/state) for the body is necessary, so their eternity entails either the eternity of al-kawn or the succession of al-akwan without a beginning, and both are impossible for what has passed. He intends that these people attempted by this proof to explicitly negate what the philosophers have held regarding the eternity of the celestial spheres with their movements, meaning that every movement is preceded by another without a beginning. And some of them [say] that the materials of the world are eternal small bodies which do not accept actual division, and they were at rest in eternity, then movement occurred to them so compounds are formed from their combination. And some of them [say] that they are moving, collide, then become still, so the spheres and elements are formed. Otherwise, there is a more concise formulation for him that does not require explaining that rest is existential and that the body is not free from movement and rest, because movement has parts one preceded by another, and it is that if something of the bodies were eternal, it would necessitate either that its al-kawn is eternal or the succession of al-akwan without a beginning, and both are impossible. As for the necessity, because the occurrence of al-kawn for the body is necessary; for the intellect, when it imagines it and imagines occupying space (tahayyuz), judges its affirmation for it. So if something of its akwan is eternal, then that is it; otherwise, every kown is preceded by
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is the beginning of a new section refuting the philosophers' claim of the eternity of the celestial spheres. The author quotes a statement (likely from the matn) that 'were it not for the intention' to refute various positions (eternity of celestial spheres and perpetual motions, or eternity of indivisible small bodies at rest then moving, etc.), the previous arguments would suffice. The commentary explains that these philosophers attempted to negate the philosophers' claim by noting that every movement is preceded by another without beginning, or by positing eternal small bodies. Then the author gives a simpler proof that does not require proving that rest is existential or that a body cannot be free from movement and rest. The simpler proof: if any body were eternal, then either its accident (kawn) is eternal or accidents succeed without beginning. Both are impossible. The necessity of this disjunction is because the occurrence of al-kawn for a body is necessary; if a body is eternal, it must have an eternal kawn or a succession of kawns without beginning. The explanation begins but is cut off at the end of the page.

Misconception: The simpler proof for the origination of bodies requires demonstrating that rest is an existential accident and that bodies cannot be free from motion and rest.
Correction: The author explicitly states that the simpler formulation (تقرير أخصر) does not need those premises. It relies only on the necessary occurrence of the accident of state (kawn) for any body, which is independent of motion and rest.

Key Takeaway

?

Refuting the Eternity of Celestial Spheres: The Proof from Kawn
The author presents a concise proof against eternal bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concise logical proof that no body can be eternal, based on the necessary occurrence of the accident kawn (state/position) and the impossibility of an infinite regress.

Core Idea
If any body were eternal, its state would be either eternal or part of an infinite regress—both impossible.

In this passage, the author distills a refutation of the philosophers' claim that celestial spheres are eternal into a crisp logical argument. Rather than relying on physics, he uses the metaphysical necessity that every body possesses an accident called kawn (being in a place or state). From this, he shows that eternity leads to an absurdity. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
The passage contains a few digressions about various philosophical views before the main proof.
2
Identify the two possibilities for an eternal body
The author presents a disjunction: if any body were eternal, then either its kawn (the accident of being in a state) is itself eternal, or there is an infinite succession of akwan without beginning.
These are the two horns of the dilemma.
3
Understand why the intellect judges that a body must have a kawn
The author states that the intellect, when it conceives of a body and conceives of its occupation of space (tahayyuz), necessarily judges that the accident kawn occurs in it. This means every body has some current state or position.
This is a self-evident premise.
4
Grasp why an eternal kawn is impossible
Every body is capable of motion (changing place), so its kawn is contingent and can cease. But the eternal is that which cannot cease. Therefore, no kawn can be eternal.
This uses the principle that what can cease cannot be eternal.
5
Grasp why an infinite regress of akwan is impossible
The author invokes the method of mutual comparison (tariq al-tatbiq) to show that an infinite series of successive states without beginning leads to contradictions involving priority and posteriority.
This is a standard kalam argument against actual infinites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال ولولا القصد إلى نفي ما ذهب إليه البعض من قدم الفلكيات وسرمدية الحركات، والبعض من قدم أجسام صغار لا تنقسم فعلاً مع سكونها أزلاً، ثم عروض الحركة لها أو بالعكس، لكفى ما قيل إن ثبوت الكون للجسم ضروري، فقدمه يستلزم قدم الكون أو تعاقب الأكوان من غير بداية، وكلاهما محال لما مر. يريد أن القوم حاولوا بهذا الدليل التصريح بنفي ما ذهب إليه الفلاسفة من قدم الأفلاك بحركاتها بمعنى أن كل حركة مسبوقة بأخرى من غير بداية، وبعضهم من أن مواد العالم أجسام صغار أزلية لا تقبل الانقسام بالفعل، وهي في الأزل ساكنة تعرض لها الحركة فتتكون المركبات من اجتماعها، وبعضهم من أنها متحركة تتصادم، فتسكن فتتكون الأفلاك والعناصر، وإلا فله تقرير أخصر لا يفتقر إلى بيان أن السكون وجودي وأن الجسم لا يخلو عن الحركة والسكون، فإن للحركة أجزاء مسبوقاً بعضها بالبعض، وهو أنه لو كان شيء من الأجسام قديماً لزم إما أن يكون قديماً وإما تعاقب الأكوان من غير بداية وكلاهما محال. أما اللزوم فلأن حصول الكون للجسم ضروري، فإن العقل إذا تصوره وتصور التحيز جزم بثبوته له، فإن كان شيء من أكوانه قديماً فذاك، وإلا كان كل كون مسبوقاً بكون آخر لا إلى بداية وهو الأمر الثاني، وأما استحالة الأمرين، فالأول لما سبق أن كل جسم قابل للحركة من حيز إلى حيز إما بتمامه كما في الحركة المستقيمة، أو بأجزائه كما في الحركة المستديرة فيكون كل كون جائز الزوال ولا شيء من جائز الزوال بقديم لأن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه وينعكس إلى أن ما جاز عدمه انتفى قدمه. ذلك. والثاني لما مر من أن طريق التطبيق تضايف السابقية والمسبوقية وغير ذلك.
He said: 'Were it not for the intention to negate what some have held of the eternity of the celestial spheres and the perpetuity of motions, and some have held of the eternity of small bodies that are not actually divisible, with their rest from eternity, then the occurrence of motion to them or vice versa, it would suffice what was said: that the existence of the state (kawn) for the body is necessary, so its eternity entails either the eternity of the state or the succession of states without beginning, and both are impossible for what has passed.' He intends that the people attempted by this proof to explicitly negate what the philosophers held of the eternity of the celestial spheres with their motions, meaning that every motion is preceded by another without beginning, and some of them [held] that the materials of the world are eternal small bodies not accepting actual division, and they were at rest in eternity, then motion occurs to them so compounds are formed from their aggregation, and some of them [held] that they are moving and collide, then become still, so the celestial spheres and elements are formed. Otherwise, he has a more concise presentation that does not need the demonstration that rest is existential and that the body is not free from motion and rest, for motion has parts some of which precede others. And it is that if any body were eternal, it would necessitate either that it be eternal or the succession of states without beginning, and both are impossible. As for the necessity, it is because the occurrence of the state to the body is necessary, for the intellect, when it conceives it [the state] and conceives occupation of space, judges its establishment for it. So if something of its states is eternal, then that [is the case]; otherwise, every state is preceded by another state without beginning, and that is the second matter. As for the impossibility of the two matters: the first [is impossible] because of what preceded: that every body is capable of motion from place to place, either in its entirety as in rectilinear motion, or in its parts as in circular motion, so every state is subject to cessation, and nothing subject to cessation is eternal, because what has been established as eternal is impossible to cease, and the converse follows: that what is possible to cease is negated from eternity. That [is the first]. And the second [is impossible] because of what passed that the method of mutual comparison (tariq al-tatbiq) involves the correlation of priority and posteriority and other than that.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage presents a concise refutation of the philosophers' claim that celestial spheres and their motions are eternal. The author begins by noting that the philosophers hold various views: some say celestial spheres are eternal with perpetual motions; some say the world's materials are eternal small atoms that were initially at rest then motion occurred; others say they were moving then collided and became still. The author then gives a shorter proof that avoids discussing the ontological status of rest or the impossibility of a body being free of motion and rest. The proof is: if any body were eternal, then either its kawn (the accident of being in a place or state) is eternal, or there is an infinite regress of akwan (states) without beginning. Both are impossible. The necessity of this disjunction arises because a body necessarily possesses a kawn; the intellect judges that any body occupying space must have such an accident. If that kawn is eternal, that is one case; if not, then each kawn is preceded by another without end. The impossibility of the first case is that every body is subject to change of place (motion), so its kawn is contingent and can cease; what is contingent cannot be eternal, because what is eternal cannot cease. The impossibility of the second case is established by the method of mutual comparison (tariq al-tatbiq), which involves correlating priority and posteriority in an infinite series, which is impossible. Thus the argument concludes that no body can be eternal; all bodies are temporal (hadith).

الأكوان (al-akwan)

الحادث (al-hadith)
السابقية والمسبوقية
طريق التطبيق
إبطال
الأفلاك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The proof relies on physical premises about motion (e.g., that every body is capable of motion).
Correction: The author explicitly provides a concise proof that avoids needing to prove that rest is existential or that a body cannot be free from motion and rest. The proof only depends on the necessity of the accident kawn and the impossibility of an infinite regress.

Checkpoint
In the concise proof against the eternity of celestial spheres, what two possibilities are presented if a body were eternal?
A) Either the body's motion is eternal or its rest is eternal.
B) Either the body's kawn (state) is eternal or there is an infinite regress of akwan.
C) Either the body is composed of atoms or it is not.
D) Either the body is eternal or it is temporal.

Key Takeaway
No body can be eternal because its necessary accident (kawn) is contingent and cannot be eternal, and an infinite regress of such accidents is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

If the universe itself is not eternal, then your own existence is not self-sufficient. What does that mean for the way you handle your fears about the future? Will you acknowledge your contingency and turn to the Eternal, or will you continue to rely on fleeting things as if they will never end?
?
The proof shows that every body, including your own, has states that are contingent and cannot be eternal. Do you live as if your health, wealth, or relationships are permanent? What would change if you truly internalised that only Allah is eternal?
?

Proof that Bodies are Temporal as Loci for Accidents
The author presents the second proof for the temporality of bodies with an objection and response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof for the temporality of bodies, based on the observation that bodies are loci for accidents, and how the author defends the universality of this premise against an objection.

Core Idea
Bodies are temporal because they undergo accidents, and the eternal cannot be subject to change.

This lesson examines a key argument in Islamic theology: the second proof that bodies are temporal. The argument hinges on the observation that all bodies are loci for accidents, and the principle that the eternal cannot be described by temporal accidents. We will see how the author presents this argument and then addresses an objection about its scope, ultimately defending the universal premise by pointing to celestial spheres and elements as examples.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal translation once. Pay attention to the logical flow: premise, conclusion, objection, and response.
The passage is concise but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the core argument
Identify the two main premises: (1) every body is a locus for accidents (observation), (2) nothing eternal can be a locus for temporal accidents. From these, the conclusion that every body is temporal follows.
Premise (2) is not proven here but referenced from the later section on Divine Matters.
3
Understand the objection
The objector challenges the first premise: if taken as universal, it is not self-evident; if taken as particular, it does not prove all bodies are temporal. Grasp the dilemma.
The objector concedes that some bodies (like compounds) are temporal, but that is not in dispute.
4
Follow the author's response
The author insists the premise is universal and demonstrates it by showing that celestial spheres (motions and positions) and elements (lights and states) all undergo temporal accidents. This covers the simples, and compounds necessarily follow.
This response strengthens the proof by providing empirical evidence.
5
Reflect on the logical structure
Consider how the argument is structured: an objection that raises a logical flaw, and a response that closes the gap by proving the universal premise via concrete examples. This is a model of dialectical reasoning in kalam.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الثاني أن الجسم محل للحوادث وهو ظاهر فيكون حادثاً، لما سيجيء من امتناع اتصاف القديم بالحادث. أي متصف بها بحكم المشاهدة، ولا شيء من القديم كذلك كما سيجيء في الإلهيات. فإن قيل: إن أخذت الصغرى كلية فالمنع ظاهر، ودعوى الضرورة باطلة، وإن أخذت جزئية لم يفد المطلوب. أعني حدوث كل جسم، فإن حدوث بعض الأجسام كالمركبات العنصرية، مما لا نزاع فيه. قلنا تؤخذ كلية وتبين بأن الأفلاك والعناصر كلها تتصف بالحركات والأوضاع الحادثة، والعناصر خاصة بالأضواء والأحوال الأخر. ويلزم من حدوث البسائط حدوث المركبات منها ضرورة.
He said: 'The second [proof] is that the body is a locus for accidents (mahall li'l-hawadith), and it is apparent, so it is temporal (hadith), due to what will come of the impossibility of the eternal being described by the temporal. That is, it is described by them [the accidents] by virtue of observation, and nothing eternal is like that as will come in the Divine Matters (al-ilahiyyat). If it is said: 'If the minor premise is taken as universal, then the denial is apparent, and the claim of necessity is false; and if it is taken as particular, it does not yield the desired result, namely the temporality of every body, for the temporality of some bodies, such as elemental compounds, is not disputed.' We say: It is taken as universal, and it is shown that the celestial spheres and elements are all described by motions and temporal positions (awda'), and the elements specifically by lights and other states. And from the temporality of simples (al-basa'it) necessarily follows the temporality of compounds from them.'
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the second argument for the temporality of bodies: the body is a locus for accidents (mahall li'l-hawadith). The argument is: (1) Every body is a locus for accidents (by observation). (2) Any locus for accidents is temporal (since the eternal cannot be described by temporal accidents). (3) Therefore, every body is temporal. An objector raises a challenge: if the minor premise ('every body is a locus for accidents') is taken as universal, it is not self-evident and can be denied; if taken as particular, it does not prove the temporality of all bodies (since some bodies like compounds are already agreed to be temporal). The author answers that the minor premise is taken as universal, and it is shown to be true because celestial spheres and elements all undergo temporal accidents: celestial spheres have motions and changing positions, and elements have lights and other changing states. Since the simples (basa'it: celestial spheres and elements) are temporal, the compounds made from them are temporally a fortiori. Thus the argument stands.

Key Terms
الجسم (al-jism)
محل للحوادث (mahall li'l-hawadith)
القديم (al-qadim)
الحادث (al-hadith)
البسائط (al-basa'it)
المركبات (al-murakkabat)
الأفلاك (al-aflak)
العناصر (al-'anasir)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the proof requires demonstrating that every accident is temporal, but the key point is that the eternal cannot be a locus for any temporal accident, regardless of the accident's nature.
Correction: The argument does not hinge on the temporality of accidents per se, but on the impossibility of the eternal being described by temporal attributes. The observation that bodies have accidents is enough to establish temporality, given that eternal beings cannot change.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, why does the fact that bodies are loci for accidents prove they are temporal?
A) Because accidents are temporal, and whatever is a locus for temporal things must itself be temporal.
B) Because the eternal cannot be described by temporal accidents, so a body that undergoes accidents cannot be eternal.
C) Because all bodies are composed of elements which are temporal.
D) Because observation shows bodies change, which implies they are not eternal.

Key Takeaway
The second proof establishes that bodies are temporal because they are observed to be loci for accidents, and the eternal cannot be described by temporal events.

The Second Argument: Body as Locus of Accidents and Its Defense
Al-Taftazani presents the second argument for the body's temporality and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second kalam argument for the temporality of bodies and how the objection regarding the universality of the premise is resolved.

Core Idea
Every body is a locus of temporal accidents, so it must itself be temporal.

Al-Taftazani now presents the second proof that bodies are temporal. The argument builds on the observed fact that bodies undergo motion and change. But a critic might object that the universal premise 'every body is a locus of accidents' is not self-evident. In this passage, the author states the argument, raises the objection, and then defends the universal premise by pointing to the celestial spheres and elements as examples.

Guided Path
1
Understand the preliminary impossibilities
The passage opens with two impossibilities related to the concept of a 'last without beginning'. The first impossibility is that every body is movable, so every atom is subject to perishing, and thus not eternal. The second involves the method of correspondence. Grasp these as context for the main argument.
These are mentioned briefly; focus on the main argument.
2
Grasp the core syllogism
The second argument states: every body is a locus of accidents (major premise). The eternal cannot be described by temporal accidents (minor premise). Therefore, no body is eternal. Understand how these premises lead to the conclusion that all bodies are temporal.
This is the heart of the proof.
3
Identify the objection
An opponent points out: if the minor premise 'every body is a locus of accidents' is taken as universal, it can be denied; if taken as particular, it does not prove the temporality of every body. Recognize the logic of this objection.
The objection challenges the universality of the observation.
4
See how the answer defends the premise
Al-Taftazani responds that the premise is indeed universal, and it is established by observing that celestial spheres and elements are characterized by temporal motions, positions, light, and other states. From the temporality of these simples, the temporality of compounds necessarily follows.
The answer uses empirical evidence to support the universal claim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بكون آخر لا إلى بداية وهو الأمر الثاني، وأما استحالة الأمرين، فالأول لما سبق . أن كل جسم قابل للحركة من حيز إلى حيز إما بتمامه كما في الحركة المستقيمة، أو بأجزائه كما في الحركة المستديرة فيكون كل كون جائز الزوال ولا شيء من جائز الزوال بقديم لأن ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه وينعكس إلى أن ما جاز عدمه انتفى قدمه. ذلك . • والثاني لما مر من أن (1) طريق التطبيق تضايف السابقية والمسبوقية وغير أدلة أن الجسم محل للحوادث. قال الثاني أن الجسم محل للحوادث وهو ظاهر فيكون حادثاً، لما سيجيء من امتناع اتصاف القديم بالحادث. أي متصف بها بحكم المشاهدة، ولا شيء من القديم كذلك كما سيجيء في الإلهيات (2). فإن قيل : إن أخذت الصغرى كلية فالمنع ظاهر، ودعوى الضرورة باطلة، وإن أخذت جزئية لم يفد (3) المطلوب . أعني حدوث كل جسم ، فإن حدوث بعض الأجسام كالمركبات العنصرية، مما لا نزاع فيه. قلنا تؤخذ كلية وتبين بأن الأفلاك والعناصر كلها تتصف بالحركات والأوضاع (4) الحادثة، والعناصر خاصة بالأضواء والأحوال الأخر. ويلزم من حدوث البسائط حدوث المركبات منها ضرورة.
Being the last without beginning, which is the second matter. As for the impossibility of the two matters: the first is due to what preceded: that every body is susceptible to movement from one place to another, either in its entirety as in rectilinear motion, or in its parts as in circular motion; thus every atom (kawun) is subject to perishing, and nothing subject to perishing is eternal, because what is established in eternity, its non-existence is impossible, and the converse implies that what is possible of non-existence negates its eternity. That. And the second is due to what was mentioned that the method of correspondence (tataabuq) involves the mutual relation of priority and posteriority, and other proofs that the body is a locus of accidents. He said: the second is that the body is a locus of accidents, which is apparent, so it is temporal, because of what will come of the impossibility of the eternal being described by temporal accidents. That is, it is described by them by the judgment of observation, and nothing eternal is like that, as will come in the divine matters. If it is said: If the minor premise is taken as universal, then the denial is apparent, and the claim of necessity is false; and if it is taken as particular, it does not yield the desired result, namely the temporality of every body, for the temporality of some bodies, like elemental compounds, is not disputed. We say: It is taken as universal and is clarified by the fact that the celestial spheres and elements are all characterized by temporal motions and positions, and the elements especially by lights and other states. And from the temporality of simples, the temporality of compounds necessarily follows.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents the second proof for the temporality of bodies, along with an objection and its answer. The author first explains two impossibilities: the first concerns the concept of a last without beginning (that is, an atom that is temporally originated but has no beginning). The first impossibility is that every body is movable from one place to another, either entirely (rectilinear) or by its parts (circular), so every atom is subject to perishing, and nothing subject to perishing is eternal. The second impossibility is based on the method of correspondence (tataabuq), which involves the mutual relation of priority and posteriority, and other proofs that the body is a locus of accidents. Then he states the argument: the body is a locus of accidents (by observation), so it is temporal, because the eternal cannot be described by temporal accidents. An objection is raised: if the minor premise (every body is a locus of accidents) is taken as universal, it is denied, and the claim of necessity is false; if taken as particular, it does not prove the temporality of every body. The answer is that the minor premise is universal and is established by the fact that the celestial spheres and elements are all characterized by temporal motions and positions, and elements by lights and other states. From the temporality of simples (like the celestial spheres and elements) the temporality of compounds necessarily follows.

Correction: The answer shows that the universal premise is not merely claimed but is supported by empirical evidence from celestial spheres and elements, so the conclusion holds.

Key Takeaway
The second argument for the temporality of bodies is that since every body is observed to be a locus of temporal accidents, and the eternal cannot be described by temporal accidents, bodies must be temporal; any objection to the universality of the premise is refuted by evidence from the celestial spheres and elements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every body—from the stars above to the atoms within you—is temporal and will inevitably perish, what does it mean for your relentless pursuit of worldly goals? Are you building your life on sand that shifts, or on the Eternal One who never changes?
?
The objection in this passage challenges the universality of the argument. In your own life, what are the areas where you refuse to accept a truth because it seems too universal or demanding? Where do you hide behind 'partially true' to avoid full surrender?
?
Al-Taftazani used empirical observation to prove that even the heavens are changing. Look at the most stable, 'eternal' thing you rely on—your health, a relationship, your own sense of control. Can you sincerely say you have not made an idol of it? What would it take to transfer your trust entirely to the Permanent?

The Third Argument: Body as Effect of the Free Agent
Al-Taftazani gives the third proof: the body is the effect of a free agent, hence temporal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third argument for the temporality of bodies: that every body is an effect of a free agent and therefore originated in time.

Core Idea
The body's dependence on a free agent proves its temporal origin.

Al-Taftazani now presents a third proof for the temporality of bodies, building on previous discussions. He argues that the body, like all possible things, is an effect of a free agent—God—and that any effect of a free agent must be preceded by an intention to bring it into existence, implying it was not eternal. This lesson unpacks the logical steps and clarifies the scope of the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Note the key terms like مؤثر, فاعل مختار, and إمكان.
The passage references earlier and later sections; focus on the logical flow.
2
Trace the argument
Identify the premises: the body needs an agent, that agent is a free agent, and every effect of a free agent is temporal. See how these lead to the conclusion that the body is temporal.
The argument relies on the principle that an effect of a free agent is preceded by intention.
3
Understand the key condition
Notice the final sentence: the argument is only complete for those who hold that the cause of the need for an agent is mere possibility (الإمكان). Reflect on why this condition matters.
This aligns with the Ash'ari position that possibility is sufficient to require a creator.
4
Avoid the misconception
The passage explicitly warns against applying the argument to God's eternal attributes. Understand why the attributes are not temporally originated.
The attributes are not 'effects' of God's free choice in the same way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الثالث) أن الجسم أثر الفاعل المختار ابتداء أو انتهاء، لما سيجيء من إثبات قدرة الواجب فيكون حادثاً لما مر. لاخفاء في أن الجسم ، بل كل ممكن يحتاج إلى مؤثر، ولا بد من الانتهاء (1) إلى الواجب تعالى . وسيجيء أنه فاعل الاختيار (3) ، وقد سبق أن كل ما هو أثر المختار فهو حادث مسبوق بالقصد إلى إيجاده ولا يكون ذلك إلا حال عدمه وبهذا نثبت حدوث ما سوى الصانع (3) من الجواهر والأعراض، وليشكل بصفاته القديمة. ولا يتم إلا على من يجعل سبب الاحتياج إلى المؤثر مجرد () الإمكان.
He said (the third): that the body is the effect of the free agent, whether at the beginning or at the end, because of what will come of establishing the power of the Necessary, so it is temporal, according to what has passed. There is no hiddenness in that the body, indeed every possible thing, needs an agent, and it must end at the Exalted Necessary. And it will come that He is a free agent, and it has preceded that whatever is the effect of the free agent is temporal, preceded by intention for its existence, and that does not occur except in the state of its non-existence. And by this we prove the temporality of everything other than the Maker, both substances and accidents, and let it not be objected by His eternal attributes. And it is only complete for one who makes the cause of the need for an agent mere possibility.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the third proof for the temporality of bodies. The argument is that the body is the effect of a free agent (al-faa'il al-mukhtaar) either at the beginning (of its existence) or at the end (i.e., its existence is dependent on the agent). This is based on the later demonstration that the Necessary exists and has power. Since the body is an effect of a free agent, it is temporal, because an effect of a free agent is preceded by an intention to bring it into existence, and that intention only occurs when the effect does not yet exist. Thus, all substances and accidents other than the Creator are temporally originated. The author notes that this argument should not be confused with God's eternal attributes (which are not temporal). The proof is valid only for those who hold that the cause of the need for an agent is mere possibility (imkaan).

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument also proves that God's attributes are temporal.
Correction: Al-Taftazani explicitly says 'let it not be objected by His eternal attributes'—the argument does not apply to attributes because they are not effects of free choice in the same way.

Checkpoint

B) It is an effect of the free agent and thus temporal.
C) It is a cause of itself.
D) It is a mixture of eternal and temporal.

Key Takeaway
The body's dependence on a free agent entails its temporality, but this argument does not affect the eternity of God's attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The argument excludes God's eternal attributes from temporality. Have you ever unknowingly treated some aspect of God's nature—like His mercy or power—as if it were created or limited? What would it mean for you to fully acknowledge that God's attributes are eternal and uncreated, even as everything else you rely on is temporal?
?
Al-Taftazani warns that this argument is only complete for those who hold that mere possibility is enough to require a cause. Do you live as if your own existence is contingent on God at every moment, or do you sometimes feel self-sufficient? What would it look like today to surrender the illusion of independence and lean entirely on the free Agent who brought you from non-existence?

The Fourth and Fifth Arguments and an Objection
Al-Taftazani outlines the fourth and fifth proofs and an objection on the fifth.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth and fifth arguments for the temporality of bodies and the objection to the fifth argument.

Core Idea
Specific determination of quantity and place points to a free agent.

In this passage, al-Taftazani continues the series of arguments for the temporality of bodies by presenting the fourth and fifth proofs. The fourth relies on a famous fallacy about attaining the already attained, while the fifth restates the first argument but focuses on the necessary determination of quantity and place. An important objection is raised against the fifth argument, and this lesson will walk through each part.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Start by reading the Arabic text carefully. Notice the structure: it begins with the fourth argument, then transitions to the fifth, and ends with an objection.
The passage references a previous fallacy (tahsil al-hasil) and a first argument not fully detailed here.
2
Understand the fourth argument
Identify what the fourth argument is and why it is based on a fallacy. Reflect on why the author says it does not require proving the Maker is free.
The fallacy is that the agent's effect on an existing thing is redundant (attaining the already attained).
3
Analyze the fifth argument
See how the fifth argument is identical to the first but specifies that the body always has a specific quantity or place, both temporal and effects of a free agent.
The argument relies on the equal relation of the efficient cause to all possibilities.
4
Examine the objection
Understand the objection that the specific determination could be due to material, formal, or atomic causes, not necessarily free agency.
The objection challenges the necessity of a free agent for temporal determination.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الرابع إلا أنه لا يتوقف على إثبات كون الصانع مختاراً ، لكن يبتنى على المغلطة المشهورة. وهي أن تأثير المؤثر في الشيء حال وجوده تحصيل للحاصل، وقد عرفت(ه) حلها . وأما الخامس. فهو بعينه الأول إلا أنه بين فيه عدم خلو الجسم عن الحادث، بأنه لا يخلو عن مقدار مخصوص أو حيز مخصوص، وكل منهما حادث، لكونه أثر المختار. إذ نسبة الموجب إلى جميع المقادير والأحياز على السواء. ويردّ عليه : أنه يجوز أن يكون ذلك باعتبار المادة أو الصورة أو عدد الجواهر (1) الفردة أو غير ذلك من الأسباب الخارجة .
And likewise the fourth, except that it does not depend on establishing that the Maker is free, but it is based on the famous fallacy, which is that the agent's effect on a thing while it exists is an attainment of the already attained (tahsil al-hasil), and you have known its solution. As for the fifth, it is the same as the first, except that he clarifies in it the non-emptiness of the body from the temporal: that the body is not empty of a specific quantity or a specific place, and each of these is temporal, because it is the effect of the free agent. For the relation of the cause (al-mujib) to all quantities and places is equal. And it is objected against it: that it is permissible that this is due to the matter, or the form, or the number of individual substances (jawhar fard), or other external causes.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth and fifth arguments for the temporality of bodies. The fourth argument does not require proving that the Maker is free (mukhtaar) but relies on the well-known fallacy of 'tahsil al-hasil' (attaining the already attained), which the author says has been solved. The fifth argument is essentially the same as the first (which is not detailed here), but it shows that the body is never devoid of temporal accidents: it always has a specific quantity or a specific place, and these are temporal because they are effects of the free agent. This is because the cause (al-mujib) relates equally to all quantities and places, so the specific determination requires a free choice. An objection is raised against the fifth: the specific quantity or place could be due to matter, form, the number of indivisible substances (jawhar fard), or other external causes, not necessarily free agency. The author does not provide the answer here.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The fifth argument is an entirely new proof for the temporality of bodies.
Correction: The fifth argument is actually the same as the first argument, but it focuses on the specific determination of quantity and place.

Checkpoint

A) That the body can be empty of temporal accidents.
B) That the specific quantity or place might be due to material or formal causes, not free agency.
C) That the fifth argument contradicts the first.
D) That the free agent cannot choose among equal alternatives.

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument for the temporality of bodies relies on the idea that specific determination of quantity and place requires a free agent, but it faces an objection that such determination could be due to material or formal causes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?
The fourth argument builds on a fallacy, warning against weak reasoning. Have you ever relied on a comforting but flawed argument for faith? What would it cost you to press into the harder, more rigorous proofs?

The Fourth Proof and Its Refutation: Pre-eternality as Mental Construct
The author presents the fourth proof and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the structure of the fourth proof against the pre-eternity of the body and how it is refuted by showing that pre-eternality is a mental construct, not a real attribute.

Core Idea
Pre-eternality is a mental consideration, not a real attribute, so no infinite regress arises.

This lesson presents a dialectical exchange typical of kalam: a proof that if the body were pre-eternal, its pre-eternality would lead to an infinite regress or contradiction, followed by a refutation that exposes the weakness of the argument. The key insight is that pre-eternality (القدم) is a mental concept, not an ontologically real attribute, so the regress argument collapses. Understanding this sharpens your grasp of how kalam arguments are constructed and critiqued.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Get a sense of the argumentative flow: a claim, two possibilities, a refutation.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two possibilities of the argument
The proof claims that if the body were pre-eternal, its pre-eternality would be an additional attribute. Then it presents two possibilities: either the pre-eternality is itself pre-eternal (leading to regress) or it is temporal (leading to contradiction). Find these in the text.
3
Understand the refutation
The author refutes by stating that pre-eternality is a mental consideration, so there is no real regress. He adds two more points: the pre-eternality of pre-eternality is identical to it, and a counter-argument. Check the commentary for details.
The refutation is in three parts: weakness, identity, counter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أدلة إبطال قدم الجسم قال (الرابع) لو كان الجسم قديماً فقدمه زائد على ذاته لكونه مشتركاً بينه وبين الواجب، وحينئذ إما أن يكون قدمه قديماً، فينقل الكلام إلى قدم القدم ويتسلسل، أو حادثاً فيلزم حدوث القديم بل الجسم لامتناع تحققه بدون القدم وضعفه ظاهر لأن القدم اعتبار عقلي فلا يتسلسل وأيضاً قدم القدم عينه وأيضاً معارض بأن الجسم لو كان حادثاً فحدوثه إما حادث فيتسلسل أو قديم فيكون الجسم الموصوف به أولى بالقدم.
Proofs for invalidating the pre-eternity of the body. He said (the fourth): If the body were pre-eternal, then its pre-eternality would be additional to its essence because it is shared between it and the Necessary Being. Then either its pre-eternality is pre-eternal, then the discourse would shift to the pre-eternality of pre-eternality and there would be a regress; or [its pre-eternality is] temporal, then the temporality of the pre-eternal would follow, rather the body [would be temporal] because its realization without pre-eternality is impossible. But its weakness is apparent because pre-eternality is a mental consideration, so there is no regress. Also, the pre-eternality of pre-eternality is identical to it. Also, it is countered that if the body were temporal, then its temporality is either temporal [leading to regress] or pre-eternal, then the body described by it would be more deserving of pre-eternality.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof against the pre-eternity of the body and then refutes it. The argument: If the body were pre-eternal, then its pre-eternality would be an attribute additional to its essence, shared with the Necessary Being. Then either that pre-eternality is itself pre-eternal (leading to infinite regress when questioning the pre-eternality of that pre-eternality) or it is temporally originated (making the pre-eternal body temporal, which is contradictory). The refutation: The argument is weak because pre-eternality (القدم) is a mental consideration (اعتبار عقلي), not a real attribute, so there is no infinite regress. Moreover, the pre-eternality of pre-eternality is identical to it (i.e., not an additional attribute). Additionally, the argument is countered with a parallel: if the body were temporal, the same regress would apply to its temporality, or if its temporality were pre-eternal, then the body would be more deserving of pre-eternality. This shows the weakness of the original argument.

Key Terms
القدم
الاعتبار العقلي
التسلسل
الجسم
الواجب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the argument is valid and forces the opponent into a regress, requiring a different response.
Correction: The argument is invalid because pre-eternality is a mental construct, not a real attribute; thus the regress is not ontologically problematic.

Checkpoint
How does the author refute the argument that the pre-eternality of the body leads to infinite regress?
A) By denying that the body is pre-eternal.
B) By stating that pre-eternality is a mental consideration, not a real attribute.
C) By asserting that infinite regress is impossible.
D) By providing an alternative proof for the temporality of the body.

Key Takeaway
Pre-eternality is a mental consideration, not a real attribute, so the regress argument against the body's pre-eternity fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you consider something in your life to be 'eternal' or unchangeable—like a habit, a belief, or a relationship—are you treating a mental construct as a real attribute? How might that be revealed as an illusion?
?
How often do you engage in infinite regress arguments in your own mind, worrying about the 'pre-eternity' of problems? Can you identify a worry that is actually just a mental consideration without external reality?
?
The refutation shows that pre-eternality is a mental consideration, not a real attribute. In what area of your life do you need to distinguish between a mental construct and reality, and what step will you take today to act on that distinction?

Fourth Proof Against the Eternity of the Body and Its Refutation
Sanusi presents the fourth argument against the eternity of the body and its refutation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth argument against the eternity of the body and how it is refuted using the Ash'ari concept of mental considerations.

Core Idea
Eternity is not a real attribute; it is a mental consideration, so no regress follows.

Sanusi presents a logical argument that attempts to prove the body cannot be eternal. The argument creates a dilemma about the attribute of eternity itself. However, he quickly shows the weakness of this argument by appealing to a fundamental distinction in kalam: the difference between real attributes and mental considerations. Let's examine the argument and its refutation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Sanusi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two horns of the dilemma
Find where Sanusi presents the dilemma: either the eternity of the body is eternal or originated. Trace each horn and its consequence.
The dilemma is the core of the argument.
3
Notice the refutation's key point
Sanusi states that the argument's weakness is because 'eternity is a mental consideration'. Understand why this stops the regress.
This is the central teaching of the lesson.
4
Consider the counter-argument
Sanusi also turns the argument against the opponent: if the body were originated, a similar regress would follow. Notice how this strengthens the refutation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال (الرابع) لو كان الجسم قديماً فقدمه زائد على ذاته لكونه مشتركاً بينه وبين الواجب، وحينئذ إما أن يكون قدمه قديماً، فينقل الكلام إلى قدم القدم ويتسلسل، أو حادثاً فيلزم حدوث القديم بل الجسم لامتناع تحققه بدون القدم وضعفه ظاهر لأن القدم اعتبار عقلي فلا يتسلسل وأيضاً قدم القدم عينه وأيضاً معارض بأن الجسم لو كان حادثاً فحدوثه إما حادث فيتسلسل أو قديم فيكون الجسم الموصوف به أولى بالقدم.
He said (the fourth): If the body were eternal, then its eternity (qidam) would be added to its essence, because it is shared between it and the Necessary Being. Then either its eternity would be eternal, then the discussion transfers to the eternity of eternity and there is an infinite regress, or it would be originated, then the origination of the eternal—rather the body—would follow, due to the impossibility of its realization without eternity. Its weakness is apparent because eternity is a mental consideration, so there is no regress. Also, the eternity of eternity is the same. Also, it can be countered that if the body were originated, then its origination would either be originated (leading to regress) or eternal, then the body described by it would be more deserving of eternity.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth argument against the eternity of the body and then refutes it. The argument begins by assuming the body is eternal. It then notes that the attribute 'eternity' (qidam) is an additional attribute to the body's essence, because it is shared between the body and the Necessary Being (i.e., both are described as eternal). From this, a dilemma arises: either this attribute of eternity is itself eternal or it is originated (hadith). If it is eternal, then the discussion shifts to the 'eternity of eternity,' leading to an infinite regress. If it is originated, then the eternal (the body) would be originated, because the body cannot be realized without the attribute of eternity (since the attribute is a necessary concomitant). The author then states the weakness (du'f) of this argument: because eternity is a 'mental consideration' (i'tibar 'aqli), not a real attribute, so no regress follows. Moreover, the eternity of eternity is identical to the first eternity. Additionally, the argument can be turned against the opponent: if the body were originated, its origination would either be originated (leading to regress) or eternal, in which case the body described by that eternal origination would be more worthy of being eternal itself. Thus the argument is refuted.

Key Terms
قدم
قديم
حادث
اعتبار عقلي
تسلسل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Eternity (qidam) is a real attribute added to the essence, like whiteness to a white object.
Correction: According to Ash'ari theology, eternity is a mental consideration (i'tibar 'aqli), not a real attribute, so it does not entail regress or additional ontological commitments.

Checkpoint
According to the refutation, why does the argument's weakness become apparent?
A) Because eternity is a real attribute that cannot be shared
B) Because eternity is a mental consideration, not a real attribute
C) Because the body cannot be eternal at all
D) Because the regress is infinite

Key Takeaway
The fourth argument fails because 'eternity' is a mental consideration, not a real attribute, so no infinite regress occurs.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on the fact that eternity is merely a mental consideration for anything other than Allah, does your heart truly affirm that only He is eternal? Examine any subtle inclination in your soul that attributes a share of eternity to something else—be it a desire, a fear, or a attachment. Are you willing to uproot that?
?
The refutation turns the opponent's argument against them. Think of a criticism you have of a fellow believer or a theological position. Are you ready to have that same criticism applied to yourself? How might this lesson humble you in your debates and opinions?
?
The argument here relies on precise reasoning about attributes. Consider a time you held onto a belief without examining its logical consequences. Are you willing to subject your most comfortable assumptions to the same scrutiny, even if it unsettles you?
